IN SMART SOCIETY.
We are all longing for the arrival of His Excel-
lency Lord Northcote, The need of a suitable
personage to crawl to is keenly felt in the very
nicest circles.
The engagement of Mr. Algernon Jonese-
Robinsone (“Algy”) to Miss Clara Victoria
Browne-Smythe has caused quite a flutter in
smart society. A formal announcement has been
pasted on the gate of Government House.
Following the latest smart custom, the names of
bride and groom will be united on marriage, and
cards will be issued in the title of Mr. and
Mrs. Jonese-hyphen-Browne-hyphen-Robinsone-
hyphen-Smythe. A portrait of Miss Browne-
Smythe will be found among our illustrations.
We regret to be compelled to draw attention to
the vulgarian habit of omitting the final e when
addressing persons prominent in smart Society.
The e is always added by the very nicest people.
We notice in an exchange a list of some of the
pleasant diminutives used in English smart So-
ciety. Twe-est means dearest; n.y twee, my
dear; diskee, disgusting; fittums, fits; Man-
Man, a Royal person.; deevie, delightful; expie,
expensive. We should be glad to see these words
adopted by Australian smart Society.
A correspondent complains of being annoyed,
HIS MAJESTY.
while promenading in her groundsatDarlingPoint,
by hearing in the street the vulgar noises of
tradespeople and hawkers. She suggests that a
smart suburb should be considered as a select
park, and that no person should be admitted to
A Leader of Melbourne Society.
the streets unless provided with a white ticket
issued by a committee of the nicest people. We
cordially approve the suggestion.
OUR ILLUSTRATIONS.
We have pleasure in referring to the unsur-
passed illustrations of smart Society which adorn
this number. The reproduction has been per-
mitted oniy to His Majesty (crawl). The por-
trait of Miss Browne-Smythe in the new smart
coat admirably exhibits her quite unique style of
beauty. Many good judges affirm that the lady
whose portrait we give as a type of Melbourne
beauty is undoubtedly the belle of Toorak.
The illustrations of His Excellency Lord North-
cote’s winning- personality have never before been
published. We expect that they will be framed
ENGINE
of the Train which conveyed His Excellency
Lord Northcote to “ Dispense Justice Faith-
fully” over Australia.
in the mansions of many of our best and nicest
people. These illustrations have been procured
only with very great trouble and expense, but His
Majesty (crawl) will count no trouble or expense
too great to be met in the endeavour to make this
journal trulyrepresentative of Australian smart
Society. Cmoe more we ask our patrons to
remember that His Majesty (crawl) is the only
genuine Society chronicle; to be careful when
purchasing to see that the name is blown in the
bottle, not merely printed on the capsule or label;
and, for greater precaution, to SMELL THE
CORK.

THE BED PAGE.
The annual exhibition by N.S.W. Photographic
Society, held last week at Vickery’s Chambers, was the
most important and interesting yet made. More than 400
photographs were shown, in seven or eight classes—por-
trait, landscape, genre and other, open to members or
open to the world ; and they were hung higgledy-piggledy
and catalogued indiscriminately, without reference to
classification. They were well hung, of course ; but the
Society should certainly hang its classes together, and
keep them distinct in the catalogue.
Exhibits came from France, England, America, Africa,
and Maoriland, as well as from Australia. Nothing like
the display, particularly in manipulated photographs,
has been seen in this country before. It was an admirable
lesson for students—and for professional photographers.
Photographers are now divided into two main camps—
the “ straight” and the “fake.” The “straight" man
depends upon his talent in selecting or grouping and his
skill in making a good negative; and for the rest he
prints fairly and gets the best tone he can. What you
see is essentially what the lens saw, though the com-
position may be bettered by the softening of detail, and
colour-sense may be considered and gratified.
The “fake” photographer considers the photograph
merely as so much raw material, which it is his business
to improve artistically with brush or pencil in order to
get a result more in conformity with a painter’s taste.
Re-touching, of course, is the beginning of “ fake ” pho-
tography ; and once you begin there is no reason why
you should stop. The misfortune of some of the “fakers,”
however, is to leave the limitations of the camera without
reaching the sphere of art. T' y are neither fish nor
flesh, and instead of making good photographs they
make bad pictures. Those who succeed are usually
painters, with a painter’s technical knowledge; and
they simply use the photograph as an outline to be filled
in as they would fill in a sketch.
In America, the debate of the two schools has pro-
ceeded with reference to artistic “ morals.” The
“ straight ” photographer who modifies his picture,
coaxing up a sky or throwing down a background,
complains that absolute alteration of the natural image
is not “ right.” But here, as elsewhere, final effect is
the onlj r criterion of judgment. If the end is good, then
the process is good.
The leading method of the “fake ” photographer is the
gum-bichromate process. The negative is taken in the
ordinary way. Then the printing paper is spread with a
mixture of . gum arabic, potassium bichromate, and
almost any water-colour pigment. When the coating is
dry, the paper is exposed to light through the negative.
The gum is rendered insoluble in proportion to the
amount of light which gets through the negative ; then
washing in water removes the gum according to
the degree of its solubility, and with the gum
the pigment. If the print is exposed long enough, you
get practically a straight photograph. But if you under-
expose the print, so as to get an outline, or little more,
you can set to work with a brush and alter tones, paint
out details, and even paint in details—such as clouds—
which are not shown in the negative at all.
The N.S.W. exhibition included excellent examples of
both kinds of photography—some of them world-
famous. Edward Steichen’s (Paris) Portrait of Rodm
was hung, and the hardly less celebrated Judgment of
Paris. The latter, at all events, did not rise to its price
or its reputation. Another leading exhibitor was David
Blount (England), whose picture of The Kitten gained
the Society’s gold medal. This represents a girl, stiff
and unsmiling, holding a kitten above her at arm’s
length. The composition and tone are admirable;
but the treatment is none the less false. There is no
natural joy, no vivacity, in either girl or kitten ; and, as
a wall decoration, the girl’s strained pose would strain the
nerves of anybody who should live with her. Not pose, but
repose, is the desideratum in a picture or a photograph ;
that is, it should not merely decorate a space, but should
“do you good,” tune you to life and living. The same
artist was given the medal for a landscape, and
his series of landscapes was technically excellent.
Yet J. Kauffmann, of South Australia, was doubtfully
inferior in Amidst the Pines— ox J. R. Wood, Newcastle,
in The Church and A Streak of Sunshine.
Good work, in an artistic way, was shown by A.
H. Kingsborough (S.A.); J. Temple Stephens (Mel-
bourne) ; and E. 0. Joshua (Melbourne). Very good work,
in a photographic way, was shown by more others than
there is space to name or criticise.
The N.S.W. Photographic Society and its officers are
certainly to be thanked for the intelligent pains they
have taken in arranging an exhibition of such high edu-
cational value.
A. H. Davis and E. A. Murphy are collaborat-
ing as author and illustrator in Steele Rudd’s Magazine
to be issued in Brisbane next week at sixpence.
A Melb. enthusiast sends copies of Christmas
cards, with the comment that they are “ designed by
Ida Rentoul, who is 16 years of age, and has had no art
instruction... Depend upon it, she will one day become
famous ; for you can no more hem in genius than you
can stem Niagara,” etc. The designs do not justify
the eulogium and the fuss. They consist of decorative
figures and landscapes, essentially thin and imitative, in
the familiar Christmas-book styie of fairy-tale illustra-
tors. The best that can be said about them is that
they show cleverness and promise in “an untrained
girl of sixteen.”
A first dividend of 11s. fid. per share has been
declared on behalf of sixty-four shares in The Bulletin
Story-Book, and is now payable. Contributors are re-
quested to send their present addresses to this office. The
Story-Book has not proved quite so good an investment
as the Reciter, for the reasons that it was a much more
costly book to print, and that until recently it has been
sold at a lesser price.
A.G.B.
RECEIVED
The Prime Minister and Mrs. Grantham, by
Carlton Dawe (Lond., Bell; 2s. 6 d.). Triumphant medio-
crity, better unbought.
John Maxwell’s Marriage, by Stephen Grwynn
(Lond., Macmillan ; 2s. 6cZ.). A sound and readable story,
just concluded in Macmillan’s Maga. The scene is
Donegal, in the Irish north ; the time, the last half of
the eighteenth century. “ A man’s will is the life of him,
if it was to sit down in a puddle,” flashes one of the
characters.

SULPHURIG TIG ID.
Mr. W. Folley, Alfred Woollen Mills,
Williamstown, Vic., writing to the Dr.
McLaughlin Electric Belt Co., tells how he
was scarred and burned by the use of a
Free Belt , which he had erroneously
been led to believe was as good as theirs.
He paid £\ for the Free Belt because
he had been convinced that by paying
this cash price he was obtaining a similar
appliance to Dr. McLaughlin’s Famous
rejuvenator which has gained Wordd
Wide fame as a Curative Agent. It goes
to show how misleading are advertise-
ments which seem to offer something for
nothing.
Gentlemen —In answer to your kind in-
quiries after my health, I beg to state that
I have been completely cured of the Scia-
tica that has troubled me for years. I
cannot thank you enough for your gentle-
manly treatment and fair dealing with your
patients, as I know from others that their
treatment has been the same as mine.
I may just as well let you know my ex-
perience in seeking a cure for my trouble
before I purchased one of your High Grade
Belts. I had been under doctors and re-
ceived scant relief; and also tried various
quacks, but it was all the same. I then
placed myself in the hands of a Specialist
(in Melbourne) guaranteed to cure Rheu-
matism and Sciatica, but received very
small benefit from the treatment. I then
had mv attention drawn to Electricity.
Well, 1 thought, “ Never venture, never
win,” so I PROCURED first of all a Belt
for £i and was very much disappointed
because I was informed that it was neces-
sary to take a long and exceedingly ex-
pensive course of drug treatment. I would
not do this and then I wrote for, and ob-
tained a so-called Free Belt , with a
most “ Wonderful Pedigree,” at a cost of
£\. I wore the Belt according to the in-
structions given faithfully for a period of
five weeks, and by that time I was Burnt
and Blistered so badly that I could not
wear it longer, and still no relief was
gained, although the Belt was guaranteed
to Cure Anything in Sciatica in One
Month. You bet I was very much dis-
couraged in regard to Electric Belts.
However, I was persuaded to come and
just see you, gentlemen. I then found
that I had been wearing a Torture Belt
and very Wrongly Applied, and quite
enough to give and patient a Horror of
so-called Electric Belts. After the con-
sultation I decided to place myself in your
hands. I knew my case was very diffi-
cult, as I was compelled to follow my em-
ployment, and standing at the vice was
irksome, and the complaint was a very
stubborn one. However, gentlemen, your
Belt, coupled with your advice, has made
me a grateful patient. I thank you kindly
for supplying me with a remedy which has
Completely Cured Me, and strongly recom-
mend it to all and sundry that I find are
suffering from the distressing malady
called “ Sciatica.”
I have not been wearing the Belt for the
past fortnight, but have passed it over to
my wife, who has received great benefit
for nervous troubles.
I remain, your grateful patient,
t W. FOLLEY

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN .]
Goldenhair.
At the mellow twilight bell,
At the passing of a day
That has blest the world full well
In its wanton, wanton way,
Through the shadow at my heart,
Past the young Dreams on the stair,
Lustrous from her mystic art
Cometh she. Queen Goldenhair.
Frail arms crush me, white lips press
To my mouth deliciously !
God above will surely bless
Heart’s dear heart, your love, and me.
He hath knowledge of our ways—
What is that upon the stair ?
*Tis an echo of dead days —
Passionate, my Goldenhair.
There were roses in those days
In that dim Arcadian deep,
Fairy bells with drowsy gaze,
Woke, to smile us, from their sleep.
Mating doves in rustling heights,
Nestling in a leafy lair,
Cooed our song of love delights,
Yours and mine, O Goldenhair !
Ah, the some that trust too well!
Untamed love will surely reel
Blinded to a bitter hell
Where there is no balm to heal.
Faultlessly we loved to live,
Kiss me now, my fairy fair;
Had we aught to plead, forgive—
No repentance, Goldenhair!
Underneath, above the skies,
Never love as ours could be !
Take that shadow from your eyes,
Shadow of Eternity;
And the vesture at your breast,
Let me rend it, so, apart,
That my soul may seek its nest,
Snowy nest, about thine heart.
Dreaming in communion sweet,
Leave me, heart’s heart, nevermore—
Hist! a footstep in the street
And a hand against the door ;
And a Something, sorrow-shod,
Muttering up the crazy stair ;
Higher—nigher—quick—Ah, God !
Kiss me—fly—O Goldenhair !
Q.
C. L. RIVERS.
The Sister.
We watch and adore from afar
As she passes along.
As ivell may we clutch at a star
Or the sound'of a song.
“Sister ! ” I’ve heard it at night,
In the hour when the sufferers wake
And turn their faces so white
To watch for the morning to break.
A hand, cool and firm, sn the brow,
And a soft—“Sleep, sleep for awhile.’*
There are strong men who think of her now—
Who dream of her glorious smile.
Sometimes she sings us a tune
That lifts happy tears to our eyes.
And Life seems a roadway rose-strewn,
That leads us to Paradise.
“Sister ! ” In years that have fli d
’Twas “Sweetheart,” and lips on her lips.
But they told her her sailor was dead,
And she will not look at the ships.
He came to-day from the ships—
At the door they called her by name—
And his lips were hot on her lips
Before she knew that he came.
And he laughed and took her an ay.
None dared to turn him aside
Who had fought through the clattering spray—
She went as a strong man’s bride.
We envied them from afar—
They were happy and strong.
Ah! the man from the sea stole our star ,
But we have her song.
M.L.
WDL LAWSON.
God’s Rest.
I saw God in a dream go by,
As if He trod the phantom air
Within a hushed eternity,
Dead worlds aiound Him everywhere.
No sign of life—and God asleep !
All things absorbed in Him at last;
Nothing for him to care for, keep,
Since thought for everything had passed.
A mystic vision on its way,
His image filled the airy gloom,
As if His work were o’er for aye —
Himself His own creation’s tomb !
As if, when sleep fell on Him, all
Sensation, with a tremor, ceased,
As all things felt the weird recall,
And the gigantic strain released.
The world stayed still without a breath,
Entranced within the phantom air,
Since God had done with Life and Death,
And ta’en His thought from everywhere.
This was His Sabbath. In the past
’Tis said He rested for a day ;
But this great sleep of His would last,
Since He had done with all for aye.
N.S.NV.
R. CRAWFORD.

The Miseries Caused
by a Torpid Liver.
The Only Safe Remedy Effects
a Perfect Cure.
The Case of Mrs. M. HICKEY.
(B? A SPECIAL REPORTER.)
There is much profound and important
wisdom in that old proverb where it is said
•—“ The heart knoweth its own bitterness.”
It forms part of a truth still more compre-
hensive—that every man knows his own
peculiar feelings, and difficulties, and trials
far better than he can get any of his
neighbours to perceive them, although in an
effort to convey such impressions to other
minds lately made by Mrs. Marian Hickey,
of No. 3 West-street, Paddington, the
subject dealt with was so clearly described
that no trouble should be experienced in
understanding her story.
“For several weeks,” said Mrs. Hickey,
“I had been feeling rather unwell, but
there was no particular cause for alarm
until one day I took a bad turn whilst doing
my work. Such a horrible feeling of faint-
ness came over me that I said I would see a
doctor, and so I did.”
“Did the doctor tell you what was
wrong ? ”
“ He said it was my liver and kidneys
which were causing the trouble, and that
the medicine which he prescribed was the
best thing I could have for my complaint,
but my sufferings, instead of getting less,
became fifty times worse than ever; so after
taking that medicine a while I thought I
had better hasten to another doctor and see
if he could bring me any relief. For a day
or two I was slightly easier, then the medi-
cine »;ad no effect upon me, and I drifted
to the lowest depths of wretchedness.
F.ven then I did not lose heart altogether,
for I consulted another physician, and then
another, and so on till I could see it was no
use going to any more. My courage failed
me after all those bitter experiences, and I
thought my only chance would be simply a
matter of luck. Il I could but get a good
physic amongst all those that were
advertised I might yet be relieved, I
thought; but after trying almost everything
procurable I was in a hopeless predica-
ment.” 0
“Are you sure you did not receive some
benefit from all those medicines ? ”
“None whatever. They made me worse,
for I became as helpless as a child. Yes,
twelve years ago I lost the use of my limbs.
Why, I could not put my hands to t»y head
to do m3' hair, and for five months I could
not walk except by the aid of sticks and
with some one to help mo along. Oh ! it
was terrible ; and to think that I was not
going to see my children grow up was a
source of constant anguish. I had given up
all hope of recovering, and felt as sure as I am
that I am sitting here that I would never
get better, and if you had seen me you
would have thought the same. You never
saw such a sigh', as I was—not a morsel of
flesh on my bones (I was as thin as a wafer),
and my cheeks quite sunken. With my
darkened complexion as well, I am sure I
looked the picture of death. I was awfully
nervous, too, for I used to jump up in the
middle of the night and fancy that some-
body was killing me, and if I had been able
to walk I would not have crossed the street,
a 9 if anybody came near me my limbs shook
like the bl anches of a tree. There was no
peace in life for me, and I was so melancholy
with the thought that I was dying that I
resigned myself to my fate. Heavy pains
in the chest gave me such a horrible choking
sensation that I was afraid to lie down, and
I had to be propped up in my chair because
of the excruciating pains in my side and
back, .deiween my shoulders the pain was
so severe that I used to have a lotion rubbed
in ; but that did not ease them, and the
piercing pains at the top and back of my
head nearly drove me frantic. My eyes felt
•s if they were not properly opened, the
sight also being very bad, and if I washed
my mouth out a dozen times a day, it made
no difference to the bitter taste that was
there. I had no appetite for food, and
what little I was obliged to take gave me
violent pains in the stomach, which seemed
to be tied in a knot. When those terrible
cramps came on I used to think, ‘ Oh, dear, I
am going to expire! ’ but even death would not
come to my relief. Soon afterwards, how-
ever, the food came up again, and it was
that constant vomiting which made me as
weak as a kitten. Giddiness and palpita-
tion came with the least movement, ahd I
felt so feeble that I knew the last *iregs of
life would soon be exhausted.”
“ Unless you received relief you mean ? ”
“I did not look for help, because I
thought I was past all worldly aid ; but, to
please a friend who came to see me, I
consented to try a bottle of Clements Tonic.
When that was emptied she insisted on me
taking more, and in a fortnight I really felt
the better for it. Something seemed to
whisper that I should persevere v ith
Clements Tonic, and, helpless and crippled
as I was, a new hope sprang up, becau e
Clements Tonic was stopping the fits of
vomiting, and I was sleeping well already.
Restlessness at nights had been one of my
greatest trials, but under Clements Tonic
I could soon sleep peacefully, without
dreams occurring to upset me. Then the
dreadful chest and stomach pains started to
decrease, and my headaches were less
frequent. For a time I could hardly realise
that I was being rescued, but when a g. od
appetite appeared and my food stayed down
regularly, 1 almost jumped with joy at the
knowledge that I was saved. For twelve
months I had suffered unspeakable torture
and misery, and those who had seen me
tottering on the brink of the grave were
amazed at the wonderful power of Clements
Tonic, wfiich cleared away the pains in my
side and shoulders, and drove the aching
sensations from my limbs, the use of which
I acquired by degrees. To be able to mix
with the outside world after despairing of
ever seeing it again is what I have to thank
Clements Tonic for, and I am sure I would
have been dead long ago only for that medi-
cine. The strengthening effects of Clements
Tonic were shown by the way I could go
about my work, as in a few weeks I was as
brisk and active as ever, and witli as much
happiness in life as I enjoyed before being
afflicted with that terrible sense of nervous
depression which nothing but Clements
Tonic could eradicate. The symptoms ot
kidney disease were banished simultaneously
with my liver disorders, and they were all
removed so effectively that for nearly eleven
years I have been blessed with the very best
of health. That is why I advise all my
friends to take Clements Tonic when they
are ill, and nothing pleases me more than
this interview, every word of which you
can publish in any form you like.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, Marias Hickey, of 3 West-strset, Paddington,
in the State of New South Wales, Commonwealth of
Australia, do solemnly and sincerely declare that I
have carefully read the annexed document, consist-
ing of five folios, and consecutively numbered from
one to five, and that it contains and is a true and
faithful account of my illness and cure by Clements
Tonic; and also contains my full permission to pub-
lish in any way my statements which I giva
voluntarily, without receiving any payment; and
I make this solemn declaration conscientiously be-
lieving the same to be true, and by virtue of the
provisions of the Oaths Aot, 1900.
Am LIU4
Subscribed and declared at Paddington, this
twenty-fourth day of April, one thousand nine hun-
dred and two, before me,
OH AS. CAMPBELL, J.P.

THE GREAT FREETRADE LIE.
This is the Australian Ministry, as depicted in the importers’ papers, receiving a shipwrecked black crew that attempted to swim ashore. No black
crew has attempted to swim ashore, but the importers’ papers aren’t troubled by small things like that.

THURSDAY, DECEMBER 17, 1903.
Something’ in the Nature of a Farewell.
Sometime during the coming year, now close at
hand, there will be a State Election in New South
A Vales, and on present appearances it is likely to
b 3 the first one for over a dozen years when The
Bulletin’s vote will not go with the State
Labor party. This paper is as cordial as ever in
its support of the Commonwealth Labor party—
per'.iaps rather more cordial than ever, for that
party has, so far, put up a record that is even
better than was expected. But the State Labor
party has so degenerated that, unless it makes a
wholly new start, it is time for good Democrats to
get away from it. It has reached that point
where it is a misfortune that it should be nomi-
nally the standard-bearer of Progress, for nowa-
days it takes the standard down so many strange
bye-ways and into so much bad company that it is
getting soiled, and decent citizens grow ashamed
to rally round it. Unless there is a very great
political cleaning-up on the part of the N.S. W.
Labor party it will soon be time for the standard
to pass into new hands, for the reason that the
present bearer has been drinking— not literally,
but in a political sense.
» * * *
The.iirst item in every democratic programme
should be the abolition of borrowing, the estab-
lishment of a systematic movement towards the
repayment of the public debt, the getting of our
country out of pawn. That comes before the Eight
Hours Movement, Woman Suffrage, Coal Mines
Regulation, and almost everything else. The
borrowing disease is the centre of so many evils
that it is difficult to enumerate them all, but a
few of them are these :
(1) Borrowing, though it makes tempoiary bursts of
artificial prosperity, is ihe chief cause of permanent
want of employment. The money we borrow conies
here in the shape of goods, and therefore the
people who should be employed making these
goo,ls are out of work. New South .Wales, in 1902,
exported goods to the value of nearly £26,000,000,
of which about £6,000,000 was required to pay interest on
public and private foreign debts, leaving £20,000,000 to
pay for imports. It imported to the value of nearly
£24,000,000. If it hadn’t borrowed another £4,000,000
abroad it couldn't have imported to the value of more
than £20,000,000, and there would have been £4,000,000
worth more work for our own people.
(2) Because of the want of employment caused by
borrowing, this country is now almost the most pauperised
white man's country on earth. The pauperism doesn’t
appear openly, but the constant struggle to “absorb
labor” by building, out of loans, railways, roads, bridges
a id all manner of works far ahead of the reasonable
requirements of the population is pauperism.
(:j) II cause of borrowing, which furnishes an easy way
of making ends meet on paper, Australian finance has
become a disgrace to any white man’s country. The
'^ii1 ea khat in any system of honest finance ends
S "°j j . ma de to nieet lias been lost—smothered by the
sanddrift of loans and 1.0. - ’s.
(4) The borrowing disease has put the whole scheme of
material progress on an unsound basis. It has come to
»e a recognised article of political faith that all public
works must lie built out of loans ; that our whole
progress depends on loans; that when Cohen won’t
tend, public works—even the most necessary public
works must stop till he will lend ; that if he perman-
ently stops lending, all public works, however necessarv,
must cease permanently. This isn’t a healthy or manly
state of mind for any country to get into, and it is better
to get out of it novv than wait till Che debt is much larger,
and the state of mind more incurable.
(5) The borrowing disease is already making the demo-
cratic programme totter on its basis. While the disease
lasts we must have new loans, and we must get the old
* a U 3 • ( on any terms Coiien chooses to grant).
And if Coiien says that he won’t renew the loans of a
country run on “socialistic lines” (and he is already
beginning to drop hints like that), then we have either
to go bankrupt or go back to Toryism. Probably when
the pinch comes there will be a majority for
going back to Toryism The spectacle of a demo-
cralic policy depending wholly for its existence on
the willingness of blatant and aggressive Tories to lend
the money, and existing only as long as they will lend
the money, and being wholly at their mercy, is one of
the saddest jokes on earth.
*** ■ »
No Democratic party which doesn’t make the
abolition of borrowing the first plank of its plat-
form has any permanent standing. It exists by
the grace of -Cohen. And the N.S.W, Labor
party is in a peculiarly bad position. It has
held the balance of power for many . years.
Every borrowing Government that N.S.W.
has had since 1891 has been able to borrow,
and squander, and pile up deficits, entirely,
or almost entirely, by the aid of the Labor
vote. The Labor party is the only one which
can be. held responsible for the loan frenzies
and deficits of both parties. And it should have
been the best party in this respect instead of
being as bad as the worst. When a Carruthers
party borrows and booms and bursts it is only
dragging the Carrutiiers policy in the mud, and
this paper has never noticed anything about the
Carkuthers policy to show that the mud isn’t
the best place for it. When the See Ministry
goes on a loan drunk it only drops its own poli-
tical principles in the gutter, and its political
principles are so indefinite and useless that the
gutter is as good a spot to leave them as any
other. But when the Labor party, which has (or
once had) a good set of principles, though it has
lost sight of the best principle of all, lets itself
get into the financial gutter with Carruthers
and into the mud with See it is time for Demo-
crats to object. If Toryism gets a set-back
through being associated with public bankruptcy,
that is nothing to grieve over, but for Democratic
principles to be brought-into disrepute in the same
fashion is something to grieve over. It isn’t good
enough to be simply no worse than See and just
about as bad as Joey Carruthers.
* * * *
The democratic programme necessarily increases
the cost of government—and it is well worth the
increased cost. To take only one example, old
age pensions mean money—a great deal of money.
And no part} 7 has a right to advocate increased
expenditure, even in a good cause, without also
looking after the means of meeting that expendi-
ture it has no right to bring a good cause into
discredit. As matters stand, old age pensions are
associated in the public mind with huge deficits
and enormous loans, and they are being brought
into ill odor and disrepute. The fact that
there were huge loans and deficits before pensions
existed doesn’t help to make these democratic
principles smell any better in the nose of a short-
memoried public. If the present drift lasts for a
few years more, and the great stoppage of credit
comes when N.S.W. owes £100,000,000 and its
land revenue has largely run out, there will be a
period of ghastly poverty before the State adjusts
itself to the new conditions. The stoppage will
mean less imports, more local production, and
more work, but until the new work is started
there will be a woefully bad time. It will be
just about then that somebody will point out that
the abolition of old age pensions and many other
things will help, materially to make ends meet,
and the public will then be cheerfully convinced
that it was because of these things that ends
didn’t meet before, and that they caused all
the trouble and disaster. Big loans and big
deficits are preparing the way for a crisis. The
Tory party can best meet a crisis; it has resources
in the way of callous economy and plain robbery
that a Democratic party doesn’t possess. That
will be its opportunity, and it will have the great
lie and howl of an unscrupulous daily press to
back it up ; and a scared people will turn on the
Labor party and rend it. Already the Sydney
daily press is preparing the way.
* * • *
The N.S.W. Labor party is a smal. one, and
easily rent when once the financial crisis brings
the other political elements together. It has had
the extraordinary luck that it has been in a
position to make four Ministries keep honest,
and it hasn’t attempted to make one Ministry
keep honest. It had the chance to associate the
Democratic policy with honesty, solvency, and
financial independence, and it dropped the chance.
Nowadays it seems to have a vague idea that its
legislative work is done, and that it has merely to
sit down in a complete and finished world, and
keep its eye, as a matter of administration, on
Bill’s dirt-money, and inquire into the case of a
dismissed messenger, and see that the workmen
get so many shillings a day on the useless job
that is paid for out of loans, and to apologise for
the Owe’See Government when it gets into
trouble regarding the Citizens’ Life Assurance Co.
It has no financial policy, and unless it gets one—
and a good one—its usefulness is about ended.
* * » •
There is no reason, save incapacity and coward-
ice, why N.S.W. should not cease borrowing, and
provide .£2,000,000 a year for public works out
of revenue, and also set aside £250,000 a year in
reduction of its old debts. The State Government,
according to the latest returns just issued, has
now about £2,200,000 more revenue available than
it had three years ago; that is to say, the State
revenue has increased by about £1,200,000, and
fully £1,000,000 of the old State expenditure has
been assumed by the Australian Government.
Even after allowing for old age pensions the State
has £1,700,000 more to spend than it had three
years ago. It should be possible, by hard economy
and the abolition of useless billets and political
frills, to get back, except in the matter of old age
pensions, to somewhere near the expenditure of
the year 1900. Then, by imposing graduated land-
tax, by increased income-tax, absentee-tax, extra
railway rates, and by ceasing to send to the Austra-
lian Parliament men who are pledged to sacrifice
revenue for the benefit of the importer, it should
be possible to find, out of revenue, £2,000,000 a
year for public works and £250,000 a year
towards a sinking: fund. If any party will
arise which will -make this policy—the policy
of paying o‘f our debt and making ends meet,
not by selling our lands or our railways,
or by sheer grit—the first plank of its plat-
form, that party will have all the support The
Bulletin can give it. Even if that one item is
the only progressive item in its programme, IHE
Bulletin will regard it, by reason of that item,
as more democratic than the N.S.W. State Labor
party is now. If the N.S.W. State Labor patty
will become the Anti-Borrowing party it will be
so much the better, but if it won’t it is
time for all real Democrats to disown it,
and to declare as publicly as possible that Demo-
cratic ideals are not bound up with big deficits,
big loans, Owe’Sullivanism, with John See’s life
assurance office, and with the system of having no
financial policy, and winking at a most disreput-
able system of finance in-return for the addition
of 6d. a day to Bill’s wages at Fitzroy dock. And
if there is no hope of establishing a real Anti-
Borrowing party in N.S.W. Assembly, it would
be better for the present Labor party to disappear
altogether. Then, when something very bad hap-
pened to the finances of a hideously misgoverned
State, the See and Carruthers factions could
divide the obloquy between them. The Bulletin
knows nothing sufficiently to their credit to sup-
ply 7 any reason why they shouldn’t have all the
obloquy that happens to be going.
* * * •
On the other hand, if the N.S.W. State Labor
party will become the Anti-Borrowing party—the
party of economy ; the party that insists on finding
£2,000,000 a year of public works funds, and some-
thing over towards a sinking-fund, out of revenue;
and the party which will support no Government
which doesn’t adhere to these terms—it will give
its cause the biggest lift it has ever yet received.
It will make Progress and Solvency synonymous
terms. It will deprive its enemies of their
strongest arguments. It will compel many people
to join it who, otherwise, would not be found dead
in the same paddock with a Labor member or a
Labor principle. It will secure a world-wide
advertisement, for Australia’s borrowings have
become a world-wide joke, and the first great Anti-
Borrowing party in Australia will create a surprise
that will make half the Cohens on earth faint in
their synagogue. But The Bulletin, looking at
the N.S.W. State Labor party’s recent record,
has so little hope that it bids the party a tenta-
tive farewell. If the N.S.W. State Labor party
becomes a real Democratic party the farewell can
be withdrawn, but the prospect of withdrawal
looks very poor at present. This paper only hopes
that the leader it will find itself constrained to
vote for won’t be Stinson. It would be a dreadful
thing to have to vote for Stinson.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Petriana Wreck Lie.
In the case of the Petriana wreck, about which
the Argus and Telegraph and Herald, and all
the organs of Toryism and Freetrade, raged so
bitterly during election week, these papers, and
the Tory class which they represented, reached
their top-note in the chorus of political lies. The
Petriana! an oil ship, went ashore off Point
Nepean, near Melbourne, early one morning
during a dense fog. There was no wind, and the sea
was calm—cahr er than the still, calm Irvine. The
Petriana carried a black crew which was in no
danger on board the ship—in no more danger than
the white captain. For two days attempts to tug
the vessel off the rocks were persevered with.
When it became perfectly certain that there was
no chance of getting the Petriana off, it was
determined to abandon her. The Merchant
Shipping Act provides that the agents of a ship-
wrecked crew must look after that crew and send
the men back to the country from which they were
shipped. The Immigration Restriction Act pro-
vides that no undesirable immigrant shall be
allowed.to land, or if he does so land, that the ship-
owner or his agent shall be responsible for seeing
that the said undesirableimmigrant leavestheCom-
monwealth within a reasonable time. If the un-
desirable immigrant is not thus re-shipped, the
party landing him is liable to a fine of JBIOO. The
crew of the Petriana consisted of very undesirable
immigrants. Some inquiries were set on foot by
the owner’s agents in Melbourne as to whether
they could land the niggers and take no responsi-
bility of seeing that they were re-shipped. They
were mildly informed that they could land any
number of niggers provided they undertook the
responsibilities cast upon -.them under both the
Merchant Shipping Act and the Immigration Re-
striction Act. This the agents refused to do. They
preferred to engage a tug and keep the niggers of
the Petriana crew out in the bay to prevent their
escape. The crew was kept in the bay for a day
or two, and then re-shipped to Singapore, via
Hongkong. These facts were twisted into the
biggest and grossest lies that even a Tory pro-
fessional liar has yet succeeded in manufacturing.
There were wild word-pictures of our black
brudder perishing in a howling sea because the
Commonwealth would not allow him to swim
ashore ; and the case was held to prove that if a
perishing nigger swam ashore on these coasts
Alfred Deakin would personally throw him in
again, and Philip Fysh would give him an extra
shove into deep water with a long pole, and Bill
Lyne would throw shingle at him while he
drowned. The black men were well treated, well
fed, and shipped home again. Otherwise they were
dealt with exactly according to the law which was
passed with the enthusiastic and almost unanimous
approval of the Freetrade party in the Australian
Parliament. That party took to itself great glory
for helping to pass the Act, and its attempt to
crawl into office by denouncing the Deakin
Government for doing exactly what it was
bound to do under the Act that the said Frje-
trade party helped to pass was a very abject
affair. The Petriana people, of course, joined in
the uproar. They knew, when they brought
their nigger crew to Australia, exactly what
responsibilities they took, and they weren’t asked
to do anything that wasn’t in the bond. But
when they found an election in progress, and
a party cavorting round which was prepared, for
the sake of a few misguided votes, to lie about
them as crushed and ill-used citizens, they
naturally cavorted also in hopes of getting out of
their fair legal responsibilities, and dodging their
fair and honest bill. Any commercial person
would cavort under similar conditions.
- _
The Pries of Australia’s Subjection.
Max Stelling, born in Egypt, the son of an
Egyptian mother and German father, was mixed
up in a smuggling transaction with the steward of
the German ship in which he lately reached New-
castle, and was sentenced to six months’ gaol.
Just before the term expired a customs officer
turned up, asked him to read a passage of Greek,
and as he couldn’t do it he was treated as a pro-
hibited immigrant. In other words, he is held as
a prisoner, nominally for six months, though if he
can get away earlier he will be welcome to go.
Out of this incident the abominably unpatiiotic
press of Sydney and of a good part of Australia
has made an election crv, declaring the treatment
of the man to be barb irous, and generally, for
party purposes, holding Australia up to ridicule
and contempt. Certainly the incident is ridicu-
lous, and Australia’s position almost contemptible.
It is ridiculous that Australia should be denied
the right to directly shut out this foreign criminal
and undesirable character just as Victoria shuts
outN.S.W.criminals. Even the S.M. HEBALDand
D.T. would refuse to Ijave about their premises a
man who tried to steal the door-mat or any other
loose trifle as he was coming in. It is almost con-
temptible to have to resort to a subterfuge in order
to kick the man out —to offer the man the run of
the house if he qan read something that we know
pretty well he can’t read, and then kick him out
if he can’t. Australia ought to be in a position
to eject the half-breed Egyptian, not because he
can’t read Greek, but because, in the only transac-
tion it ever had with him, he proved himself a
criminal, and in every civilised country.on earth
the prevention of the influx of foreign criminals is
regarded as a matter of absolute necessity. It
isn’t Australia’s fault that it hasn’t this right ; it
isn’t its fault that it has to tell a man that it is
refusing him admission for one thing when, as amat-
ter of fact, it is refusing him admission for another.
It is quite willing to go straight and tell the
truth ; but it is not allowed to, and it is Britain
that won’t allow it. It was Britain that insisted
upon the miserable backdoor “education test’’;
and it was, it may be noticed in passing, George
Reid and George Reid’s party—the party that
is making all the row now—that first accepted
that test, and made it law in New South Wales
long before it was law for all Australia. If
George Reid had then made a fight, the full right
to frankly bar such undesirables as Stelling
might have been conceded ; but the other satisfied
George Reid, who was then Premier—being a
backstairs and more or less crooked way of arriving
at a desired result, it probably appealed to him.
This is the utmost that Britain will concede Aus-
tralia in the matter of barring immigrants. In that
case we see one of the prices Australia has to pay
for its connection with Britain : it must dissem-
ble ; it must be a trickster; it may do the right
thing, but it must pretend to be doing something
else. Whether the world will think better of
either Australia or of Britain for pursuing this
policy of crooked cunning is another matter. But
all that is part of the price ; and it will have to
be reckoned in when Australia comes to count up
whether the pr ce isn’t too high.

The Degraded Daily Press.
The Australian daily press is fast acquiring a
villainously bad reputation, which didn’t improve
during the recent electoral campaign. If there
ever was anything of the so-called English virtue
of fair-play—supposing such a virtue, exists—in
Australia, it has wholly disappeared so far as a
large section of the press is concerned. In the
way of degraded lying, foul slanders and sordid
misrepresentations, many of the Australian dailies
are calculated to shock an aboriginal and make a
Chinaman feel that he has met with a friend and
brother. The unanimity of their lying during
the recent campaign was almost magnificent.
When a handful of wrecked Malay sailors were
taken off their ship, well cared for. and sent
back to their native land, the Tory dailies started
a theory that they were drowned on the shores of
Australia because a shockingly inhuman Federal
Government wouldn’t let them land on account of
their color; and almost every Freetrade and Tory
paper took it up, and repeated it with a dogged
persistence that carried belief, in many cases, solely
by reason . of its persistence. Though every
person of intelligence knows perfectly well that
no six hatters were ever excluded from Australia
by the Barton-Deakin Government, yet the
unamimous wail of the Tory press that that
Government should be expelled from office
for excluding six hatters, and thereby covering
Australia with disgrace, sounded so genuine that
it might have passed for the cry of a broken
heart, and the groan of a pure and disinterested
spirit suffering under tribulation. A handful of
Australian papers is fast creating the impression
that there is no truth, honesty nor decent
public spirit in this country, and that Aus-
tralia in general is as degraded as its
press. A country is judged by the papers
it keeps, even if it keeps them through
sheer apathy. And, strangely enough, the men
who supply Australia with this sort of literature
claim to be honest gentlemen, and their claim is
not generally repudiated—also, to a great extent,
through apathy. It seems to be quite com-
monly held that an individual may tell lies daily
to the public at large and yet be regarded as a
truthful person ; and that he may be, in his offi-
cial capacity, a malignant slanderer for hire,
and yet be considered an honest and reputable
citizen in between times. Some day, perhaps,
there will be a moral revival, and the man who
tells gross falsehoods to GO, 000 people for money
will rank no higher in public estimation than the
individual who lies privately to one person gratis.
He will find his level as a common perjurer, and
an individual with false evidence for sale to who-
ever will buy. Unless some such revival comes
about it seems that the present carnival of
slander, misrepresentation, untruth and press
blackguardism is likely to continue indefinitely.
There is no visible remedy except in
the arising of a public spirit which will
make clean-minded citizens refuse to associate
with the stipendiary falsifier—which will make
them hold it no excuse that he is a hack who
receives money for his slanders, or a wealthy
capitalist who pays liberally for having un-
truths invented and circulated. There is alto-
gether too much tendency to split the human
being up lengthwise into two capacities, and
assume that the business half of him may be
a sweater, an adulterator, or an inventor of
malignant calumny to order, without any disgrace
or loss attaching to the other or private half.

A Bank and Some Reflections.
Ii won’t do any harm to recall now to the
memory of a country which needs a lot of
reminding of some things, that the failure of the
Bank of New Zealand 10 years ago, and the
patching-up which has been called for several
times since—the last time only a week or so ago—
was due to O we’Sullivan-ism and littleelse. Vogel
had much the same ideas that the Ovve’See has
been afflicted with. He wanted to make a splash.
He talked unendingly of the magnificent resources
of the country—and pawned them. Between
1871 and 1892 Australasia borrowed, for
public and private purposes, £246,000,000, of
which Maoriland’s little lot was £44,000,000, most
of it taken between 1875 and 1885. No country
with a handful of people could absorb so much in
a natural way. A condition of artificial prosperity
andgaiish magnificence was produced. Instead,
however, of putting it down to the borrowings, it
was all credited to the “ magnificent resources” ;
and, when the artificial prosperity eased a little,
the only thing the Vogel party could think of
was to borrow again and “ develop ” the resources
still more. But the time arrived when the system
came to a temporary stop, just as the time
has come to the Owe’See. Instead of living


luxuriously on borrowed money, a demora-
lised country had to live almost entirely on
its own earnings, reduced now by , th .® . interes ‘ l
on the £44,000,000. The struggle to do this lasted
for a year or two, and then was given up as hope-
less The Bank of N.Z. had done business on the
boom basis; it had either to do it that way or not
do any at all. Values fell, securities had to be
taken over wholesale at an enormous sacrifice,
and the State had to find £2,500,000 somewhere
and somehow to cover the deficiency. There have
been since then 10 years of partially clean poli-
tical living and great industrial prosperity ; and
vet even now, the bank has been obliged to ask
for’ £1,500,000 from the State—£soo,ooo for
shares and £1,000,000 by way of guaranteed loan.
New South Wales has just had a loan drunk, not so
long, it is true, but even more intense while it
lasted ; and the idea prevails that just one good
season will put the State on its feet again. Yet
here is a case in which an almost national institu-
tion, the victim of just such a “drunk,” has not
been able to get really on its feet even after 10
years of steadily increasing, and at times pheno-
menal prosperity. The Owk’See’s excuse for the
drunk—the latter part of it, anyway—was that, a
bad season having come, it was necessary to borrow
in order to keep things going. Even assuming that
it was a bad season (and it has been shown that,
in other directions —mining, for example—the
season was unusually good), this means that, after
years of borrowing—after contracting a public
debt almost the greatest in the world—the State
cannot carry on through one little streak of
adversity without going to the pawnshops in a
huge, wholesale way. No more crushing indict-
ment of the foreign-capital policy could be filed
than that. N.S.W. had half-a-dozen excellent
seasons, the Broken Hill mines had given millions
of unsuspected wealth to the country, wool prices
had been better, the coal industry had flourished,
and still the State has not been able to stand a
partial failure of one single harvest. This, of
course, merely strengthens the Maoriland experi-
ence—that the borrowing disease breeds a weak-
ness which not one year, and not ten years, of
prosperity can fully repair. And Maoriland, in
the midst of the repairing process, has started
another considerable loan drunk, the results of
which will appear later.
The N.S.W. Labor Party’s Cheap Labor
Paper.
During the late electoral campaign the N.S.W.
Labor party assumed an attitude—or allowed an
attitude to be assumed in its name—which
requires a good deal of explanation. The party
is understood to have “sunk the fiscal issue,” and
however unwise and absurd that pretence may be,
the party, while the pretence lasts, should at
least endeavor to make it a plausible pretence.
Unfortunately it does nothing of the kind. The
Worker is understood to be the press organ of
Labor in N.S.W. In election matters, it
ably—very ably—supports the Labor candidate
where there is one. In those cases where
there is no Labor candidate it almost un-
disguisedly supports the Freetrade candidate if it
speaks at all, or at least it throws its angry sneer
and its half-brick of scorn at the Protectionist
candidate. When it took a hand in the Federal
election, wherever direct Labor interests were not
involved, it was for the party that shouts for the
free admission of the goods of the foreign blackleg,
the foreign cheap laborer, the foreign nigger and
the foreign Chinaman —all to down the Australian
unionist. It hardly made any secret of the fact
that its leanings were towards the crowd which now
jeers at the Aliens Restriction law—the side which
is represented by the anti-Labor Telegraph and
S.M. Herald and Argus —the side which says
that no Australian unionist workman should
get the job if some foreign blackleg
oji ’ :blackfellow will do it for a fraction
side which runs the lie about
the .' excluded six hatters who weren’t
excluded, and the fabrication about the drowned
Malay sailors of the Petriana who weren’t
drowned. This sore of thing isn’t sinking the
fiscal issue. Where no Labor candidate is directly
concerned the organ of Labor in N.S.W. almost
looks as if it were owned by Bruce Smith, and
run by Austin Zeal, and maintained in the worst
interests of Toryism.
The Shindykit* of Slowbart.
Thk ancient and fish-like Vandemonian way of
doing things is strikingly illustrated just now by
the respective positions of the Hobart Corpora-
tion, the Hobart Gas Co., and the Hobart Tram-
ways Co. The Corporation, some years ago,
made over the streets of the city to the Tramways
Co. in perpetuity. The Tramways Co._ is, to all
intents and purposes, a foreign corporation. The
Gas Co. also enjoys a close monopoly, by which it
supplies gas to the Corporation and the citizens
at an extremely high price. But the proper light
for Hobart is obviously the electric, since the coal
necessary and suitable for the making of gas has
to be brought a long distance by sea. And
further, owing to the close monopoly enjoyed by
the Gas Co. and its consistent preference for
keeping up dividends rather than reducing the
price to consumers, it does not seem reasonably
probable that gas will ever be substantially cheaper.
However, some years ago the Gas Co. obtained
electric lighting powers also, and here again suc-
ceeded in establishing a partial monopoly. But
the Corporation presently turned over in its sleep
and acquired lighting 1 powers of its own—on
which it promptly slept with renewed determina-
tion. The Gas Co. strongly discouraged the use
of the electric light, by making it as costly as
possible; and last year the Tramways Co. got
angry at the Gas Co., and succeeded in obtaining
from Parliament lighting powers along a belt ex-
tending fifty yards on each side of the tram routes.
To this concession the Gas Co. bitterly objected, and
the Corporation (temporarily awake some more)
also objected with some fluency. The City Council
still has a theoretic intention of lighting the town
some day; but nobody takes the City Council
seriously. And now the Tramways Co. applies to
Parliament to have its lighting powers materially
extended, and the Council and the Gas Co. are
once again refreshingly indignant. The plain
fact —everybody knows it—is that the City Council
will never do anything particularly sane or reason-
able till the present infamous system which puts
all civic power in the hands of propertied plural
voters is abolished. If the City Council in the
past had been sane and reasonable, neither the
gas monopoly nor the tramways monopoly would
ever have existed.

The N.S.W. Day-Labor Inquiry.
It is announced that an official inquiry into the
“day-labor system” in N.S.W. elicits that it is
unsound. As a matter of fact, there has been
little of a “system ” to be unsound or otherwise.
Day-labor employment, in any case, is a principle
rather than a system ; and the fact that it fails
under certain circumstances doesn’t condemn the
principle, though it may condemn the application
of it. No one is foolish enough to say that steam-
power is an unsound principle because Bent’s
engines, manned by blacklegs, have broken
down and landed passengers in the middle
of nowhere at the dead of night. That
circumstance, everybody knows, is merely a
reflection on Bent and his blacklegs, not on
Watt and Stephenson and steam. Theoretically,
day-labor employment is unquestionably sound.
It eliminates the contractor and his profits. It
gets rid of the incentive to “scamp” work. It
abolishes sweating. Certainly, there are times
when under it work cannot be done as cheaply ;
but that is not always a demerit. There are
other things to be considered besides cheapness ;
it is possible to get men at such low wages that
they can’t pay their rent to the Fatman, can’t buy
M ‘Millan’s softgoods, can’t pay their taxes (to
the great detriment of the revenue), and can’t
support the local grocer, butcher, tailor, boot-
maker, jeweller, draper, &c., as these estimable
persons deserve to be supported. It is possible to
get wages so low that people will give up buying
the Sydney Telegraph to save the penny, and
give up advertising in it to save the shilling, and
when it won’t be worth while for the big draper
to put in big column notices, for it is no use
appealing with drapery to a people whose wages
are so low that they can’t buy drapery. If the
blessed era of low wages arrived in real earnest
three-fourths of the shops of Sydney would have
to close, and three-fourths of its houses would be
deserted (for four families would huddle into one
house), and three of its four daily papers might as
well give up publication. Merely stating that
day-labor costs more than contract labor doesn’t
necessarily prove anything—perhaps it ought to
cost more. The only point really worth insisting on
is that works should be paid for out of revenue
instead of out of loans. So long as the works
are paid for by this generation, this
generation has a right to build them as
expensively as it pleases. This generation
pays the money, and this generation gets
the money back again in customs duties, excise
duties, income-tax, land-tax, and in the pleasure
of knowing that the worker is enabled
to buy M ‘Millan’s goods, and pay for Randal
Carey’s daily paper, and support J. T. Walker’s
life assurance institution, and be punctual with
the rent of Vickery’s cottage, and give a well-
deserved support to Tooth’s beer. Moreover, if
all current expenditure is paid out of revenue,
there is a solid and reliable and continuous check
on extravagance. When the Ministry spends the
revenue extravagantly the fact declares itself
immediately, and 1,400,000 people, with the fear
of new taxation upon them, want to know the
reason why. When it spends the loan money
extravagantly the people may notice it a few
years later—but that isn’t exactly the same
thing. It is the loan system, not the day-labor
system, which requires abolition. With the for-
mer abolished the latter can take care of itself.

M‘Millan’s Circular in Defence of
Protection.
At a country meeting where he had talked of the
glory that comes to a country through importing
all the calico it wears, William M'Millan was
asked an awkward question about the circular
issued by his firm in which it was announced that,
following upon the tariff, the firm had added to
its factories, and was now selling high-class goods
at “ below Freetrade prices.” This, according to
the D.T., was M'Mtllans reply
It is not Messrs. W. and A. M‘ Arthur that held the
Wentworth seat in the Federal Parliament. That circu-
lar was published by my firm, and I may say that I have
sacrificed my firm very much. Is the cause of Protection
so weak and puerile that those who believe in it have got
to send all over Australia a circular published by Messrs.
W. and A. M‘Arthur ?
In other words, either M’Millan issued the circu-
lar and told untruths in the circular, or he told
untruths in his political capacity when he denied
the statements contained in the circular ; or else
his partners issued the circular without M‘Mil-
lan’s cognisance, or, at any rate, without his appro-
val. In the latter case the community has to decide
whether it will believe M‘Millan’s firm when it
says prices are lower under Protection, or
M'Millan himself when he says prices are higher.
It is most likely that it was M‘Millan’s partners
who issued the circular. M‘Millan’s partners
are men who make flannel waistcoats and things
that is their sole business, and they attend to that
alone. M'Millan, on the other hand, lias been
standing with one leg in Parliament and the other
behind the counter, and naturally he hasn t been
a success in either case; he. has “sacrificed his
firm ’' and he has sacrificed his constituents. But
M‘Millan’B partners, who don’t have to spare an
eye to catch either votes or the Speaker s eye, and
who can keep both legs behind the counter—who
know, in short, the whole business of making flannel
waistcoats and things, and have and know no other
business — they give this testimony to Protection
and to the Deakin tariff to which the divided
M ‘Millan is so much opposed. And their testi-
mony is worth something; M‘Millan s, being
that of a man who has too much to do to do
any of it well, wouldn’t count for anything.
The public,*or a large section of it, has hitherto
been inclined to put a good deal of confidence in
what M‘Millan said on the ground that he was a
successful business man. The success of the firm
of W. and A. M‘Arthttr has been reflected upon
him. Now, however, the public knows that
M'Millan and the M ‘Arthur firm are separate
and distinct entities, holding quite different
views about things; wherefore M'Millan isn t
entitled to any reflected glory whatever. Yet
without that glory—well, would M‘MILLAN ever
have been heard of ?
Bait for the Red Indian.
That cheap*jack assurance concern, the 1.0. F.,
which was introduced to Australia by. a. Red
Indian doctor, and which, in the preliminary
advertisements, dwelt, not so much upon the
solvency of the society as upon the im-
posing shape of its grand panjandrum s nose,
has been oresented in a somewhat new light
during the past week or so. Hitherto it has been
considered exclusively, or almost exclusively, as a
society which has, so far, accumulated about five-
pence in the £ of the amount which it has pledged
itself to pay to its policy-holders, and as a. society
which, if it can’t otherwise meet its claims, has
the right to increase the premiums and make a call
upon its policy-holders. It has now to be con-
sidered also as a society which, not content
with pursuing cheap-jack methods in its
advertisements, has introduced them also to
Australian politics. Not able to travel upon, its
own merits —for the whole system upon which it is
based is a delusion and a snare —it apparently seeks
to hook clients by first hooking men who have more
or less prominent names to recommend them.
Few people, comparatively speaking, understand
the science of life assurance; and of that com-
parative few there are very few indeed among
those to whom the 1.0. F. chiefly appeals. A few
plausible statements, backed up by a few promi-
nent names, and the small clients are un-
fortunately just as ready to become mem-
bers of a bad society as of a sound one.
In the case of the 1.0. F. they are told
that the society has paid every claim as
it has fallen due ; and the Red Indian asks,
with fine indignation, how dare anyone traduce a
society which has done that ? There is at such
times nobody on hand to remark that the claims
have been met by taking from the funds a great
part of what ought to have been put aside to
meet claims which have not yet .matured. The
most hopeless society that ever bought a brass
plate could, with ordinary luck, last a little
while; the collapse would come when, having
exhausted its assurance fund in order to pay the
earlier claims, it would be left without accumu-
lated funds to pay the later ones. And that, is
likely to be the position of this Red Indian
society: in order to pay the widows of the
members who have already died, it has left itself
with only about sd. in the £ of the amount of
insurance held by those members whose wives
have yet to become widows. But the society
won’t necessarily snuff out. If it can’t pay,
something quite new to Australia may happen—
(l) It may increase the premiums.
(2) It may make a call ; or
(3) If the astonished policyholder rebels, and won t pay
up (or if he can’t pay up) a higher amount than he had
bargained for, the policy will be forfeited and he will get
absolutely nothing.
Nothing of this sort can happen in connection
with a policy in any established Australian
office. And no such hook was at all likely to be
taken while it was exposed. The Red Indian
society’s only hope of success was to bait it; and
for all sorts of fish all sorts of bait.' For some
there was the fascination of remoteness. It
wasn’t a local concern. It had its home away out
in Canada, and it looked attractive because it was
a long way off. For others there was the
picturesque Red Indian doctor himself, and his
majestic nose, and large head of hair,, and the
other things mentioned in the advertisements.
And for others the wily fishers baited their hook
with Peacock, and, let it be said with regret,
with George Turner. Possibly there were other
politicians ; these two for certain. Peacock was
misunderstood by the Argus to say that he was
given a paid-up policy; he says now that
he pays the premiums. Turner does the
same ; * nobody ever suspected that he didn’t.
But Peacock, on his own confession, had a know-
ledge of the methods of the society which it does
not seem that Turner possessed—no doubt they
took good care that Turner should not be
alarmed ; as bait, he was too valuable to frighten
off. With Peacock, they took greater liberties;
while the 1.0. F. was under hot discussion they
handed him £5O in notes to pass on to a new
member for his election expenses. It was not a
bribe, of course; yet what does an assurance
society hand round £5O notes, taken presumably
from its assurance funds, for? That the money
was not accepted goes for nothing ; it was offered,
and that was as much as the 1.0. F. could do.
Even the 1.0. F. can’t compel a man to take a
bribe, or £5O which isn’t a bribe; it can only
offer it. Peacock thus knew the sort of society
it was; and yet he continued to associate with
it. Possibly—not being nearly as wide-awake
a person as he has sometimes been credited with
being—he didn’t know the public harm that he
was doing ; possibly he didn't grasp the fact that
he was bait. Even now there is no sign that he
realises it. Turner, on the other hand, with a
bigger reputation to lose, promptly cut his con-
nection with the society as soon as he found what
sort of a society it was. That is one bait the less,
anyhow; and, with Turner off, the public may
have a better chance to see the hook. And, after
all, that is the most important point.

Jawbone Neild, he of the nocenc waterspout and
the wild moustache, found a new evidence, during
the recent campaign, of the horrors of Protection.
Under the Federal tariff some 200 extra hands
are employed in the brushmaking industry, and,
according to Neild, “it would pay the painters,
etc., of the Commonwealth to subscribe to close
up the factories, and subsidise the workmen to
walk about the streets, ” sending the money for
brushes to Germany. Which is logical, in a sorb
of nocent waterspout fashion. But, after all,
there are difficulties in the way. When this sort
of thing is done all round, there will be about 60,000
people walking the streets, as well as the 200
brushmakers. And the house-painters can’t subsi-
dise them all to walk about in order that cheap
things may be imported. And then there will soon
be 60,000 less houses to paint, which will be bad for
the house-painters. And when we buy from
abroad everything that the foreigner can produce
more cheaply than we do, we will have an awful
lot of extra imports to pay for, and we must
produce something with which to pay for them,
whether we can produce that something more
cheaply than other people do or not. The
question, in short, is this: If we are to buy
everything from abroad that some foreigner can
produce more cheaply than we do, we must still
pay for it. We can only pay for it, according to
the Neild doctrine, by exporting something that
we can produce more cheaply than anybody
else. And is there any such article; also
if there is, is it something that the outer world
wants in sufficiently large quantities—some-
thing apart from kangaroo-tail ? It is best
to make sure of that first. The country can’t live
by exporting crows and drought, or by admiring
Neild’s face-hair. It must produce something,
even if there is somebody somewhere who can
produce the same thing |d. cheaper. According
to the Freetrade doctrine, there is only one man
on earth who has a right to produce anything—
the cheapest man. There must be somewhere the
one cheapest man on earth.

“ C.” lodges a just complaint:—
In spite of all protestations to the contrary, there is
something decidedly wrong about an M.L.A. running a
land agency business, the essence of which is to obtain
concessions from the Lands Department, which the land-
owner cannot obtain on his own. For instance, if after
repeated applications for rent, or instalments, and
numerous threats of forfeiture, a selector’s land is at
last gazetted as forfeited, he is liable, a few days later, to
receive a circular from a metropolitan land agent drawing
attention to the Gazette notice, and offering, for a con-
sideration, to get the forfeiture cancelled. What sort of
a row would there be if an “Agent for Justice”
offered for a fee to get Judges’ decisions re-
versed by private interview? That the adminis-
tration of N.S.W.’s land law has always been
a scandal no one can deny who has had experience of it;
and the wire-pulling land-agent is only a small spot in
the rottenness of the system. The department will never
have a chance of being run decently until it is managed
by commissioners, free of all political influence. Failing
that, the job might as well be put up to auction and let
the highest bidder have a free hand. In the meantime,
will some member of the House call for a return showing
the amount owing to the Lands Department for arrears of
rent and instalments of C.P. and C.L. in each electorate ?
Mr. Affleck might inquire.

“ Chasselas ” about an urgent want:
A trip through the big Melbourne agricultural
implement manufacturing works sharply emphasises
the urgent necessity for the Commonwealth to manu-
facture its own iron. Old established firms such as
T. Robinson and Co., H. Lennon, and Nicholson and
Morrow are at present straining every nerve to fill
orders for farm machinery for home and outside demand.
One firm alone turns out 14 complete harvesters a week,
besides other implements. All these firms have a vast
amount of machinery ready packed for export to
Argentine and S. Africa. This despite the fact that
they are at the mercy of foreign competitors for their
iron and steel supplies, and that not a single firm in the
Commonwealth manufactures the malleable castings
absolutely necessary to obtain lightness combined with
strength in machinery. The Commonwealth imports
annually iron valued at £6,500,000. To mine the material
and coal necessary to produce this iron, it is computed,
would provide employment for 4500 workmen, and
many more would be required to manufacture the raw
product into rails, girders, sheets, plates, bars, rods,
galvanised iron, and a host of other things. The
£6,500,000 sent away to fatten the importer and pile up
the foreign makers’ millions would be spent amongst our
own workmen, tradesmen, and manufacturers. Land
values would be enhanced, consumption of natural pro-
ducts increased, and the tax per head of population
diminished. The Australian States have fooled away
millions on waterworks, seed wheat, free carriage of stock
on railways, &c., to help the man on the land. They
have also spent thousands in developing other mineral
resources, but the iron that has made the wealth of the
world’s leading countries lies untouched. The iron-
workers have been left out in the cold with nary a bonus,
and it is up to the Australian Parliament to give them
a turn and see if they won’t shape better in the matter
of shooing Cohen off the premises than the much-pam-
pered, much bonus-fed cockie.
• ♦
Under Federation, no State is entitled to fix
up its own customs tariff, and there is the same
reason for not giving it the right to fix up its
industrial laws. Victoria, unable to protect
itself by tariff against Tasmania, nevertheless
finds its industries exposed to the unfair
competition of the latter State, where there
isn’t any factory or wages legislation, and
where factory girls can be had for ss. a week.
Unless the same authority which has taken over
control of the Customs has control also of indus-
trial legislation, the meanest and most inhumanly
selfish State is thus given a start. And then
something else happens. In Tasmania, where
an Industrial Arbitration Bill has just been
thrown out, it was openly pleaded that the
State ought to be prepared to “take advan-
tage of its geographical situation.” “ They
have thrown open their markets to us, and
they have tied their hands,” these mean Tas-
manians say in effect; “now, if we aren’t so
foolish as to tie ours, we shall have a fine time.”
This appeal to the island’s cupidity—this holding-
out of a prospect of a good time —not only had a
great deal to do with the rejection of the Arbitra
tion Bill, but it is also being used against those
who might support such a Bill in the future ; and
thus the whole democratic cause in the island is
thrown back. But that concerns only the island.
What concerns the other part of the Common-
wealth is that, having agreed upon inter state
freetrade, it has put itself at the mercy of any one
State which chooses to be a blackleg in industrial
concerns. And, since that puts a strain on
Federalism, it ought to be ended with all possible
speed ; and it can be ended by handing over to
the same authority which controls the Customs
control also of industrial legislation.
*
Some cold dry figures that have appeared in print
about the public service vote of Victoria create a
sudden suspicion that Premier Irvine is a
monumental ass. He deprived the public servants
of their share in the ordinary franchise because
they were supposed to exercise an absurdly unfair
influence in returning State members, and he gave
them instead three Assembly members of their
own. Therefore it is dead certain that in future
they will have three members ; and now that the
number of public servants in each electorate has
been published they prove to be so much more
scattered than was popularly believed, that
it is very doubtful if they could have returned
three representatives under ordinary conditions.
In Fitzroy, for instance, there are 88 State
servants to 4946 ordinary electors; Bruns-
wick, 200 to 4866 ; Carlton, 209 to 4953;
Abbotsford, 160 to 4746, and so on. Irvine
seems to have been full of the worst inten-
tions, but whether he has succeeded in carrying
them out is very doubtful. In a House of 65, three
members who are good stayers often count for a
good deal; and on hot nights, when there is a bare
quorum, and the affluent Tory has gone away to
pub ice on his head, they often count for a great
deal.
Pluto and Demos.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Do you hear the workers toiling in the murky
shaft of Fate ?
Heavens ! hearken to the thunder of the sound
Of a million whispered curses ; a million thoughts
of hate;
A molten stream of anger—underground.
Do you see the miner sweating, reeking naked to
the loin,
Oh, you pious, Bible-thumping Man of God ?
Do you see his widow weeping by his body, Man
of Coin,
And his children starring, homeless, at your nod ?
If the game is worth the candle
’Tisn’t you who turns the handle ;
’Tisn’t you who mans the windlass—Man of God !
Oh, listen to the roaring in the stokehole and the
mine
Of a million speechless voices, all a-wail;
In the flax-swamp and the quarry, ’mid the grain
and bush and vine,
In the stockyard and the foundry and the gaol.
Do you hear the spirits stirring in the graves of
shattered hopes,
Oh, you soulless, brass-encrusted Man of Fat ?
Do you hear the clanging voices of the dead men
in the stopes,
And the whisper of the blackleg and the “rat” ?
Oh, it’s dividends and hampers,
To the music of the stampers ;
It is you Dame Fortune pampers—Man of Fat!
Do you hear the faint, far rustle in the tree-tops
on the peak
(How the murmur gathers volume as it nears !) —
Do you hear it, Man of Mammon? You’re the
cursed thing they seek ;
’Tis your victims—and the tempest is their jeers.
Oh, just hearken to the shrieking and the howling
of the blast!
Hear the trump of resurrection, Man of Cash !
For the one that once was tyrant shall be serf, in
fetters fast,
And the broken slaves of years shall ply the lash.
Oh, it’s greed and niggard wages
Make the booming gale that rages
Down the ghastly path of ages—Man of Cash !
