4
The appearance of that charming opera,
“Fra Diavolo,” at Sydney Royal, on Saturday
night, was not hailed by an enormous crush. In
fact, everyone who had money to spend on music
had apparently gone to hear the divine Ada at
the Town Hall, and a dreary waste of empty
seats cast a gloom over the gay freebooter and his
environment of rock and greenwood tree. Still,
genius will tell, and the beauty of Auber’s music
made bright spots—when the music got a show.
The company worked hard, but the thing
was clearly beyond its capabilities. Henry
Hallam was the Fra, but Henry’s once service-
able tenor is getting frayed at the edges and
shows signs of weakness up the centre, where-
fore there were regrets for what might have
been. Miss May Beatty strove with the
part of Zerlina, the pretty daughter of the host,
who is betrothed to one man and is desperately in
love with another, and though the bright little
girl wrestled as energetically as did Jacob on a
momentous occasion, she had not the same luck
as the ancient Israelite. Nothing she could do
could atone for lack of voice, although the way
she undressed and got into her nightgown, after
revealing an excellent pair of dainty pink “limbs,”
was highly approved. That episode lit up the whole
piece. It shed tranquility over the ruffled mind,
even when Lauri repeated with wearisome mono-
tony his ridiculous “You do make me so wild.”
The other bright spots included Piddock as
Brigadier Lorenzo, which he acted well and sang
better. Lempriere Pringle was associated with
Lauri as Beppo, and his Beppo is something to be
remembered, and the excellence of it gave Beppo
an importance that would have astonished Auber
could he have seen the great basso in the part.
But the brightest spot in the show arrived when
the curtain stuck, and the audience cheered wildly,
and the only Jew in the D.C. commenced to make
a book and plunged on its corning down at the i
third try, and was completely broke by it staying
up till the fifth, when he adjourned to the bar to
pay up.
» * * *
“ Fra Diavolo ” is here for a very short
stay ; in fact, it will be so nearly dead by the time
these remarks appear in print that the remarks
may as well be short, as all inscriptions and
epitaphs should be. The Musgrove season ends
on Saturday with one performance of the
“Daughter of the Regiment.” After that the
Royal will devote itself to getting ready for J.
C. Williamson’s pantomime of “Sleeping Beauty
and the Beast.”
*» * *
Miss Ada Crossley ended her journey from
Auckland on Saturday at a time when her
audience for that night’s concert was pouring into
the Town Hall. The prima donna’s constitution
must be as fine as her voice. She had been sick
all the way right to the Heads. Reaching that
psychological point under the shades of evening,
she was fed, dressed in a white and silver concert
robe, hustled on deck, where the health officer
passed her, then dropped into a special steam
launch, rushed up to the quay and straight to
the Hall, where she sang as well as ever before in
her life. “Nearer My God to Thee” (new
setting), with its unusually high vocal passages,
enabled the singer to send her devotional, organ-
like tones much nearer the great white Throne
than in preceding numbers. Name of the song
was simply a statement of fact after Ada had
sent it afloat. Her “Oh, That We Two Were
Maying,” described by an M.L. admirer as
“ better than a sermon by a great divine,”
is a standing favorite, especially with the audi-
ence that is under 30 years of age. Loving, and
the gathering of spring flowers, and the thought
of the final sleep under the churchyard pansies
are very dear to the young and strong, and the
final goal doesn’t sadden them at all—it seems so
far off. “Lost Chord” and “Better Bide a
Wee,” two more of the contralto’s songs, were
worth any money. Messrs. Peterkin, Jacobs, and
Grainger, who arrived a day before the prirna-
donna, were respectively in great voice, fiddle,
and fingers. First had annexed some of the
storm at sea which he had experienced, and let it
out in his “Hmor and Arms.” He was encored
all the time. Percy Grainger and violinist
Jacobs got a triple encore for their “Finale on
Scandinavian Airs,” and the pianist’s other num-
bers were so warmly acknowledged that he will
leave Sydney with a high opinion of its musical
taste.

Friday of this week sees the last of “ A
Country Girl” at Her Majesty’s; that night the
Rawson Exes, will look in and see the lady safely
off the premises. For part of the next week
“The Toreador ” will return, though the Spanish
gentleman will take a night off on Monday to allow
of something extra in the way of a “grand com-
plimentary benefit ” to George Lauri, who by that
time will have finished his 12th year with
J.C.W.’s co. And after all this—well, next week
will be time enough to explain what the bounteous
Miss Jeffries and the beauteous Julius Knight
will do during the holidays.
Sydney had seen “The World Against
Her ” two or three times before William Ander-
son revived it at Sydney Lyceum on Saturday
night, and had labelled it as better finished and
more coherent melodrama than most of them.
The main story, of course, was grown on the
same fruitful tree as the others. Inman, happy
when the curtain rises, is the victim of Lawrence
Dunbar and his hireling villains through about
five acts; but towards the end of the sixth a
southerly springs up, the atmosphere clears,
Inman finds that it was all a mistake,
and he is re-united to Miss Duggan,
whom he had divorced between acts 1 and
2on painfully insufficient evidence. Generally
speaking, Inman isn’t married until late in the
evening, or if he is married it is to some painted
hussy who has already a husband or two on hand ;
and the present experiment of making him
respectably married to begin with is an agreeable
change. This time he is a budding inventor; he
puts in anxious midnight hours probing into the
depths of mechanical things, and doesn’t tell his
wife, who, of course, suspects another cat. Mean-
time, Dunbar, who is Inman’s boss, tries to make
hay with Inman’s wife while hubby is out invent-
ing ; and these two things lead to all the trouble
and the six acts before-mentioned. The old hands
of the Lyceum co. play their old characters in their
usual substantial way ; and Loma Sinclair takes
a child’s part very well indeed. Miss Duggan,
Inman, Carey, and Miss Fergus, with Bailey and
Miss Leighford in much better comedy parts than
melodramas are usually provided with, earn
mention; and so does the mounting. “The
World Against Her” will run till Christmas; on
Boxing night “ The Great World of London ” will
come on.
* *
You can’t, as W. Elton used to say, tell a
sausage by its overcoat, and you can’t tell a melo-
drama by its name. You picture “The Assassin,”
for instance, going round Sydney Criterion with a
dagger always in his sleeve and a writhing corpse
in his wake; and, instead, the assassin turns out
to be the heroine, and there isn’t a streak of
crimson till you’re reaching for your hat and try-
ing to remember what time the last boat leaves.
Pretty often the piece recalls the “French Spy”
—especially in that scene wherein the Russian
Chief-of-Police holds an inquiry into the guilt of
the wronged hero, and at which the proceedings
are hung up while J. P. O’Neill and other
foreigners make nasty remarks about the
alleged Russian way of doing things. The story
i 8 about a Russian Countess who, to avoid
marrying one of those wicked princes of
whom that snowy land (of course there’s a snow
scene) seems full, marries instead a private in the
prince’s regiment, which, as might be expected,
doesn’t turn out good business for the private.
This story is supplemented by a shocking amount
of alleged “comic relief.” Almost half of the
characters have, in fact, nothing whatever to do
with the yarn, and they fill whole scenes. Even
the Amurrican young woman (rich, of course) who
is roaming around St. Petersburg looking for a
husband (about the only thing, it seems, that the
U.S. can’t produce), and who has almost as much
“ fat ” as the heroine, has only the flimsiest
connection with the story In this part Miss Irby
Marshall made her first professional appearance,
and a full house gave her a warm welcome.
Chiefly, it is a “comic relief” part, and
shows little or nothing of what the new-
comer may be worth in serious business. All
it proves is that she has a good presence,
an easy manner, and plenty of confidence.
The heavier part of the countess is capitally
taken by Miss Gresham, and Stanford (the
wicked prince) and Hesford (his father) are the
other conspicuous successes of a long cast. The
mounting is unusually effective. The piece will
probably run until Charles Holloway puts on
“Why Woman Sins,” his Christmas attraction.

Frogs’ feet, chameleons’ tongues, and
toads’ heads are nowon the bill at Sydney Tivoli;
and those light-minded people who go to see

lesson in natural science. Probably this is the

J.P. ; as a pillar of the State, he knows that the
enlightened citizen cannot live by the “splits”
alone. The innards of the frog’s foot, &c., are
exposed by means of the micro-biograph, a new
and very remarkable application of the old cine-
matograph idea to microscopic subjects. Winton,
M‘Ginty, cricket and election jokes, Ernest Fitts,
Whitburn and others are thrown in ; and the
Cirnocs are coming.

“ L. Paul ” writes :
The production of Auber’s “Fra Diavolo ” once more
in Sydney makes one wonder why the modern manager
does not revive more of that Frenchman’s work. “ Fra”
is about the only opera of Auber’s that keeps the stage,
and it is by no means his best. “ Masaniello,” the over-
ture to which is the darling of concert-room orchestras,
is most charming, and was until recently in the reper-
toire of most grand opera companies touring the English
provinces. Perhaps being a grand opera is what is the
matter with it; and the same with “Gustavus III.,”
whose plot is identical with Verdi’s “ Un Ballo in Mas-
chera.” “ Gustavus III.” figured in the London Royal
Italian repertoire in 1851, and for a time more than held
its own. The ballet music alone won it a European repu-
tation. No more charming comic opera than “ The
Grown Diamonds ” could be wished, and this work
in its Italian dress for several seasons showed
Patti as La Oaterina in one of her fine im-
personations. The “Domino Noir” and “The Am-
bassadress,” though resembling each other in style,
do not contain two melodies alike, and it is this wealth of
bright, gay melody, occasionally melting into tenderness,
which constitutes Auber’s chief charm and speciality.
Though “II Prodigo,” a lyric version of the Prodigal
Son, figured frequently in the fifties at the London
theatres, a fat-headed Lord Chamberlain in the nineties
forbade a dramatic “Joseph of Ca "
because it emanated from Australia. ftn( j
was Eugene Scribe; these two were the fh • ' ks
Sullivan of the fifties. English versions o i allt heir works
are in existence, and if it pays to revive Fra Diavolo,
why not continue to work the Auber vein r

caire ” revival with her bounteous presence, but

was finally withdrawn. last Wednesday, after

with their tongues. “The Eternal City on
Friday and Saturday brought the season at Her
Majesty’s to a close. . The advertisement bade

At the Melbourne Royal they are still
proceeding with “The Great Millionaire,” and
the demand for that important melodrama retains
much of its early vigor. Bland Holt is now left
in sole possession of public patronage, as far as
dramais concerned, thereforehisthreatened change
of programme will not occur before Xmas. More-
over, the new show is not to be what he had in view a
month ago. The joy in store is coming Along
under the title of “ A Desperate Game, and
Bland, with commendable audacity, describes it
as an Australian Anecdote. A Special Seasonable
Scenario is being prepared for the anecdote by
John Brunton, who has no false pride about him.
If Bland were to describe a six-act melodrama as
a casual remark, or a local wheeze, Brunton would
not consider his scenery insulted.

About the Slap, case : .
The case of Madame Slapoffski v. George Musgrove
even if the present decision should he upset on
appeal, is a caution to managers who accept a
“star” performer’s services without settling the terms
of payment—if there ever was, or is, or could be
another manager so unbusinesslike as Musgrove was
in this instance. Madame claimed that her salary for 21
weeks of work in “The Fortune-Teller” and “The
Serenade” should have been £59 per week, whereas
Musgrove claimed that, in offering her £25 a week
soon after the work started, he had practically
given the artist the option of continuing the job on
his terms or dropping it altogether. She did neither. She
continued to do the work whilst refusing the proffered
payment, and the relations between the parties remained
friendly. Justice Madden summed up in favor of the
employee—“ Although Mr. and Mrs. Slapoffski frequently
met Mr. Musgrove after the first week of the opera and
she had refused to accept the £25, nothing was said about
her salary. Plaintiff said that Mr. Musgrove should have
spoken about it and plaintiff was unquestionably right,
because it was for the person who accepted the
services to say what he was going to pay.” That Mus-
grove had at the outset instructed his treasurer to tender
payment of £25 a week was a fact which weighed nothing
in the Judge’s opinion. Heleftitfor the jury to say whether
Madame Slapoffski wanted too much, or her manager
offered too little, and they found that the error was £l5
on one side and £lO on the other— i.e., they gave the
plaintiff £35 a week for the 21 weeks in dispute. But
this was not the whole of the case. Defendant Musgrove
made a counter claim against Madame Slapoffski for
breach of agreement. She had thrown up her job
without giving him any sort of notice, and had
injured his business by thus putting him “ in a hole."
Justice Madden instructed the jury to dismiss the
counter claim, unless they thought that plaintiff had
actually agreed to accept £25 a week. The fact of her
having worked for 21 weeks without any promise of
receiving more than £25 a week from Musgrove did not
disentitle her to break her hazy “agreement” with
impunity—said the learned Madden. So the verdict
went against G.M. on both issues. In addition to the
money admittedly due to her, Madame Slap, got judgment
for £2lO with costs—the costs to be taxed. And when
the costs in a long-drawn-out law suit have been taxed,
how much does the winning side get out of a verdict for
£2lO ? And supposing there should be an appeal, and all
this law happens to be upset, with more costs. Costs are
a very massive animal, and give very little satisfaction
on either side.
#* * *
Madden, 0.J., Victoria’s corseted Lieut.”
Governor, and lawyers Isaacs, Duffy, and
Coldham, managed to turn the Slapoffski-
Musgrove case into a musical farce without the
orchestra. For this, Madden, C.J., as stage
manager, was primarily responsible, whioh seems
wofully inconsistent in one who, with corsets, glossy
hat and beautifully-seamed trousers, strives so
strenuously to maintain the terrible dignity of the
law. Insumming up the Judge condemned
himself out of his own mouth. He remarked,
“ The action, stripped of its extraneous charms,
which have not been few, is a simple one. Cer-
tainly it has taken a long time, but a great many
bright and clever people have set their wits
against each other, and as the jury and I have
not to pay for it, we have not much to complain
about.” This appears to be a justification tor
introducing extraneous matter into a law case, so
long as it is entertaining and the judge and jury
have not to foot the bill. If his Honor s point is
carried to its logical conclusion, it would be
justifiable to interlard any law case with chapters
from Mark Twain or Dooley, or acts from rmero,
Grundy, or Jones. Mr. Madden ought to be called
upon to pay for the time wasted on the ‘extrane-
ous charms,” as he styles them. Apart altogether
from the expenses of the parties, there is the little
matter of £3OOO a year between the taxpayers and
Madden, O.J.

William Anderson is playing a December
season, at Adelaide Royal, with ‘‘Greed of Gold
and “The Shadow of Night.” The latter play,
in recognition of the fact that night is just the
time when there are next to no shadows, intro-
duces a burning petroleum store, which casts any
number of them. Adelaide dress-circle patrons
invariably treat this sort of thing as if it didn t
exist, but the other parts of the house do it full
justice. Acting good, mounting excellent, ihe
weather-clerk got heavily on to a harmless, recent
remark of The Bulletin that the Adelaide
climate in summer is well suited for continentals,
he temporarily abolished the first of these
entertainments, and gave the second a cold, gusty
night. There are more to come, however.
TO AGENTS AND OTHERS.
THE BULLETIN XMAS EDITION was SOLD
OUT at 11 a.m. on the DAY OF PUBLICATION.
Although a heavy rush had been anticipated, the
demand exceeded all expectations. Agents who
are now complaining of being unable to obtain
increased or supplementary supplies will please
remember that they were advised by circular to
forward orders not later than 23rd November to
ensure delivery. All orders received by that date
were filled, and it is quite impossible to supply
many of those which were received afterwards.

railway receipts were £14,835 worse. The “ break-up o
the drought ” takes a long while to show itself in these
fio-ures. The Nov., 1902, figures in Queensland were
swollen by land receipts; apart from that item the
returns are only £10,117 worse, in spite of the fact that
£52 474 less was received from the Commonwealth.
Except as to the Commonwealth refund the Victorian
returns are also generally better than they were 12
months ago. .

going :
(а) New Zealand.—The Assets Board lost £7117 on
sales ; and £15,001 was written off. The bank had the use
of £2,000,000 of State funds at 4 per cent.
(б) Australasia.—£3o,ooo was written off.
(c) Q.N.—Repaid Government £3OOO, and private de-
positors £6000; written off £9660. The bank holds
£378,331 of State funds (repayable 1921 at latest) with-
out interest.
(and) Royal of Melbourne.—This and other Vic. co.’s were
this year called on to pay income tax.
(e) E.S. & A. —£1828 profit was made on repurchase of
deposits.
(/) Ballarat.—£2so written off premises.
( 9 ) City.—£lo9,ooo of reserves “ transferred to contin-
gency account.”
(h) Commercial of Aus.—Capital written down during
the year, so that a comparison of profit per cent, would
be misleading.
(k) L0nd0n.—£368,272 now written off capital.

holders’ funds (capital and reserves) the two Maoriland.
banks stand at the top of the list as they did a year ago.
The Australasia, Westralia, and Commercial of Tasmania
move up ahead of the Q.N. The Commercial of Sy r dnev,
City, National (Melb,), National (Tas.), and North Queens-
land also move up. The Australasia and Royal (Melb.)
have increased their dividends ; in no ease has a dividend
been reduced. In proportion to profits, and excluding

reserves, 58 per cent, of the profits ; then the National of
N.Z., 54 per cent. ; the E.S. and A., 51; Com. of Tas., 41;
and Adelaide, 39.

The reserves are made up as follows Reserve fund,
■ u ’ s, ' ecial reserve to cover possible loss in realising
the Planet Society’s assets, £IOOB ; reserve against pro-
perty sold on terms, £6213 ; and undivided profit, £1936.
Of the profit stated above only £BO4I is shown as such in
the profit and loss account; the balance results from sales
of property on terms. On the realisation of the 1 lanet s
assets there has apparently been a loss, the reserve held
against this having been reduced from £2054 to £IOOB.
This is shown as a writing-off of £1046. The progress
shown is still remarkable, even though some more of the
smaller reserves should have to be written down. The
Fourth Victoria, which lived through all the troubles of
the early nineties and paid its debts, is now increasing
its business pretty rapidly ; in the 12 months the deposits
have grown from £i08,544 to £145,827, and on the
other side its loans on mortgage and balances due under
contract have increased from £155,190 to £191,028.
Practically the whole of the deposits, as well as its own
funds, are locked up in mortgages, balances due, or free-
hold property ; its liquid assets, in fact, consist only of
£2331 cash, and presumably the dividend would absorb
that. This is, no doubt, the way to make profits while
things are going: well; but, in a country where they don’t
always go well, it is risky. #
Some N.S.W. court proceedings the other day
gave foreign insurance co.’s trading in Australia the
worst advt. for a year or two. An Australian co., the
North Queensland, had taken a risk on a sugar cargo in a
Dutch ship. It applied to an English co.—the oldest, or
one of the oldest, of them—for re-insurance. A cover-
note was issued by the English co., and three weeks
afterwards the vessel was reported lost. The N.Q.
claiming on the note, the English co. pleaded,
among other things, that its Sydney manager’s
power-of-attorney did not authorise him to insure
sugar; and the Australian company was non-
suited on that ground. _ So, apparently, it is
necessary before any premium is paid in to the Australian
office of any English or other foreign co. to. see the
manager’s power of attorney. Or the manager might
paste it on the shingle on which the co..hangs out its
sign. Why a respectable English co. should employ a
manager here who can’t be relied upon to see that the
business is confined within the limit of his authority is
difficult to understand.
* • • •
Some of the methods of the fivepence-in-the-£
1.0. F. alleged insurance concern have transpired in the
Victorian Assembly. Peacock, Premier when the request
was made that the affairs of the concern should be
officially inquired into, admits that he took a policy,
but asserts that he is paying for it. It is a very small
policy, and the fact that Peacock took it isn’t any guaran-
tee that he believed the concern sound. Peacock used
to be prepared to become a member of almost anything—
that was mainly how he got into Parliament and finally
into office. But even if be is paying for his policy he
doesn’t come out of the business any too well, for he
admits that he took from Dr. Montague, the Red
Indian’s agent, £SO to hand to a member for
the latter’s election expenses. The fact that the
money was refused doesn’t make things any better
for the 1.0. F.; that it was offered shows the
methods the society adopts. Also it shows that Peacock
knew what its methods were, and still stuck to it., The
wily Red Indian, it seems, made George. Turner its
solicitor very earl}’ in the proceedings ; afterwards he
took a policy and some sort of an honorary office. But
after the disclosure of the society’s methods Turner cuts
his connection with the concern—a step which The
Bulletin urged him to take long ago. Presumably
Turner didn’t know the sort of company he was in.
• * * •
N.S.W. Lands Department’s report shows that,
as the result of the reappraisements which were con-
tinued throughout last year, conditional purchases, home-
stead selections, conditional leases and settlement leases
for which the holders had agreed to pay £3,933,534 were
revalued at £2,409,521. That is to say, the State wrote
off, in respect to these comparatively few holdings,
£1,524,013. And this includes very little of that part of
the country—the far west—which has depreciated most.
At the end of 1896, N.S.W. had still £12,381,760 to collect
from its conditional purchasers. At the end of 1900, the
amount due had fallen to £10,305,902. With the ordinary
decrease and the writings-off it must be now down to
about £8,000,000. A large source of revenue bids fair to
fall through very shortly.
* • • •
How the Australian tariff is ruining Sydney :
In the July-Sept. quarter of 1901, under the George Reid
tariff, the exports of boots and shoes amounted to
£10,354 ; in the corresponding part of 1902, they had
reached £18,445 ; this year, £22,248. In two year 3 the
tariff has thus more than doubled the exports—to say
nothing of what it has done for the goods for home use.

Over £200,000 of rents and purchase instal-
ments, mostly in connection with Western (N.S.W.)
lands, is overdue 18 months and more. This amount is
independent of money advanced through the Advances
to Settlers Board.
• • • •
The Owe’See Government is finding out that
it isn’t all beer and circus to be a slum landlord. There
were 434 residences in the resumed properties that were
handed over to the Harbor Trust. Of these 106 had to
be pulled down ;95 times the bailiff has, so far, been put
in ; and even when the rent has been collected, just upon
20 per cent, of it has gone in “maintenance and repairs.”
* * • •
South Australia, whose Parliament, the other
day, cut down the land-tax on the plea that the money
wasn’t wanted, is now sending its Treasurer out with a
small-tooth rake and authority to offer 4 per cent, for the
loan of a few pounds for a year or two.

The proposed Federal bonus on cotton recalls
the fact that some Cairns (Q.) growers are reported to be
making good progress in the propagation of a perennial
plant, said to possess the longest and strongest cotton
fibre in the world. On this subject Mr. David Thomatis
(an ex-Victorian), now at Caravonica plantation, writes :
“ The Italian spinners offered me BJd per lb. at Genoa for
6000 bolls, but I have not had time to grow this quantity.
They will give me even 9d. per lb. when I have the stuff
for sale. From Germany, France and Hungary come
demands for Caravonica cotton. Cotton culture of this
variety will pay handsomely when grown by' white
families. It will be the largest industry of tropical
Australia and New Guinea, and could be made to supply
England. There are millions of acres in Northern
Queensland, the Northern Territory and New Quine*
admirably suited for the industry.”
• * « *
Re that “quaint person named
Craig.” Wonderful, isn't it, that for over 50 years, or
since 1852, when Dr. Lang wrote “Freedom and
Independence for the Golden Lands of Australia ”
in which he showed that cotton could be grown along
the coast for 300 miles, at prices, according to quality,
ranging from 6d. to 2s. 6d. per lb.—no, private enter-
prise has tackled growing it, except, perhaps, the fitful
effort made in Queensland when the Government gave a
bonus. Cotton was successfully grown about Ipswich, but
when the bonus was knocked off the tired farmer knocked
off also. And if cotton could not seduce private enter-
prise at from 6d. to 2s. 6d. per lb. with plenty of
land, and labor at £2O per annum as quoted by Dr.
Lang, what chance would there be now with cotton at a
few pence per lb., and when even coolie or Kanaka labor
would cost nearly as much as white labor did in ’52, and
with land dearer and taxation heavier ? The cry is all
bunkum.
* ■' * * *
“Ake” about a M.L. bank: Seddon has been
requested to set up a Royal Commission to inquire into
the liquidation of the Colonial Bank. Some inquiry is
certainly needed. The bank went into liquidation in
1895. The liquidators received £132,000 cash from the
Bank of New Zealand ; then the bank’s other accounts
were put into three sections, set down as capable of
realising fully £270,000, £65,000, and £40,000 respectively’,
making a total stated to be available for disbursement of
£507,000. A div. of 10s. per share accounted for £103,000,
and a later div. of Is. per share for • £IO,OOO.
This was in 1895. The liquidation continued until
matters were handed over to the official assignee
in 1901, in pursuance of a special amendment of the
Companies Aot, 1882, introduced by the Seddon Govern-
ment. All that time it was unknown how the liquida-
tion was being conducted. Now the shareholders find
that there is nothing left to disburse, and they want to
know what became of the £400,000 that was said to.be in
the liquidator’s hands in 1895. The Petitions Committee,
to which they applied, recommends a Royal Commission.
But M.L. Royal Commissions are generally the grazing
ground of political hangers-on; and, if this alleged
£400,000 is to be traced, Seddon must appoint a Commis-
sion whose members are both impartial and disinterested.
This is all the more necessary as one of Seddon’s col-
leagues was a debtor of the bank. Just at present, it
looks as though Seddon will talk at large, and the Royal
Commission will look wise and scratch its empty head,
and pocket its fees, and that there the matter will end.

• * • •
Block 14, which, in proportion to its profits,
has spent more in plant than any other of the principal
Broken Hill co’s, will be the first to use a chemical pro-
cess for turning out a zinc product. The Central (Sul-
phide Corporation) has for some time been making a zinc
product, but it is using a magnetic separator. The Aus-
tralian Metal Cos., which works on purchased ore, mainly
from Block 10, also uses magnets. But the worst of all
these processes is that the product is worth little to Aus-
tralia ; it is nearly all shipped for final treatment in
Europe, and it is in this final treatment that most of the
money is spent, in either coal, coke, or labor. The Sul-
phide Corporation has a smelting plant at Cockle Creek,
Newcastle, for reducing the product (really a concentrate)
to metal; but, in the absence of a bonus, the industry
has never been able to get fairly on its feet, and practi-
cally the whole plant is now idle.
* * • *
In the tussle over the zinc patents the B.H.
Prop, has scored another point, the Vic. Patents Com-
missioner having decided that the co. has not infringed
Potter’s patent and is entitled to one of its own. Potter’s
process requires the application of heat after the pulped
ore has been immersed in the acid-water, while the B.H.
Prop.’s doesn’t, and the Commissioner appears to have
been much impressed with that difference.
• « • •
The Carmichael-Bradford desulphurising pro-
cess, about which nothing has been heard for a while, is
stirring again at Broken Hill. The B.H. Prop, directors
watched tests at the Port Pirie works, made partly with
the object of ascertaining the price at which sulphuric
acid can be produced ; for they are interested in sulphuric
acid in connection with zinc extraction. It is said that
the stuff can be made for 15s. per ton ; if the figure is
correct it is far below anything previously done anywhere
else. Cheap sulphuric acid will go a long way towards
solving the zinc-extraction difficulty ; Prof. Schnabel,
who was imported from Germany 10 or 12 years ago to
make experiments with sulphides, was only barred from
recommending a sulphuric acid process by the price of
the acid. The B.H. Prop, will put up at once an experi-
mental acid-saving plant at the sintering works, a few
miles outside Broken Hill.

Broken Hill Block 10 (a column in whose report,
relating to lead discovery, was misread in 8., Dec. 3)
saved last half-year 38'63 per cent, of the silver in the
ore, and 64 - 87 per cent, of the lead ; the zinc was, of
course, turned to practically no account —the vast bulk
of it was simply put into the dumps. Taking the lead at
£ll 55., silver at 2s. 2d., and zinc at £2l, this was the
result:—

Of £378,770, the gross value of the metals in the ore, only
£89,562 was in the concentrates ; the other £289,208 is
waiting for the metallurgists to get out. After 10 years
of continuous work in concentrating these ores, "they
haven’t got very far.
• * * •
Broken Hill, after its famine, has a flood.
Everything there that will hold water is full, or there-
abouts. The Water Co.’s reservoir never had so much
in it before; another few inches and it will overflow.
Behind the weir is a sheet of water two miles long, and
in places a mile wide. But most of it will evaporate ;
the call on it will be small for many months, and 1904
will be one of the leanest years the Water Cos. has had

» • * *
From Anakie, the Central Queensland gem-
field, 199 miles from Rockhampton : “ Till within the
last two years our gems only fetched corundum prices,
but we are getting a better price now. The field has a
population of about 120 miners, 20 women and 30
children. Sapphires (yellow, blue and green), zircons,
and one or two rubies have been got here and good prices
obtained for them. Mr. B. Dunstan, Acting Government
Geologist, showed us some he had cut the last time he
visited the field, one in particular being a yellow sapphire,
for which he refused £ls per carat —hot a bad price for
■corundum,’ as ‘ H.S.R.’ (B. Nov. 5) calls our gems.
Two or three diamonds have also been found. If

The dividends paid by W.A. mines during
November total £198,160, made up as follows: Golden
Horseshoe, £90,000 ; Boulder Perseverance, £87,500 ; Mt.
Morgans, £15,000 ; Princess Royal, £4OOO ; Hampton Syn-
dicate, £1300; and Lancefield, £360. This brings the
total for the eleven months to £1,838,071. For December
Great Boulder Prop, will pay £66,025; Cosmopolitan,
£20,000; and Lancefield, £360, increasing the ye?Vs total
to £1,924,056. Final dividends may be looked for from
Oroya-Brown Hill, Associated, Sons of Gwalia, Peak Hill,
and possibly Golden Link. Should these eventuate, the
grand total will be well over the two-million mark, and
may even establish a record.

A deposit of blue clay just struck at Mount
Lofty, behind Adelaide, is supposed to have a remarkable
commercial value, and local experts reckon it equal to
anything they have used when in Staffordshire. S.A.
Telegraph Department promises to take its insulators
from this source, provided they pass the electrical test;
ab present all insulators come from Germany, but the
article now locally produced is in appearance fully equal
to them.

It is recognised that much of the loss in con-
centration is due to the feeding at the same time of many
different sizes of ore particles, and while to a certain
extent the loss is obviated by the installation of hydraulic
classifiers, the constant addition of volume of water in
the various stages of classification practically always
entails a heavy loss in fine mineral carried away in tee
overflow, thus, though a more or less regularly graded
product is obtained and the percentage of saving over the
concentrators, calculated on the value of the material
fed on to them, has been increased, yet the aggregate loss
has not been diminished. The McDermott Sizer differs
from other classifiers from the fact that the separation
into various sizes is effected by means of a submerged
screen. Careful tests have proved there is an absolute
freedom of movement of the particles under water, and
that the pulp can pass in succession through a series of
screens e? «h finer than the preceding, thus permitting
effective separation into sizes most admirably adapted for
close concentration work. The Austral Otis Engineering
Company of South Melbourne are the Agents and Sole
Manufacturers of the “ McDermott Sizer.”*

British manufacturers have been beaten in an
open competition by an Australian engineering firm. We
have ascertained that the Mond Gas Cos. of Staffordshire
have accepted the tender of Mephan Ferguson, of Mel-
bourne, for the supply of about 13 miles of Ferguson’s
Patent Lockingbar Steel Pipes, from 18 inches to 36 inches
diameter. The machinery for manufacturing these pipes
has all been made at Ferguson’s General Engineering
Works, Footscray, and shipped to Tipton, in Staffordshire,
where the branch works are being erected. The same
firm have also erected engineering and pipe works at
Wanganui, New Zealand, and have contracted to supply
the Wanganui Borough Council with about 20 miles of
Spiral Riveted Steel Pipes for the town water supply.*

| lEven the Chinaman seems to have a low opinion
C;his violent fellow-countrymen as workers. This is a
Vic. mining item “The happy relations which charac-
terised the inaugural ceremony last week at the China-
man’s claim, Wooragee, took an unpleasant turn on
Friday evening. Mr. Anderson, the manager, ordered a
Chinese off the works. This led to an emeute among the
16 Chinese employed, who attacked the manager with
shovels and a crowbar. Two of the leaders attempted to
drown Mr. Anderson in a dam close by the works, and
only for the timely arrival of a number of Europeans on
the scene serious consequences might have resulted. The
police from Beechworth and Yackandandah were sum-
moried, and .this had the effect of quieting the Chinese.
Meanwhile somh of the directors arrived from Melbourne,
and an inquiry was held on Sunday, and it is expected
that for the future only Europeans will be employed,
notwithstanding that the claim belongs to a Chinese
syndicate—Shi Geen and Ah Catt and Cos., of Mel-
bourne.”

ANOTHER WEAPON.
HUSBAND : “ Oh, I can't take you away next
week; I’d lose my vote.”
WIFE : “Well if you don’t, I’ll vote Freetrade,
and you’ll lose it just the same.”
A new development of Prohibition. At
liquor-barred Ashburton (M.L.), some recent
drunks admitted that they had been taking yeast.
It appears to be a paralysing concoction, and the
yeast-fiend has to betaken home in a wheelbarrow
every time.
A man turned up in Adelaide the other
day and said he had invented a resurrection
machine. If a live corpse were buried, he said,
and happened not to be dead, the slightest motion
would set the machinery going, and bells at the
top of the grave would ring persistently, and
naturally people would know then that the
deceased wasn’t defunct, and they would come
and dig him up.

“ Marat ” : Looked up a small lane off
George-street t’other day, and saw a man posing
bareheaded, while an-
other sketched him in a
large and greasy sketch-
book. Went up to in-
vestigate, and found the
study was a defeated
candidate at a recent
election, who told me
he was being sketched
for The Bulletin. The v i
artist made an awful
botch of the sketch, but
said it didn’t matter, as it
was just an outline study
for him to work upon. The
defeated candidate asked
him to put a silk hat and
pince-nez in the finished draw-
ing, as lots of people would
recognise him. The artist
promised, and we adjourned to
the nearest hotel, the sitter
shouting. The artist told me he
never signed any Bjlletin work,
although he did thousands of sketches. Spoke
patronisingly of “Hop,” Alf. Vincent and
Lindsay. Said that we would see the drawing in
The Bulletin, which would be published the
following day. Then, after swallowing his beer,
he left post-haste for your office.
* * * *
Melbourne was startled the other Satur-
day by the appearance of a new morning news-
paper, the People’s Daily, as a thunderbolt out
of the blue, without any previous notice what-
ever. There are said to be two others coming.
The first of a few disinterested observations that
occurs to the public is the difficulty of doing with-
out cablegrams, as this new paper does. It cannot
even comment on cablegrams, while they are
fresh. The copyright prevents this. A cablegram
has about one day’s life secured to it, as property.
Newspaper proprietors sleep a little on this right,
but they don’t sleep much. When the Melbourne
papers got separate cables the Argus even stopped
the Hekald from commenting, except on its own
wires.
* * * *
Dr. Reddall, giving evidence recently in
Sydney Divorce Court, shocked his Divorce
Honor by saying that women are much more
hysterical now than 20 years ago. It was thought
some years ago that the increasing tendency to
outdoor life and athletic exercises would reduce
hysteria, and that position was strongly taken up
by medical men who championed women’s right
to belabor the earth with their athletic feet. Now
doctors begin to differ again. Doctors differ so
much that it is generally necessary to pay three
of them —two for differing, and the third for giving
a casting vote.

“ J.M‘C.” tells about his first job :
When I left school I answered an advertisement from one
G. Sorabjee who wanted a small boy with all the cardi-
nal virtues, the
disposition of a
cow, the streng th
of an ox, and
modest views on
salary. There
were three other
members of the
boy tribe on the
ma t when I
arrived one
with a big collar
and specs., an-
other with no
collar and his
dinner, and a
third with his
mother. The boy
whose mother
will come to help
him get a job is
guilty of the
refinement o f
cruelty. The
other kids reck-
on him a black-
leg, and they are
right, for the
woman will al-
ways cut the
wages to get the
place. I had a
good position on
the rails, and got
the job. Had
sometroubleper-
suading the
boy’s mother to. go away, and the youth with the
dinner wanted to fight me. Wages were 2s. 6d. a week
when biz. was good, and, when nothing was doing, a half
share of the takings. The boss was a Hindoo masseur,
and, I think, an ex-coolie, with all that creature’s
antipathy to work. He would send me over the road for
fruit and watch from an upstairs window to see I did not
help myself. I blew up his gas stove one day, but he for-
gave me this, as well as when I looked in casually while a
lady patient was unrobing. His frugality was too much
for me in the end, and I left when he paid me ljd. for 10
hours overtime. He said it was pro rata, and I said it was
swindling. He carried his Asiatic methods into wider
spheres later on, and is now using his magic powers on
solid bluestone out at Pentridge.
“J.D.”: A religious landlady, who owns
a dilapidated terrace in a Sydney suburb, makes a
point of asking ladies who wish to become
tenants to produce their marriage-lines.
Four out of her five houses are generally
to let. Agents will not take the letting
of them since a local agent got a broken
head for asking a big lady surrounded by
four youngsters to produce her certificate.
THE COACHMAN WAS A BIT DEAF.
There seems to be no end to the troubles
of that unfortunate Sydney dentist who claims
that he was forcibly married to a lady he didn’t
want at any price, after being foully battered by
her friends. He had to give up his suit for the
annulment of the marriage because he wasn’t
making enough out of the teeth business to pay
law expenses; and then, by way of rubbing it in,
he was arrested on a charge of intending to leave
the State without providing for his wife’s keep.
An interesting question for a law examination
would be: “ Enumerate all the different kinds of
trouble a man may get into with the civil law
through getting married, and supply a complete
list of the costs that may arise in consequence.
* * * *
The women students of Sydney University
held a “ fete ” one Saturday in aid of the “Bombay
Missionary Settlements.” The B.M.S. is one of
the numerous schemes of Christian Britain for
enlightening the darkness of our dark brudder in
India, and takes the form of despatching white
women from the English Universities to dwell
among the Parsee women. Since 1899 some dozen
have gone out; one has died, two have collapsed
physically and have had to be brought back again,
and there isn’t much information about the rest.
New workers are wanted, and the promoters of
the fete appeal to the Sydney University for
volunteers. Yet the women of Sydney University
should be able to find plenty of scope locally for
any missionary instincts they may have. They
can start by trying to bring Dill Macky and
Cardinal Moran, with their respective followings,
to a Christian frame of mind. When one brand
of Christian calls the other a “ dirty cur” in Par-
liament there is evidently need of local Christian
effort. Meanwhile the Parsee had better be left
alone; he has never called us anything like that.
* * * *
Herbert Flemming, who has returned to
Australia after a lapse of 13 years as manager of the
Mel. B. Spurr show, brings a large stock of infor-
niation re the Boer War in all its bearings. Flem-
ing left England for S’Africa ten days before the
war broke out, and ran his own dramatic co. at
one town or other all the time the guns were
going. In Bloemfontein he played many weeks
right off the reel—comedy, drama, farce; “Mrs.
Tanqueray ” one night, “Jones ” the next, and so
on, always under the patronage of the blessed
British military authorities. He has the measure
of Boer and Briton. Found a lot of fine, brainy
fellows among the progressive Boers, and a per-
centageof ditto among the officahs from “Home,”
leavening a host of insufferable bounders. Whereas
the average town-bred Boer is progressive, the
average town-bred lieutenant in the British army
is an arrogant Tory ass—and an unprinoipl'* J ass
at that.
“Melb.”: A lady was killed, the other
day, by falling to the pavement from an upper
window at Menzies’ Hotel. Although wealthy,
she was doing some economical laundry-work in
her bedroom. She hung the things economically
out of the window. A miserable cuff dropped to
a lower ledge, and, reaching for it, she met her
fate. Died and left all that money behind her in
the struggle to save a twopenny cuff, and the cuff
was lost after all, and there were also the burial
expenses. Such affairs are bound to occur in
couples, and this was the second one—three days
before, a woman was killed by falling from a
window at South Melbourne. It may be remem-
bered that Mrs. Vezin, the actress, known here
as Mrs. C. Young, was killed in England by fall-
ing from a window. I don’t know what she was
reaching for, but, in most cases, when a woman
falls out of a window she is struggling to get a
good view of a funeral.
Nosea are now being made of paper.
Anvbody who has had the misfortune to lose
his “boko” can have one, beautifully
enamelled, for £l, and they are said to be
very like the real thing. Also they don’t
flush through drink.
The bluest-blooded of European reigning
families—the Hapsburg to wit —seems to be doing
its best to bring monarchical principles into utter
contempt. A succession of royal scandals has
happened within the Austrian regal circle. The
latest story is of a double murder, committed by
Emperor Joseph’s grand-daughter. Getting wind
of her husband’s liaison with an actress —
which was nothing for any princess to
make a fuss about, anyhow—she killed the giddy
charmer, after she had put a fatal bullet
into the faithful flunkey who tried to prevent
her intrusion on the privacy of the lovers. The
Emperor of Austria’s descendant, as it happens,
is of all women in Europe the least entitled to
protest against conjugal infidelity. Her family is
full of it. Her father, Crown Prince Rudolph,
was officially stated to have committed suicide in
a rural castle some years ago. There was a woman
ill the case, and the details of his sudden death
were shrouded in gory mystery; And the mother
of the insanely jealous princess was a daughter of
Europe’s grand old debauchee, the King of the
Belgians, noted as the principal recipient of
Modern Babylon’s Maiden Tribute. All things
considered this recent work of matronly vengeance
is a_ Joke with a huge capital J. One
imagines the two aged grandfathers exchanging
notes of exclamation on the unreasonable vin-
dictiveness of their 20-year-old relative. Also one
supposes that pretty actresses will begin to be shy
of royal endearments, otherwise a number of
violent deaths in the theatrical profession are
certain to ensue. By murdering her husband’s
best girl with impunity Archduchess Elizabeth
has established a dangerous precedent, and the
next time a princess comes along to catch the
Guilty Pair she will not need to step across the
bleeding corpse of a flunkey. The faithful servant
will abandon sentry duty when he catches sight of
the boss’s lawful Liza.
* * » •
A phase of the struggle for a crust ! A
couple of men drew mild attention in Melbourne
streets lately by meandering around bareheaded,
dressed in khaki suits. One was as bald as a
’Pootra’s egg ; the other was luxuriantly thatched.
Latter’s back bore an inscription stating he had
been using Slubb’s Hair Exciter or some such
nostrum; his mate notified the public that he was
going to use it. Victoria has so far kept clear
of that abomination, the London sandwich-man —
the image of God degraded to the uses of a blatant
advertisement—but it is getting very near it.

Cabled that some European miners in the
Transvaal have petitioned in favor of Chinese
labor. How the petition was faked is shown by a
letter in the Johannesburg Stab from Mr. H. S.
Raitt, member of the Transvaal Legislative
Council, in which he protests
against the cowardly methods of the pro-Chinese
party. They have enrolled the mine managers, arming
them with petition forms, which they are inducing, or
rather coercing, their workmen to sign in favor of Chinese
importation. Those who know the mines know what this
means. It is a question of sign or sack. The Chinese
importation proposition has been introduced as a care-
fully matured plan, not as a conclusion to which we are
driven when all else has failed, but as a fixed determina-
tion to be forced on the country, whether it wants it or
not, whether it needs it or not. It is a determined
effort to supplant the white race as a factor to be con-
sidered in the colony.
THE COMMISSION OF THE PEACE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Recently at Goulburn the Police Magistrate, Mr.
Stephen Murphy, fined a man £2O for betting. Shortly
after a number of persons were brought before the court
tor similar offences. Things looked promising for the
revenue, but this time many Justices of the Peace
attended. The Police Magistrate made some pleasant
references to Bench-packing. The Bench had, he said,
resolved to take a lenient view, and had decided that in
the present cases a fine of 40s. should be imposed, though
he was in favor of a higher penalty : ■
Of course it’s always possible for blunders to occur ;
The wisest and the best of men are liable to err,
And then we know that gentlemen who sign themselves
“J.P.”
Are always recommended for their strict integrity.
That noble institution, the Commission of the Peace,
Is honestly respected by the public and police.
There never was a gentleman appointed, we may state,
For lending his assistance to a Party Candidate 1
Upon my word
It's most absurd
The notion ought to cease
That honorary magistrates promiscuously increase.
You must possess ability,
Position and gentility,
Or you cannot be appointed as a Justice of the Peace !
And then to think that persons of position and renown,
In Bathurst or in Goulburn or in any other town,
Would dream of going on the Bench to lend a friend a
hand
Is something too preposterous for"me to understand !
If Mr. Stephen Murphy is an officer of zeal
It seems to me on second thoughts he really ought to feel
The deepest of regret for inadvisable remarks
On gentlemanly Justices at Goulburn or at Parkes,
Or here, or there,
Or anywhere,
Because it’s all the same ;
All Justices are gentlemen of high and honored name
Who fill the Bench with dignity,
Sans favor or malignity,
Regardless of the influence of flattery or blame 1
We know, at least we ought to know, that Ministers
select
Those gentlemen for Justices who’re worthy of respect.
Of course there have been rumors (spread by evil-
minded men)
Of Justices who hardly knew the way to hold a pen.
But these are idle rumors, silly slanders, nothing more,
And st iries which we all of us must certainly deplore ;
For facts that daily come to light continually increase
Our general respect for the Commission of the Peace !
I honor them
And Mr. M.
Will quite agree with me
That none but educated men are ever made J.P.
It’s certainly untenable
To think they are amenable
To influence ; for such a thing could never, never be 1
N.S.W,
W. T. GOODGR.

One of the most pathetic sights in Melb.
is the unfinished soldiers’ memorial on St.
Kilda-road, the erection of which was started
something like 18 months ago, and has
progressed only so far that the eye of
the passing citizen is offended by an
unshapely mass of stone with scaffold-
ing all round it. Nothing has been
done to the concern for many
months past, the men engaged on
the job having apparently forgotten,'
along with the public generally,
that Australia ever sent a horde of
its wildest youth to S’Africa on a
hazardous cattle-duffing expedition.
Melbourne, by the way, has more
incomplete public works and more
half-baked monuments in it than
any other Australian city. The
central railway station, which
doesn’t happen to be in Thomas
Bent’s electorate, stands just as
that statesmanlike market gardener
left it twelve months ago, most of
the old Flinders-street station
having been pulled down, and noth-
ing but an elaborate, unfinished
subway and some unpicturesque
excavations having been contri-
buted towards the new terminus.
The Public Library extensions
have also been held up indefinitely,
and everybody is puzzled to
know when the skeleton building
is to be proceeded with. The
Fever Hospital, which only needs
a few thousands for completion,
is still the haunt of the stray cat
and the casual tarantula. Like-
wise, the present Cabinet is such
an insanitary one that it sees no
urgent need for sewering the
public buildings, and is the only
offender in Melbourne in this
respect.
* * *
Evidence given in Scotland
in a recent adulteration case
elicited that “brandy is a liquor
imported from France, and is a
mixture of grape spirit and some
spirit unknown except to pro-
ducers. If the liquor looks, smells,
and tastes like brandy, that is
enough for retail purposes. Brandy
is brandy, whether it is made from
grapes, grain, rotten figs, potatoes, or
black ants. No one can buy real brandy at 89.
a bottle.”
* * * *
“ Adelaide ” : Accept an indignant protest
against the statement (8., 22/10/3) that “ the nude
lady out in the open air is always regarded with
a certain amount of gloom in Adelaide.” If it
refers to pictures, it is to some extent true ; but
in that case why the qualification “ out in the open
air ” ? As for the nude lady in the open, why, she
is probably more numerous in (i.e., about) Ade-
laide than in any other large city since Sparta
was in its prime ! Its adjacent seashore is one
long stretch of perfect sand, including six well-
populated places, but only two enclosed baths.
The S.A. summer makes two baths a day not
only a luxury but a necessity; cheap trains run
the six or eight miles from the city, and sharks
(though always spoken of with respect) have never
been known to harm anyone on this coast. So
for two hours every morning, and more than that
every evening, there may be seen some 14 miles
of undressed humanity, of which the handsomer
sex forms more than half. Not that it bathes
actually in the altogether; in the sheltering waves
a trifle of flannelette preserves the conventions.
But, once out again on the beach, even the most
modest woman has to dry herself.

Any youth under 16 years of age who
smokes in sight of a policeman in Maoriland after
Jan. 1 will be liable to a fine of .£lO. A well-
meaning lawyer named Field has for years been
toying with this Juvenile Smoking Suppression
Bill with very little hope of success, but it passed
at last by one vote, and Field awoke one morning
to find himself remarkable, if not famous.

A P.M. was called to Ballarat Gaol to punish an
obstreperous confinee on remand, but declined,
saying that any charge laid against a nominally
tree man must be investigated in open court. As
the btate has been calling in J.’sP. in an off-hand
way to suppress unconvicted prisoners for many
years it is beginning to wonder what sort of a bill
for damages it may have to pay when the news
gets about.
NOT ALTOGETHER A FAILURE.
MASTER : “ Well, Tom, don't yon feel ashamed
at your little sister gaining her certificate,
and you not? ”
TOM : “ Please, sir, I got a certificate once.”
MASTER : “ What for?”
TOM: “For being born.”
“Periwinkle”: Daring the past week
have inquired a bit off the women around Grass-
fireville to learn how their votes might go at the
Federal scramble. Out of 22 skirties I yarned to
20 had votes and didn’t know it. The other two
had heard that they were expected to record their
votes by a chance remark made by their parson
(Methodist). Where and when they were to vote
they couldn’t tell, or who the candidates were—or
anything. They didn’t seem eager for informa-
tion, either. Most of them had, however, a
vague recollection that the local trooper had called
hurriedly one day, when the rain was falling, but
then he often calls about rabbits, grass-fires, ac-
counts not settled, and stray beasts reported
stolen —and Dad always attends to that! Strikes
me, most of the women look upon the Franchise
as another burden thrust upon their lean, bent
shoulders—at least,_ the sigh of relief the whole 22
women gave when informed that they needn’t put
on last season’s bonnet to go to the poll, unless
they cared to, led me to believe they were the
possessors of something they hadn’t asked for !
“ 0.C.C.” states a truth, which ought to
be true if it isn’t:
The G.O.C. is, as you know, an insufferable blusterer,
but the other day he was “ taken down.” Hutton had
been getting on to a prominent and ordinarily lady-like
officer. The imported General had often carbonadoed him
with great success, but this particular morning he had
missed his breakfast, or cut herself while shaving, or
something, and his temper had gone to little pieces. So,
when Hutton had dug the spurs of his eloquence deeply
into the flanks of the offence (whatever it was) that
gentle warrior stopped the stream of Huttonian exple-
tives by starting up suddenly and bringing his fist down
right on the G.O.D.’s—that is, G.O.C.’s—sacred blotting-
pad with a blow that nearly split the table and upset the
holy inkpots over the consecrated despatches. “By God,
sir!” said he, as Hutton blinked up foolishly—“by
God, sir ! There is a point beyond which even you shail
not go 1 There is a time when even discipline cannot
restrain 1 Row dare you talk to me like that? You know
you’ve no right to do it—and, by God, sir ! you shan’t l ”
Hutton, for once, hadn’t a word to say, but meekly
accepted the reproof, and even asked the officer for a
cigarette. He got it.
“ Polly,” from Hobait :
Received an insulting electioneering notice from Mr.
Post Ollice Fysh, in which he addressed me as “ an old
supporter” of his. Now, in the first place, it’s bad
enough to have had him taking all the test-paying public
billets—from State Premiership to Agent-Generalship,
and now a Federal Cabinet snap—without rubbing it in
on every possible occasion that he gets his support out
of the public’s pockets. As I take in washing, keep
boarders, and run the corner store, I manage to support
my husband and such of his male relatives as are out
of a job, which they mostly are. I can sincerely
sympathise with Mrs. Fysh, and if she were asking for my
vote, she should have it. As for Fillup’s insinuations that
I’ve been supporting him as well as my own husband they
might pass, but he calls me old. I’m old enough to be
his grand-daughter. He must be old enough to need a
caretaker, if he doesn’t know better than to try and
coax votes from the ladies by labelling them old and
printing it in bold, black letters in the most prominent
spot in his begging letter. Oh, I didn’t forget to vote.

“C.C.”: The latest scheme for raising a
drink. Yesterday a respectable-looking wreck
drifted against me on a pub. verandah and whis-
pered confidentially that he was one of the
notorious six hatters. “Gerrymandered by the
Deakin Government,” he breathed heavily in my
ear, and then he offered to give me the full
strength of the trouble if I would shout. When
informed that I had already met 35 of those six
hatters he bore away looking mad enough for the
whole consignment. If the calamitous six really
exist, George Reid missed a splendid opportunity
by not engaging them tor his electioneering tour.
Properly stage-managed, they would have drawn
crowded houses. Something in this style :
Cue: “ This traitorous Government that prates of
loyalty to the motherland, and then refuses a landing to
six of her noblest sons.”
Enter six hatters in glossy bell-toppers decorated with
a card bearing the legend—“ This style, 10s. 6d.” All
wave union jacks with one hand, and rattle their bell-
toppers on their foreheads with the other. Patriotic
song and the “Mad Hatters’ Fandango” as a finale.
But perhaps the hatters are too busy rehearsing
for the Xmas pantomimes.
* * * *
Just when there has been a revival of the
liberty-of-the-subject cry in Tas., and when the
man who won’t join a union has been praised to
the. clouds, a row has arisen over the new Health
Officer of Hobart. The appointee belongs to the
Homoeopathic Union, and won’t join the Medical
Union ; and the members of the latter, who have
been yelling that it oughtn’t to matter a straw
whether a man is a unionist or not, are raising
Cain because this man is carrying out their own
alleged principles.

“ Burglar Bill ” : This weird expectancy
that Bulletin writers mention is very noticeable
in gaol. Every prisoner is ex-
pecting to be released on peti-
tion, and if called up before the
gaol governor regarding any
small matter, fully expects to
hear of his immediate dis-
charge. Knew one who never
undressed for years, afraid
that he might be sent for
at any time and acquainted
with his good fortune.
Then he started to talk
queerly; said he had
done 15 years when he
had just finished four;
would shriek out in the
middle of the night:
“ How long, O Lord !
how long ? ” and when
he got no answer would
wake the wing repeat-
ing the question. They
took him away some-
where at last when he
had become a hopeless
lunatic; the “expec-
tancy” had destroyed his
mental balance. After
an execution —the first
for years—the sweeper
put a clean towel and a
fresh piece of soap into
the condemned cell.
When I asked him why,
he said, “ Oh, there
might be another in at
any minute.” He had
the expectancy. All the
“ screws” are expecting
stripes or the sack; they
mostly get the sack.
The governor is expect-
ing a pension after herd-
ing convicts for 40years,
and the executioner is
expecting to hang some-
body.
* *
Adelaide crematorium is now quite exten-
sively patronised by the medical profession, the
latest offering being elderly Dr. Wylde. -The
deceased was one of the promoters of the “ oven,”
and was determined no discredit should be cast
upon his hobby. His coffin was constructed of
the thinnest Californian pine, and was saturated
with spirits to help combustion. It and its con-
tents disappeared in ten minutes. Cremation, by
the way, should in time become a boon to those
individuals interested in ancestors. Some enter-
prising person will yet set up a high-class dust
shop where the ash of noble progenitors can be
bought in jars, and Ham fat, with his newly-
acquired fortune, can then set up as the heir of
a hundred burnt earls.
When are the Victorian central authorities
going to compel the Bumbles of the country
shire councils to keep the dairies and farm yards
clean? Director of Agriculture Wallace showed
the whole dirty business up the other day when
he published disgusting details from the reports
of the dairy inspectors. These reports set out
that cows were milked under the filthiest con-
ditions and that the milking utensils were en-
crusted with dirt and manure. Some of the shire
councils have tried to get back on Director
Wallace by passing resolutions condemning him.
Wallace, however, hits out vigorously and if one
were to wash the dirt off the farmers’ faces they
should be found to have black eyes. He publishes
figue3 showing that out of 120 municipalities in
which dairying is carried on to any extent, eight
only are carrying out the inspection of dairies in
a satisfactory manner, 26 are carrying out the
inspection indifferently, 38 in name only, and 48
are sitting down admiring the accumulation of
the filth, and are not causing any inspection to
be made whatever.
VENGEANCE IS A DISH
TO BE EATEN COLD.
MISSUS (who has had words with Sarah Carly
in the day, speaking loudly for the benefit of
visitors): “Have you cleaned the silver,
Sarah?”
SARAH (in a cold, distinct voice that can be heard
in the drawing room): “ Yes, mum. I've
done the knives an ’ the saucepan lid.”
Motor-’busses are starting in Melbourne,
and the horse may as well recognise that in the
end he is a lost animal. Besides casual machines
running to cricket-matches and the beach, regular
trips are now made out to Newmarket at 3d. a
head. All along the wide, empty sweep of
Flemington-road they do al2 mile gait. Private
motors become increasingly frequent, and even
the trams and the railways may find the new
arrival a thing to be reckoned with very
seriously.

UNDER COVER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In the park the other night,
She and I;
Dainty sweet she looked in white —
Bright the sky.
Soft the glorious moonlight beamed,
As we sat, and gazed, and dreamed,
Kissing infra dig. we deemed —
Others nigh.
Evening next the heavens shone
Like a lamp,
Though the maiden whispered “John,
’Twill be damp ! ”
In surprise I simply stared
“ Tumbled 1” and her view I shared.
As the dear girl promptly reared
Her big gamp !
TAS.
J. A. WALSH.
ONCE MOTE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Once more the sluggish hours go fiendish slow ;
Once more my tongue thicks hairy in my mouth ;
Once more a fattened head begins to bow ;
Once more this heavy gloom and bitter drouth.
Once more the spectre of a dead resolve ;
Once more a loathing of this inward din ;
Once more the wheels of conscience slow revolve ;
J Once more the fitful peg is shoved hard in.
—Once more 1
ILL.
A.B.
ON THE BEACII.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
You were spick and span, in your shoes of tan,
And the ivory lace at your neck, Marie.
You were sweet and fair, with your raven hair
On a marble brow—how it clustered there !
And the smile on your lips—how it laughed at care
On that summer’s day gone by, Marie,
On the yellow beach by the sea 1
You were good and pure, with your eyes demure,
And your beautiful gossamer sleeves, Marie ! -
And the little town, with its roofs of brown,
On our loves and hopes looked idly down,
And you wore a rose in your silken gown,
On that day that is dead and gone, Marie,
On the long, lone beach by the sea.
M.L.
E.V.H.
