A WOMAN'S LETTER.
Sydney, December 15, 1903.
Mr Dear Arini,—
I put in an hour at the opening day of the test
match on Friday, and came away feeling that
my status had sunk to that of a lost soul.
There were 17,000 (official figures) other lost
souls there. The majority of them sat with
eyes olosed slitwise, and downward - curved
mouths, staring out toward the circle of green
earth where some:mighty atoms fooled with a bat
and a ball. I’ve always wondered what was the
only unforgivable sin, mentioned with vague
awesomeness, but hot defined, in the New Testa-
ment. Shouldn’t be surprised if it turned out to
be cricket.
Got a look at the two grandstands from
an opposite coign. The flowered, ribboned,
feathered hats of the lost souls massed into
chromatic points gave the impression of tier upon
tier of those tiny, many-oolored sweets with
which certain sticky buns are smothered, and
which are known as “hundreds and thousands.”
Didn’t know till I got a closer and back view of
the seated thousands on the grand-stands—mostly
women—what a number of grasshopper backs we
have amongst us, and the prevailing tone of string
color in the blousing of these backs does not make
for freshness. Their Rawson Ex’s and daughter
were present, with Private Sec. Share, who
looked like the Rajah of Bhong without the
latter’s bay window. Some day cricket will be a
medium for advertising—and a bowler will hurl
a ball labelled, “Drink Somebody’s Bella Donna
Cocktails,” and the bat that stops it will carry
advice about a certain liver pill.
When Miss Ada Crossley made her re-appear-
ance at Sydney Town Hall on Saturday night the
sea of faces within her ken seemed to turn to real
climbing green seas, whilst the walls of the hall
dipped and rose and shuddered, after the manner
of a buffeted vessel. The contralto, who is visibly
thinner, had had a rough, seasick passage from
Auckland, and reached Sydney only in time to
dress for the concert. At 7.15 the Sonoma had
not quite tethered itself at the Quay. For all
that, the singer was in fine voice. All through
Maoriland she got triumphal receptions and
had some local-color experiences. At Masterton
(near Wellington) she received some Maoris of
note, who taught her one verse of. “ Home, Sweet
Home ” in their language, and she sang it that
night at her concert. After the item, Mrs. Te
Taw, the chief’s wife, threw a splendid kiwi bag
on the platform. At Palmerston, a crowd of per-
sons who could not get into the hall to hear her,
built up a very temporary scaffolding beneath
a row of windows that ran on one side of the
hall, and so a few persons at a time could see as
well as hear. When the people on the roost had
had what the crowd below considered their fair
share they were promptly hauled down. In the
middle of one of Miss Crossley’s numbers there
was a crash, and a row of heads and hands disap-
peared amid shrieks and groans from the fallen
and fallen upon. Miss C. stopped, and Herr
Scherek hurried from the piano to learn the
number of killed and wounded. There were none,
and the concert proceeded.
Some of the press notices of this concert were
sparklets. Local paper spoke of the items given
by the rest of the co. as mere “stepping stones”
to those of the principal performer, but a Mas-
terton paper damned Peterkin as “ the weak spot
on the programme.” Jacobs raged: “III! with
a European reputation, to be called a stepping-
stone ”; but Peterkin was greatly tickled.
A resident assured him that M.L. was very fas-
tidious. After Santley sang in Napier a local
paper declared, “We much prefer Mr. Jobson (a
Napierite), and he can sing much louder too.”
Also in weightier matters than music M.L.
citizens assert themselves. Some years ago,
during an English political crisis, an Invercargill
resident cabled to Lord Salisbury, “Fight or
resign.”
Whilst in Wellington Miss Crossley dropped on
some pretty songs, the compositions of a local man
—George Kennedy. One is a lullaby, which she
will sing before her present tour ends.
A woman and her vote—and sectarianism. A
man was showing her how she should treat the
ballot paper. “You put a cross opposite the
name of your candidate, and ” “Across/”
interrupted the lady, “a cross indeed ! That’s
some more of Cardinal Moran’s dirty work. I
won’t make a cross—l’ll make just a plain mark.”
New Lord Mayor Lees has been Mayor of
Sydney previously. He is a printer, has two
married and some unmarried daughters, who are
very good on the banjo.
Longstaff’s portrait of the King has been highly
co nmended by Edward VII., who recognised
himself at once. Edward, who is a master of
t ivialities, had some slight alterations made,
while he waited, in the location of the orders on
the portrait’s breast. One was a mustard seed’s
length too high, another was a sixteenth of an
inch too close to the second button of the waist-
coast. Queen Alex’s portrait will be Longstaff’s
next royal work. Her Majesty has had a narrow
escape from being a burnt offering through badly
installed electric wires—same thing that caused
s °me disastrous Sydney fires. King Edward
wasn’t at Sandringham for the fire. He generally
wasn’t and isn’t.
That poster of Blamire Young’s advertising the
new monthly, His Excellency, is the best thing
of its kind ever seen on our hoardings. Faces you
a Spanish Excellency of the old, proud, knightly,
drinking, duelling days. He rests his right,
gloved hand upon his sword, with his left hand
he has just swung off his wide-plumed hat low as
his knee. From out his love locks frowns the stern,
haughty face of a man past his prime—one who
has lived every day of his life and accepted
or wrested all that life and love and war
could offer. And now—the lion eats mice in his
old age. The memory of it all is so strong within
him, that it exudes, and you know a hush falls
upon the salons full of pretty women and young
swashbucklers whenever he is announced. You
know he pauses a moment in conscious dignity
before he carries his portly figure and armorially*
embroidered suit into the courtesying group.
To live up to that poster a paper would have to
be something very good, indeed. As it is, His
Excellency, “a paper for society,” was born
with the death-damp on its brow. And are those
portraits really representative of Sydney society ?
We own more practical, if less picturesque,
Excellencies for this workaday period. A week
or so ago our Rawson Excellency and Lady went
to _ Manly to open a charitable fete, and whilst
doing the side-shows, got on the merry-go-round
and had a whirly ride. The Empire ought to be
safe for another hundred years at least. By the
way, the owner of that merry-go-round is Gypsy
Smith, one of the specialities of Manly. He
wears gold earrings, and a seal-skin coat with
actual sovereigns for buttons down the front.
Amy Castles will appear, her teacher Bouhy
told a returned Sydneysider, in grand opera in
Paris within a twelvemonth. Her voice, accord-
ing to said ’sider, is more beautiful than ever.
Saw Carrie Moore’s two sisters in short pink
frocks in the chorus of the “Fortune-Teller,” at
the Royal, last week. Nice, bright-looking girls,
miniature editions of Carrie herself. Dorothy Vane
Menzies will come through Sydney presently en
route for her pleasure-trip to the land of the
slant-eyed peoples who cultivate chrysanthemums
and misunderstandings with Russia. Buxom
Blanche Wallace is announcing her engagement
to a Westralian rich man. The Countess of
Darnley is in Sydney for the cricket. Also Mr.
Davies, of Davies and Fehon, has secured a
divorce from his wife. He has a beautiful home
at Point Piper. The foregoing may be
classed as fashionable intelligence because of its
mention of stage people, and a countess and a
divorce.
Our friend Arthur Adams, the Maorilander, has
a short story in Nov. number of Cosmopolitan, all
about a variety-show girl, and the yellow flame-
like kowhai blossoms, and a boundary-rider with
the familiar name of Jim. It gives Arthur a
chance to describe triple-peaked Ruapehu very
prettily.
You may remember the Mackenzie Fairfaxes
who stopped awhile at the Australia Hotel, in
Sydney, a year or so ago. They stirred the society
waters by a ripple or two ; and she gave a concert
and some smart at-homes. He is a son of Mr.
Ross Fairfax. She, a pretty little body, was a
concert singer when her gentlemanly-looking,
silly-faced John wooed and won her. Well, Mrs.
M.F. has secured a judicial separation from her
husband with the custody of the child and extensive
alimony. She declined to go for a divorce although,
to quote London D. Telegraph, “he offered to
allow her £B5O if she would leave her flat in
Ashley Gardens so that he might bring another
lady to live with him there.” The arcticness of
the proposal! The discordant third who destroyed
the matrimonial duet is alluded to in the pub-
lished reports of the suit as Vera Eyton, an
actress.
The English cricket team is putting up at a
leading Sydney hotel, and has provided, or has
been provided, with a fearsome sort of flag, a
combination of the team’s own colors and of the
Berritish Union Jack. When the team is at the
hotel the flag proudly floats over the city ; when
it is away, the flag comes down. This is the story
that was told to me, but it doesn’t look quite con-
vincing. What happens to the flag when half of
the players are at home and half are out ?
Manly water chute is being opened just as The
Bulletin rushes into print. No official word was
received in this office that the Governor would
shoot the chute, or ride the camel-backed tobog-
gan, or waltz round on the fiery serpent, but in
all probability his coat-tails were seen streaming
like a pennant over the first boat-load that flew
down to the duck-pond a hundred or so feet
below. His Ex. could get more thrills out of it
than he did out of the Gypsy’s merry-go-round.
My Hobart correspondent writes:
“Ada Crossley, at Hobart, got a reception bigger
and more enthusiastic than that of any Governor
that ever went to rule the Speck Island. The
last big crowd that assembled at the railway
station was when the Cameron came from
S’Africa, but Crossley’s event might have been
the reception of Boadicea by her people after she
struck Rome in the eye. It was queenly. Mrs.
Premier Propsting . and Secretary-to-Premier
Addison met, at Bridgewater, the train that
brought Ada in the Ministerial carriage from
Launceston, and bore her in triumph to
Hobart. Then Premier Propsting, Minister
for Lands Lyne (brother of Sweet William)
and Mayor Bennison, hats in hand, went into
the carriage and did homage to Ada, and
when the voice-queen appeared on the platform
a mighty cheer rent the air. Mrs. Bennison gave
her a bouquet, and Tim Hayward, the city
organist, gave her a big basket of strawberries,
absolutely the first of the season given or sold to
anybody. Timotheus had had them specially
forced in a hothouse for Ada. Then the empresa-
oontralto was conducted to what was said to be an
open carriage, but nothing was visible but rose 3
and two horses. Five thousand people cheered
and flew handkerchiefs as Ada nestled in her bed
of roses, and drove round town to Heathorn’s
hotel, with two troopers in attendance. The two
concerts at the Royal (dress-circle and stalls 10s.)
were attended by every Hobart man and woman
who had anything to pawn for half-a-sovereign
and when the second concert was over the men
rose up in their manhood and cheered and shouted
for Ada till Frahm, the scene-painter, wept and
made a scene, and Benno Scherek tore his beauti-
ful hair with joy.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent
“The going of Governors from this square and
straight city of ours has dwindled down to the
interest of a minor show. Chinny Clarke came in
for the full flood of farewells, so that Gov.-Gen.
Tennyson, setting off with his kit and clan a
week or two later, merely got a backwash.
Besides, there was the wind-up of the Slap.-
Musgrove extravaganza as a counter attraction.
“The Mayoral holocaust to the departing
Tennysons, the other Saturday, was on the same
dizzy scale as that sent up to the minor vice-regals
of the previous fortnight. It couldn’t very well
be dizzier, seeing that the Town Hall is not built
on the extension system, and when a hall already
holds all the Dazzle that can possibly be put into
it, the impossibility of inserting any more Dazzle
is too obvious to require any further explanation.
“Quakerish grey broke out virulently at that
M'Eacbarn show. The Mayoress led off with the
demure hue, but added to it the pink roses of hope
and promise. An entire grey is as depressing as a
whitewashed circus jester. Mrs. Waxcellency
Madden was another prominent grey garber, but
her brightening process consisted of a shower of
silver sequins and a goodly outlay of jewels. In
the gem department she had them all on. Coun-
tess Darnley’s gold-brown head, with its weird
half-hoop of diamonds, had an inch or two of
advantage over most others. Her niece, Miss
Daniels, was about the daintiest little flutterer in
the throng. Her wealth of deep red hair was
massed into a lovely crown pile, leaving the brow
bare. Like most of the red-haired, Miss Daniels
has a pure cream skin. Also she had a lilac frock.
“How vastly different the strained, uncomfort-
able leave-taking of Countess Hopetoun to that
of chirpy and homely Lady Tennyson ! The
Excellency of Chill had only a sparse number of
the Chosen around her on deck, and these stood
far-off, out of sympathy, beyond the understand-
ing of that tall, shy woman with the cold lips and
the wistful eyes. Her loneliness was apparent,
but the Elect did not know how to act. So a
heavy silence hung about the scene. Suppressed
emotion was not the sign of the Tennyson send-
off. Her amiable Ex. hurriedly shook hands all
round, laughed and made her regrets in turn, and
finally hung out of the car window fluttering her
bit of cambric in a last ta-ta.
“Lady Tennyson impressed, over and over
again, upon an interviewer that she really and
truly was sorry to give up Australian life. This'
protestation seems to throw doubts upon the fare-
well expressions of the preceding Excellencies.
The next departing Vice-regentess will have to
go before magistrate Panton and swear to her
regrets.
“ May Beatty, the star-performer of the Slap.-
Musgrove court show, threw a touch of nature or
else a bit of clever acting into her rble of witness.
She declared that she loved the part of Pom-Pom,
and counsel Duffy remarked, ‘ And we all loved
it, too.’ The charmer leaned forward excitedly
saying, ‘Did you?’ For a minute she forgot—
or seemed to forget—the whole court business in
the sudden access of her young enthusiasm.
Again, ‘You are the lady,’ twitted counsel, ‘ who
charmed us with “ How I Love Society”’—to
which May, under drooping eyelids, answered
softly and with a blush, ‘I did not know I
charmed you.’
“Madame ‘Slap’ laid herself out to fetch the
jury in a chic blue-and-white scrolled costume
with white collar and cuffs, and balloon sleeves,
white gloves, narrow green belt, grey feather boa,
and sweeping black hat with black ostrich feathers.
Apparently, too, she did fetch it. She fenced
brilliantly with Isaacs, but though that eminent
counsel had no grey feather boa to impress the
public, he more than once out-manneuvred her.
Madame evidently has a temper of her own, but
she kept it well, biting her lips over unruly re-
plies that were just on the verge of escaping her, and
tapping a shapely foot. Husband * Slap ’ grinned
hugely. Much merriment was occasioned by the
recounting of the latter’s persistent endeavors to
get his better half into harness. Whenever
Musgrove was stuck for someone to take a part or
to double two, ‘Slap’ would chip in, ‘Well,
there’s the missus.’ And the ‘missus’ took her
part well, all along the line.
“Judging by their scarcity on the so-called
‘ second day ’ of the Austral meeting, the Smartest
see few points of attraction in scantilv-clad Aus-
tralian wheelers (with a few Yanks thrown in)
as compared with bronzed Britishers in well-cut
cricket flannels. The bike hero has his feminine
following. The members’ enclosure was full of
her, the other day, but she’s not the cousin, the
sister, or the aunt of the ‘ First-born of Egypt,’
as Shakspeare called the Naicest of his time.
Nevertheless, the ‘ bicycle girl ’ is young and
pretty and dressy. Her shoes are small, as a rule,
and her hat large. At the Austral meeting, the
women’s millinery crowded the men out. In the
marquees there were rows and rows of Jills with
never a Jack amongst them. Mere man was off
on his own, watching the sports unshadowed by
his best girl’s hat-brim. This year of defensive
broad-brims will be, methinks, as unfruitful of
engagements as ’93, with its financial disasters.
The man of to-day can’t get near enough to his
girl to propose, all by reason of her hat.

“ The throng took up every inch of Town Hall
space not actually required by the youthful
performers at Mrs. W. Green’s fancy ball the
other Monday night. It would seem that the
dancing craze increases, and that young Aus-
tralia’s toe becomes daily more fantastic. Irene
Dillon, the best-known young tripper of the
amateur stage, was weird, dazzling, seductive in
a Sirenesque measure called ‘La Caprice En-
chanteur,’ and the fantastic whirlings, the flash
and brilliancy of Mrs. Green’s sylph-like daughter
brought down the packed hall in her cascade pas
seul. Toodles and Babs take very joyously to the
dancing business, and school teachers complain that
it becomes as a consequence more and more diffi-
cult to drive them in the weary ways of the multi-
plication table and the steep paths of geography.
“ Apart from the very emphatic desirability of
Miss Una Bourne being despatched to the
European masters, that she may add a final touch
of inspiration to her piano play, her worn and
pallid look makes its own tender appeal for a rest,
a voyage, and a general loaf. The more her
talent grows, the more fleshless is her face, the
more luminous —in a spiritual way—her eye. All
the bodily strength of the clever pianiste seems to
be concentrated in her fingers. When she is
playing most brilliantly the pallor of the face is
alarming, and the body, if allowed to relax, might
snap. So Una needs her holiday, and, as she is
certainly Melbourne’s'most applauded and capable
strummer, the city should lose no time in pushing
her off.
“A much-talked-of item at the District Nursing
Bazaar was a doll’s trousseau (worth £6O), ex-
hibited by a fashionable drapery firm, which had
made it to the order of a little Tasmanian girl.
The lilliput outfit was a copy of a well-to-do flesh-
and-blood bride’s plenishing. The gowns had silk
linings, there were hats to suit all occasions,
festive or informal. Gloves, shoes, fans for
evening or day use respectively were on hand,
also coffee-coats, dressing-jackets, and ‘ twelve
each ’ of underwear items. Pity to see morbid
extravagance beginning in our hitherto compara-
tively healthy-minded Commonwealth ! We may
end by thinkingnoshameinlavishingsmallfortunes
on dogs’ dinner-parties and cats’ christenings, after
the manner of the degenerates of London and New
York. Don’t believe £6O, spent on a wooden-
headed toy, gives Dolly’s owner anything like
good value in pleasure. Now, if the investment
had been on behalf of a real, live baby ! Scribe
knows a 10-year-old lass, at present, who can’t sleep
o’ nights for joy and excitement, just because the
postman’s wife has had twins. Postman confessed,
when he handed in the letters, that ‘the missus had
provided skimpy enough for one, let alone two!’
Ten-year old’s mother is letting her daughter deal
with all the gaily-colored things in the little
strangers’ wardrobes —the Turkey-red, the pink

“The latent ‘Melbourne Musical'Magazine,’
published by Allan and Co., includes the song
that Ada Crossley warbles so beautifully, *Oh !
that We Two were Maying.’ There are twelve
other songs in the book —a carefully mixed lot of
ballads, comic choruses, and otherwise—but the
popular Crossley item is the most pressing induce-
ment to pay a mere shilling for the lot. By the
way, the sentiments of ‘Oh ! That We Two’ are
very conflicting. In the first verse the singer
wants to be picking flowers ; in the next she would
prefer to be seated on the grass ; her third desire
is to be lying buried beside the other person.
Yours affectionately,

PERSONAL ITEMS.
J. H. Abbott, of Vic. House of 48 Rich
Landlords, is probably the oldest newspaper pro-
prietor in Australia. He owned the Diggers’
Advocate (Bendigo) 50 years ago.
John Darling, father of S.A. Opposition
leader, says he used to drive a cart selling water
to Adelaide residents before there were any
Government waterworks in-South Australia.
Deakin spoke one night at Glen Innes ;
G. H. Reid the next. Local paper announced
its verdict—“Deakin was as much above Reid
as Heaven is above earth. ’ It was after that
Reid declared himself tiring of politics.
“ B.” : Digby Berry and Dill Macky are
said to be descendants of two of Oliver Cromwell’s
daughters. Hence their continuous desire to hew
Agag in pieces before the Lord. But Agag, as
usual, objects to be hewn, and makes a dust.
Speaker Lee Steere’s death in Westralia
robs the big wool and station firm of Dalgety of
its third head within a few weeks. First, there
was John Lackey, long N.S.W. chairman of the
co.; them W. Kilgour, manager in Sydney ; now
the Westralian chairman.

party swept into a certain railway refreshment

about catering and little of

lion of the show was named
Barton, and heard some of
the more eminent of the
menagerie caff him

An old German couple of South Australia,
Mr. and Mrs. Gerlaeh, the other day finished
their 65th year of married life. Emperor Biff,
prompted by the local consul, sent them out a
silver medal.
Profit Dowie’s wife and son travel Aus
traliawards with a lady secretary and an undesir-
able immigrant in the shape of a negro butler.
The profitess has a dressing-maid, but she isn’t
coming all the way.
Mr. J. A. Hill, a pioneer of South Aus-
tralia, has dropped into a baronetcy. Mr. Hill
was one of the little hand of ad venturers who landed
in S. A. in 1836 from the ship Africaine. He lives
now at Laura, S.A.
Many persons were under the impression
thatF. A. Wright, M.L.A. (of Wright, Heaton
and Co.), died poor. His will for probate pur-
poses has been proved at <£39,463, all of which
passes to his widow and children.
Clement Sabine, who died rather suddenly
in Adelaide, the other day, was the secretary of
the Northern Territory Co. when it was formed
in 1864. It finished a short career Hve years later.
Sabine was one of the promoters and one time a
director of the Glenelg railway.
Herbert Nicholls, the athletic Tasmanian
Attorney-General, and the most pronounced and
resolute democrat in the Propsting Government,
has not been in his place in Parliament these last
few days. He has the mumps, which is an un-
usual complaint for an Attorney-General.
Professor Henderson, of Adelaide Uni-
versity (S. A.), who is engaged in writing an official
biography of Hori Grey, is taking a trip to Cape-
town to examine in the museum there papers pre-
sented by the great pro-consul, and dig up lost
and forgotten information about his career.

and his pranks without remembering Josh Billings
apt piece of advice —“ Thare iz grate risk in play-

“Steele Rudd ” has lost his job as Queens-
land Under-Sheriff. The retrenched humorist
threatens to publish a Steele Rudd’s Journal,
also to contest the next Camboova election against
Tire Macintosh now in possession. “Steele
Rudd ” hails from Cambooya, and if popularity
counts he should oust the Scotchman who goes
ploughing in kilts.
Judge Boucaut, who stepped from the
Premiership of S.A. to the Supreme Court Bench
in 1878, is in indifferent health, and it is probah'e
that Ire will retire on a pension in a few uronths.
He is*72 years old, while Bundey is only 65. Ex-
Senator John Downer is likely to succeed Boucaut,
if Gordon’s successor as Attorney-General does
not jump his claim.
Professor M’Callum, of Sydney Univer-
sity, in allowing himself to be elected to the
committee of the Preferential Trade League, nee
Imperial Trade League, pokes his nose farther
into contentious politics than did the Professor of
English History when he elucidated aspects of the
Boer war, and was threatened by intolerant
jingoes with loss of office.
A. H. Barlow, Government representative
in the Q. Legislative Council, was the lad in the
Bank of Australasia, Sydney, who, just 50 years
ago, had the job of packing up the books, station-
ery and clock required for the opening of the first
branch in Queensland —the Moreton Bay branch,
it was called. The clock is still at work in the
Ipswich office, but the pens, it is believed, are
worn out.

John Russell, recently deceased at Tra-
ralgon, Gippsland, aged 75, was one of the first
two white men to ascend Mount Baw-Bfl-W, his com-
panion being the late Ferdinand Von Alueller.
He was also the discover of the Russell s Cteek
diggings in the same locality.
The death is announced, in Scotland, of
T. L. Learmonth. aged 84. He became a squatter
at Buninyong (Vic.) in ’37, with his late brother,
and afterwards explored the country from Mount
Macedon to the Beckwith Ranges. For a time
he sat in the old Leg. Council.
Bigjohn Forrest has been telling his old
friends at Fremantle that “his pilgrimage is
nearly at an end,” and that he will probably
return soon to live among them. Forrest
has looked all his life more like a big, indignant
invader than a pilgrim, and this kind of mild,
resigned pathos is unusual in his case.
Appointment of J. H. Gordon to the
South Australian Bench closes what is probably a
record for any place possessing three judges.
There had been no change for just on 20 years .
Thus more than half of the present Bar never
knew any judges but the Way-Boucaut-Bundey
combination which has just been-broken.

“ That Alderman Harris be elected. ” Alderman
Harris voted for that. “ That Alderman Mullins
be elected.” Alderman Mullins voted for that
Finally, “ That Alderman Lees be elected.' And
Alderman Lees voted for that.
Some rich people have been dying of late.
The will of Mrs. Jessie Blackmore Campbell,
widow of an M-L.C. and squatter, of Nowra
(N.S.W.), was proved recently at £129,166 ; and
that of, Robert Landale, squatter, of Deniliquin
(N.S.W.), has been proved at £78,476 Victorian
estate. In New South Wales he held nearly
30,000 acres, at a mere nominal rent.
When Governor Loch first arrived in Mel-
bourne, he knew so little of the duties of his
position that he thought he was expected to take
charge of a department. Immediately after
being sworn in he said to Premier Service— 1
am now ready to take over the defences. lhe
old wine-and-spirit merchant broke the news
softly to Loch that he was only an ornament.
Through Charles Harper’s succession to
the Westralian Speakership, vacant through
James Lee Steere’s death, the office goes, for the
first time, outside the Leake family. Hie original
Luke Leake was the State’s first Speaker; he
dated from the time when its only Parliament was
partly Crown-appointed and partly elected.
When he died his son-in-law, Lee Steere, suc-
ceeded.
The combining of two official positions in
the one person sometimes leads to peculiar re-
sults. Up North there is a certain Trustee in
Insolvency who is also a Curator in Insanity. On
one occasion the Trustee in Insolvency wrote a
long letter to the Curator in Insanity re a certain
estate. The Curator replied—also at length. A
spirited correspondence ensued, the Curator in
Insanity finally emerging victorious. When a
country’s insanity is triumphant over its Insol-
vency the outlook is, indeed, a gloomy one.
Old Queenslander Augustus Gregory, who
has been beknighted in his advanced age on the
ground that he did some exploring a long while
ago began that business in Westralia the year
before Big John Forrest was born. He taught
himself most of the surveying he knows, and did
the first of it with a compass of his own manu-
facture. He claims that he and his two brothers,
on their maiden expedition (in 1846), discovered
' the first coal found in the State —this was at the
Irwin River—and also located lead deposits on
the Murchison. The lead mines in that district
were subsequently worked for years, the stuff being
taken to Europe as ballast in wool ships ; but the
industry has been dead for a long while it hardly
pays anywhere now to work lead ore that doesn’t
also carry silver.
Sydney City Coroner Woore, who has just
retired on pension, was a bit of a martinet when
police magistrate at Wollongong (N.S.W.). The
sergeant of police offended his magisterial dignity
once by being seated in court. Mr. Woore
insisted on his standing ; and, with a prospect of
committal for contempt to his own lockup, the
sergeant stood. But he immediately placed him-
self in communication with Inspector-General
Fosbery, who told him that lie could remain
seated except when examining witnesses or
addressing the court. Next sitting day the ser-
geant was again peremptorily ordered to his feet,
and respectfully stated that he would see the
court boiled first, or something to that effect.
Then he handed up his documentary evidence,
and after that the heavens fell.

Z ELMAN, SENTOB, of Melbourne Block.
Writes “ G.C.” :
Architectural Mavor CorVie keeps Brisbane society from
becoming a Golgotha of dry bones and melancholy. He
entertains class and no class or Prime Minister
—to his utmost on the munificence of his official allow-
ance of £5OO per annum. Governor ’Erbert Chermside
tvpifies the splendor and mystic awe of British isolation,
plus red tape, official etiquette, and military haw-haw.
Three at-homes and a ball to the Elect in Government
House garden are faintly remembered during the past IS
months, with a dinner thrown in on the birthday of
Edward VII. On agricultural matters and the breeding
of horses Gov. ’Erbert is a giant of theory and practical
ignorance. Farmers blush at their incompetence as
Ohermside urges them to plant the turnip-tree early,
and explains how the hen’s infant should not he weaned
too young.
Another of “Spring’s” Parliamentary
pictures:
N.S.W. Lands Minister Crick once described himself as
the “ stormy petrel ”of N.S.W. politics. To-day he
might be described as the stormy cockatoo of the poli-
tical aviary. His presence generally means wild wing-
flapping and strong language, which shocks the gentle
Sleath and other prudish legislative canaries. Crick’s
piloting of the Lands Bill was a cinematograph of atti-
tudes, wild and strange. In the early stages of the Bill
his earnestness was amazing. One night I saw a careless
figure huddled up on the Ministerial chair with legs
entwined, backbone protruding, shoulders high over the
short neck, head lowered, and the mind thoroughly
concentrated on a difficult amendment. The change
from the Crick of the street and of the lounge was a joy.
Admirers hailed it as the birth of a new Crick who had
dropped his past brilliant uselessness. As the Bdl
progressed and the Melbourne Cup drew near the
Minister lost all his dash. The last days of the Bill
found him restless and seemingly unconcerned. A
Council amendment brought on what the dailies
called a “crisis.” Crick, drowsy and hoarse,
looked as if he’d been round town all the night
before, backing horses. Although he considered the Bill
a child of his own brain, the members generally held it
as much theirs as his. Consequently, the House cor-
rected and spanked its own kid with true parental vigor.
The spanking took so long that Crick crawled off the
chair and was soon curled up on the bench dreaming that
Ossian was winning the Cup by a street. He thought he
heard the scream of the bookies, and opened one eye to
see Willis from the Banvon shouting out an electioneering
speech. He listened a minute to the clear, sincere,
manly, ringing remarks of Willis, then slammed his hot
head down again with a hang. James Ashton, staid,
deliberate, and with his pants turned up out of the wet,
made some remarks which soothed the Minister to further
sleep. When hq finished,, Gormly caught the Speaker’s-
eye and clung there for about fifteen minutes. Gorinlx’s
voice is guaranteed to destroy anyone’s slumber. The
tortured Minister groaned, sweated, wriggled, muttered,
and finally flopped over to the table and squeezed his
throbbing head between his hands. The third pict.i re
is that of Crick averting the “crisis.” Having
wearily commanded Waddell and other unimportant
supporters to go home to bed, he mooched in and then-
out again to get a lemon for his throat, leaving John
Kidd in charge. The “Domino King” allowed the
division to be taken after a strong anti-Upper House
speech by Ferguson. Being defeated by two, Crick
telephoned for the sleeping supporters, and, to save
time, moved that the Chairman leave the chair. The
“crisis” stirred Crick up. Like a bear in danger,
he shook the sleep from his eyes and the
clouds from his brain and stood at bay. The
minor Oppositionists, imagining him temporarily
down, yapped, snarled and wrangled. Crick stood with
his back up bluffing, abusing, threatening to go to the
country, and checking the wild, meteorological Dr. Ross,
who wanted to know the cause of weather, with a
honeyed w6rd. He held the House by the wool until the
absent sleepers arrived. Then he tdhl the Chairman,
with a grin that the rules of the House wouldn’t allow
him to leave the chair. He went on with other amend-
ments, and carried the defeated clause later in the
evening. What a pity that such a capable man should
assist in transforming what should be a dignified House
into a hack-yard where all the cats in creation hold forth
at night '

FAIR WARNING.
DICKY (with the pup): “Better look out what
you’re about, you blokes—he killed a China-
man once!”
“Ne’er-do-Weel”: With reference to
children “spitting their death” (B. 8/10/’O3).
Though this system appertains to the female
children in the Queensland bush and in Adelaide,
I have noticed a different custom among the
school children (female again) in Brisbane. Here,
the narrator who observes that her story is being
received with marks of disbelief suddenly breaks
off, places her finger in her mouth, withdraws it,
holds it up, and says solemnly, “That’s wet.”
Allowing this self-evident fact to soak into the
minds of her audience, she draws her finger sud-
denly across her throat, holds it up again, and
continues, “That’s dry. Gawd’ll cut my ’ead orf
if I tells a lie.” Then she goes on with her yarn.
There seems to be some analogy between these
ceremonies and certain aboriginal rites, not to
mention portions of the ritual of some Mutual
Millinery Lodges.
* * * *
“ Cornelius ” : Man told me t’other day
that he once saw a number of crows flying up
with pebbles in their leaks, which they dropped
from aloft. On going over to investigate he
found a nest of emu eggs, which the birds were
tr\ing to break. 1 think he was au Ameri-
can ; I’m sure he was a liar.
They are cutting things pretty fine nowa-
days in Queensland. From the report of Dr.
Roth on the aboriginal mission station at Cape
Bedford, Far North (supported mainly, by the
way, by a Dutch society in Batavia): —
With the present prices ruling for flour, &c., the
Government grant of £250 is spent wholly and solely on
necessary rations, so that any bought clothing is out of
the question. Common decency, however, required that
the children should have a covering of some sort, a
difficulty which Mrs. Schwarz overcame by cutting up
calico flour-bags, piecing them together, and herself
doing all the sewing. During the past ten years she has
thus made some fifty pairs of trousers for the boys
attending school, and several dresses for the girls.
On another point Dr. Roth has this interesting
remark -
In the course of a conversation with the superintendent
as to the limits to the school subjects that should be
taught to our Australian natives, I learn that, after 17
years’ continuous residence among the Cape Bedford
blacks, he is in agreement with me in the view that no
practically useful results can possibly accrue by teaching
our mainland blacks composition, fractions, decimals, or
any other subjects that will in any way enable them to
come into competition with Europeans.
* * * *
“Thurla”: The bush birds were forced
during the dry spring of 1902 to regulate their
families, owing to the hard times, and very little
nesting was done. But this spring the cries of
young birds fill the land. A pair of wagtails
built near my camp and reared a brood of three,
and as soon as they could fly one of the parents
started to build a new nest, whilst t’other attended
to the wants of the still dependent family. They
were shoving along the birth-rate.
* * » »
“Elmore”: Wonderful how tame bush
birds and animals become when not molested.
Have been camping alone for three weeks, and
already have half-a-dozen pets—magpies, wood-
peckers, and a jackass. On warm days a gohanna
sits placidly at the door waiting for scraps, never
heeding the magpies who snap viciously at him.
In the evenings a brown bush-rat runs fearlessly
about, foraging for crumbs, and a wag-tail
whistles half the night on the ridge pole, some-
times coming to the door and having a tuck-in.
Magpies are the worst-tempered, and also the
biggest thieves, and the hungriest. Their great
delicacy is centipedes. Have turned over logs
for them till I got tired ; one magpie had eaten 44
centipedes before I left off log-rolling. Then I
went back to the tent, and found that some other
magpies, helped by the gohanna, who had opened
the tucker-box for them, had taken possession.
No bread was left, the gohanna was finishing a
leg of mutton, and some billions of ants were
making themselves ill with jam. The bush-rat
had gorged himself into convulsions, while the
jackass was howling himself hoarse with delight.
In two seconds the slaughter was over. Hence-
forth bush pets are off.

“Brumby”: What a flow of “ skite ”
there is about the Horsetralian youth. Pass a
group of two or three standing in the street and
the chances are
10 to 1 you’ll
overhear one
relating, amidst
a critical sil-
ence, how he
“stoushed the
bloke wid a
brick’’and then
had to lay out
several police-
men before he
could get away.
H e describes
the hits and
other incidents
with appro-
priate gestures
and with a glow
of enthusiasm
quite foreign to
his usual air of
gloomy reserve.
The trait is just
as noticeable in
the other sex,
and Jane may
be heard drop-
ping her “h’s”
with dignified
acerbity as she tells her friend Mary
Ann just what she said to the missus
when remonstrated with by the old cat
for breaking a few plates and com-
ing home after midnight from the
“darnce.” The raconteur always
emerges from these encounters with triumph, the
enemy being routed with great slaughter. Per-
haps these aesthetic flights of imagination act as a
safety-valve, and prevent many a squalid tragedy.
When a man has killedsix policemen in his own
mind he has slaked his thirst for gore; but for this
outlet he might go and be very uncivil to one real
policeman.

W.A. Bushman writes :—•
Have ever your aboriginaliar woodcutters noticed what
appears an optical illusion in the bush when tree-felling
where straight sticks are required ? I have been a victim
many a time. Repeatedly I have sighted straight trees
from about 200 yards, but on approaching same have
found them as crooked as blazes. Have discovered the
reason of this, and now never make a mistake. You have
either to sneak on the tree, taking advantage of every
cover, or else rush it with all your might, with axe
behind you, before tree gets a chance to bend. If you
are not smart the trunk will curl 17 different ways.
Some of my majgs, when I told them this, said it was
too strong, even for a mining expert (which I am not,
because, although poor, I am honest) ; but when they
tried it they discovered I was right. It’s wonderful
what intelligence trees possess. Young salmon gum and
gimlet are very cunning. Had some experience in New
South Wales with mountain ash, which is frightful stuff
to warp when green. Have known it to warp itself into
a reef knot in two days, and Bxl battens would plait
themselves together like a stockwhip. Had a sawmill in
Kanimbla Valley, and the stuff was so restless that we
had to build a stockyard round it to keep it in bounds.
When we had a couple of truck loads cut, used to open
the sliprails, and the planks warped themselves to the
station right into trucks on the siding with the aid of
two sheepdogs.
“STEELE RUDD” AND HIS FAVORITE POLO PONY.
Photo, by Poul C. Poulsen, Brisbane.
“ Wanderer,” who says his address is
“Kicking Round the World,” writes from Floria
nopolis, Brazil: —
Australia experienced a rough time from the tick.
Here we have the tick also. The natural enemy of the
tick is a bird called the ano. These birds in great flocks
sweep over the open country here and clear the ticks off
the cattle. Might it not pay Australia—especially
Queensland —to import some of them with a view to their
acclimatisation ?
Australia has imported so many birds and animals,
and has fallen in so often, that it will probably
prefer to send Frank Farnell across as a Fishery
Commissioner to inspect the ano before buying.
If our local crow, who seems to have a good deal
of spare time on his feet, could be induced to
tackle the tick, or if the rabbit would learn to
run him down and bite him, it would certainly be
better than fooling with new fowl that might go
back on us.

“ G.H.” : The Mongol who supplies my
house with vegetables (none being obtainable from
any other source) brings daily bunches of flowers
and offers same at the shrine of Maly Ann.
Maly Ann, who is a humorous female, at
times affects coyness, and it is most touch-
ing to hear the Chow Romeo whisper pain-
fully, “ You likee losheebud (rosebud),
Maiy Ann ? Plitty losheebud.” The red-
haired Juliet is an expensive charmer,
and leaves Wing very little cash for fan-
tan or pak-a-pu ; in addition to flowers he
has to further bribe her with about half
a cwt. of preserved ginger per week. Wing
is a bit of a humorist, too, and occa-
sionally pretends, in a high - )Itched,
chrome voice, to be the “ Butchel ! ” or the
“ Bakel !” or some other tradesman. And
when Mary shoves her head hastily forth
and yells “ threepounderchops,” the simple
joy of that Chow is just like the joy of the
domestic magpie or tame raven that has
come up behind the mooning cat and barked,
thus making the animal leap three feet in
its sudden surprise.
“ Tambo ”: At a bush shanty out
West (temperature 115 in the shade) one of
half-a-dozen men who were Xmasing died
suddenly, and his mates, desirous of giving
him decent burial, adjourned with the corpse
to an adjacent sand-hill, where, after clumsily
covering defunct, they sang with due solemnity
“The Ship that Never Returned.” Having
thus done their best by their departed brother
they returned to “fill ’em up again.”
“ Rypo ” : “The prospects for a record
harvest are excellent.” “A record wheat-yield is
anticipated in this district.”—(Extracts from any
recent paper in Victoria.) Hence, the next
trouble with the poor farmer will be a shortage of
harvesting hands. Then the Government will be
approached with some violence for help. Men
will be wanted from the Solomon Islands, South
Pole—any where. The good old socialistic farmer
man is sure to be on top of his haystack yelling
for State aid, whatever happens.

“ Kerani ” : The Bairnsdale correspon-
dent of the Argus got in a good one recently
about a schoolboy who induced a swarm of bees
to settle on his waistcoat, walked a quarter of
a mile to the school with the insects, put them
into a box, and did not get a sting. I do not
know the dimensions of this correspondent, so
I’ll not call him names. I only know that I
myself once acted as a conveyance for part of
a swarm of bees for about half a mile, through
pretty rough country. I don’t know whether the
insects were satisfied with their trip or not, but
I got all I wanted. My brother (who was present
when I started) says there were only four bees on
me altogether, and two of them were blowflies,
but you can’t take much notice of him—he wasn’t
brought up as strictly as I was.
The luck of the Victorian Mallee cocky is
awful. Last year he came in hundreds to S.W.
Victoria and struck the driest time experienced in
those parts for 50 years. While battling with
almost insurmountable difficulties in the south he
missed the only good season up north for 10
years. Now the caterpillars have been going
through his crops, and leaving nothing but the
headless straw. It is about 13 years since S.W.
Victoria was last visited with this pest.

“J. Carew ” : After dark, every night,
we hear at intervals, carried along the ground,
and conveying no idea of distance or direction, a
long plaintive boom. The line runs in our memo-
ries, “The bittern booms.” Are there bitterns
in Australia ? And what is a bittern ? And why
and how does it boom ? In many years’ bush-life
have never heard the sound before.
A THREEPENNY DOSS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mystic seance, strange communion hold I in the doss-
house here ;
There are many spirits present, also many brands of beer.
Comes to my discerning nostril from the man in bunk
below
H raith of Rum —it came from Queensland not so very
long ago.
It has taken to tobacco even in its fiery youth,
And its label very likely indicates its scorn of truth.
Unsophisticated spirit loveth not to scoff and jeer
At its derelict disciples dossing by the dozen here !
From an evidently fresh and most adjacent son of toil
Wicked reek of Whisky rises garbed in fiery fusel oil.
Spirit born of humble parents—“ poor but honest” if you
will,
Save occasional connection with a bush illicit still.
Bred they this rebellious spirit, which developed as it
grew
Great reluctance of increasing Edward Rex’s revenue.
And the early introduction of companions exercised
Influence that left their offspring thoroughly demoralised.
Bushmen drank it, for the bushman loves a spirit that
can fight—
Yea, he almost worships whisky warranted to slay at
sight.
Jamborees of joy gigantic 1 Sprees of infinite excess !
Drunks defying all describing, awful in their sinfulness 1
But I tarry, other spirits astral apprehension sees ;
Let us leave this spirit to his damned indecent devotees.
Biting ghost in bunk above me ! More transparent fraud
perhaps!
Name coincidental merely, or did humor name it
Schnapps ?
Once “white sprit,” cheap and nasty, till man’s
inhumanity
Unto man made countless thousands drink of it un-
wittingly.
Blend of subtle toxic agents such as set the brain aflame,
How it gloats above its victim’s tattered livery of shame !
Spirit that parades as Brandy haunts the dosser over
there;
Brandy? Not by many jugs full! That’s the demon’s
“nom de guerre.”
Possibly potato spirit; stranger to the soil of France.
‘ Brandy ” on the label ? Bless you, that is but a circum-
stance !
Generous the juice of grape is, never serves disciples
thus.
This disciple wouldn’t drink it—likes it less innocuous.
I, however, am digressing—time for persiflage is past
While yon terrifying spectre looms abnormally and vast.
’Tis the dominating demon, boss of all Beelzebubs ;
Beer—the fetish of their worship—all these sweepings of
the pubs !
Bah! these fearsome exhalations! I am sick at sight
and smell—
Noisome devils populating this, their temporary hell.
Choked with devils ! Breathing devils ! Devils, devils
everywhere !
Watchman, let me out of Hades ! Ah 1 the blessed,
blessed air!
W. A. PEAKE.

Pearson’s Magazine for October contains
an article on an old man kangaroo, in the shape
of a history of his life, told by the monster him-
self. Among other things, the narrating ’roo was
born on the Clarence and spent his youth on the
Murrumbidgee, having casually hopped across.
MADAME SLAPOFFSKI.
Photo, by Talma, Sydney & Melbourne.
“Ess.”: Precautions are being taken at
many places in N.S.W. to guard against bush
fires, the risk of which is unusually great this
year in consequence of there being little stock
beyond caterpillars to keep down the prolific
growth of grass that followed the rains. Settlers
are forming themselves into amateur fire brigades,
and each man is pledged to look carefully round
himself every five minutes for smoke, and to keep
a telescopic eye and a blunderbuss on any chance
swagman who obtrudes on the horizon. New
handles are wedged into tomahawks, and the
wood axe is ground in readiness for chopping
bushes, and a supply of bags tied on the ends of
sticks is kept on hand. Instructions have been
given to station-masters to transmit telegraphic
and telephonic messages free of cost, so that
when Jones drops a match, or a spark from
his pipe, he can ring up Smith and Robin-
son to come and put their conjoint foot
on it and prevent a national disaster. Wax
matches are taboo for three months, and store-
keepers are asked to sell nothing but safeties
(made in Germany). It is rather amusing to note
that the persons who started all the hubbub anent
the anticipated blaze are those who drive about in
buggies, with “refreshers” under the seat, and
shy empty bottles out as they go along. A bottle
and a hot sun will start a fire very quickly in dry
grass—and the unwary swagman, who happens to
Eass the bottle within reasonable time of the out-
reak, runs a big risk of being lynched.
* * * «
A cry from Maoriland :
Recently attended a Farmers’ Union picnic. Of all
mean, niggardly affairs it took the bun. Trains arrived
up till dinner-time. People mooned around the one-horse
township looking for the picnic. At 2 o’clock a youth
with a cracked bell went round informing intending
picnickers to roll up “ in the paddock back of the pub.”
They went and found a five-acre paddock to stare at each
other in. There was just about nothing to eat. An
hour before train time some superannuated old fogies
stood on a beer-barrel and shouted long addresses about
the glorious union of its members. Some girl races were
run, the winners receiving lucky-packets. Then the
show shut up for the night.

“ 0.C.” I was travelling between Car-
coar and Cowra (N.S.W.) one hot, dusty, mid-
summer day. Coach crowded, passengers listless
and thirsty. Pulled up to water horses and re-
fresh generally at a roadside pub known as “ The
Sheet of Bark,” and kept by Jack Lynch, a
brother of Andrew, who for some time
represented Carcoar in N.S.W. Leg.
Assembly. The passengers quickly dis-
mounted and swarmed into the bar.
Jack was attending, but he had nothing
to sell, “Not a drop av anything.
They were burying old Mrs. O’Some-
thing yesterday in the little cemetery
beyant, and the mourners drank me out
of every drop I had.” There is a little
cemetery about a quarter-of-a-mile from
the Sheet of Bark, and we glanced list-
lessly at Mrs. O’Something’s grave as
we went on our way dry. Andrew
Lynch, brother of the host of The Sheet
of Bark, was a quaint “cuss.” When
member for Carcoar a constituent hur-
ried out to his home one day with the
intelligence that a certain road-in-
spectorship was vacant, and the con-
stituent wanted Andy to get it for him.
As an incentive, he added: “There’s
,£4OO a year hanging to it.” “Is there,”
said Andy: “well, make your mind
aisy, for I’ll apply for the billet for
myself.” Members were not paid in
Andrew Lynch’s day.
* * * *
Though the Rabbit Act has been
in force for several months the N.S.W.
Government has made no move towards
destroying the rabbits on reserves which
are, as a rule, densely timbered and
inaccessible to trappers. Owners of
land are compelled to destroy rabbits,
but where privately owned land abuts on
unoccupied Crown lands the owner
hasn’t Buckley’s chance of keeping his
land free from the pest. The Government should
be treated as a private owner, liable to prosecu-
tion for not destroying its rabbits.
“Ku Klux”: Apropos of nothing in
particular, I spent a day in Jerilderie once. It
was Federal-referendum day,
and we were building a wool-
shed at a station close by.
Nineteen men decided to take
a day to go and record their
votes. After a wild and woolly
time we all got back safely
except one man ; he had been
run in, but turned up later.
Being curious to know how
all had voted, I canvassed the
nineteen, and found that six-
teen had clean forgotten all
about the yes or no question
till after the booths closed.
One of the remaining three had
carefully scratched out “ No,”
then as carefully wrapped up
the paper, and put it in his pocket, where
it was found next day. The second had written,
“Ned Kelly was the best blanky man ever
was in this town,” and he also pocketed it.
The third wrote: “This blanky country is
going to perdition.” How did I vote ? I was one
of the sixteen.

THE SELECTOR’S WIFE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Her husband’s gone where husbands go, when droughts
are hard out-back,
And rents are due and wheat-crops fail—to face the
droving track.
All through the long, lone day she lives a soulless, aim-
less life,
Environed by the solitudes that speak to her of strife.
And watches from her homestead door the saffron whirl-
winds rise
And mournful move across the plain, their spouts turned
to the skies.
She sees the dancing mirage like a ruffl.'d lakelet gleam,
The throbbing, flowing heat-waves rise like colonnades of
steam.
The unrelenting sun-flames beat around her sere, dry
home,
And nought but phantom cloudlets rise to deck the
steely dome.
She sees the setting of the sun—the rising of the moon —
And soft she hears across the bush the sunset breezes
croon ;
She sees the dusky night come down, and one by one the
stars
Drift into sight; and, rustling low, she hears the bush
belars.
She sees the brilliant, sad stars shine, and hears the
sough of trees,
And far across the flattened earth the sighing of the
AorossHflie Godforsaken Land the distant curlews call,
And deep within her heart she feels the pathos of it all.
And from the tortuous road that funs the clumps of
timber through
She sees the gleam of travellers’ fires ascending to the
blue.
The wild swan calls above her home, the wild duck
answers back,
And far within the forest gloom she hears the dry limbs
And then some night the sound of hoofs she hears adown
The house dogs bark—she knows it is her husband
coming back.
N.S.W.

No notice (except of a hostile character) will be taken of
communications insufficiently stamped. Name and
address must appear on MS. No liability is under-
taken in any case re contributions voluntarily sub-
mitted, whether sent by post or handed in ; and in
no case will MS. be returned unless stamps (of any
State) accompany.

WHERE THE DRY BONES BAKE.
I’ve tramped far out, and I’ve knocked about
In the western drought-cursed land
Where the swagmen tire and the sun strikes fire
On the hungry wastes of sand.
Where the dry bones bake and the deadly snake
Reigns king of the reeking corpse,
And the bleaching skull, that is void and null,
That the blist’ring red sun warps.
Where the gaunt trees stand on the scorching sand,
In the mirage like spectres grim,
With a fiery brand in each fleshless hand,
And death on each fleshless limb.
'Tis the land of doubt, where the brave snuff out
Who follow the Dead-Man-Track
To the goal accursed with eternal thirst—
To th« Western Hell “ Out Back.*

NOT WANTED.
“The Federal nominations to-day created no surprise of any note. Owing to attacks made upon him in a section of the press, which accused him of having been a pro-Boer during the
South African war, Mr. J. J. Wilkinson retired from the contest just before the nominations closed.” —Westralian paper.

FOREIGN-TRADE GRANNY: “NO, YOU DON’T; YOU WERE A PRO-BOER, AND WE DON’T WANT ANY OF THEM.”
CANDIDATE: “BUT I’M A PRO-AUSTRALIAN.” GRANNY: “WE DON’T WANT THEM EITHER.”

YOU CAN BE CURED.
You may have been suffering even for years from
Rheumatism
Gout
Neuralgia
Backache
Sciatica
Indigestion
Anaemia
4 f
Blood Disorders
Biliousness
Jaundice

Sick Headache
and you will continue to suffer unless the disorder is treated, as it ought to be treated, by striking
direct at the cause. All the disorders mentioned are due to one cause and one cause alone; namely
the disability of the
KIDNEYS AND LIVER
to perform the work allotted to them in Nature’s Scheme. Nature will tolerate no irregularity.
When the kidneys and liver are working perfectly, it is impossible for anyone to suffer from any
of the disorders named. In order that this important fact may be realised, the following description
of the work performed by those vital organs is given :
The Kidneys filter and extract from the blood about three pints of urine every day. In this
quantity of urine are dissolved about an ounce of urea, ten to twelve grains in weight of uric acid,
together with other animal and mineral matter varying from a third of an ounce to nearly an ounce,
When the kidneys are in health, all this solid matter is in solution and is invisible. Directly the
kidneys, through either weakness or disease, become unfit to do their duty properly, a proportion of
the solid matter remains in the blood, becomes actively poisonous and causes us to suffer from uric
disorders such as Rheumatism, Gout, Neuralgia, Lumbago, Backache, Sciatica, Gravel,
Stone, Bladder Troubles, and Bright’s Disease. A simple test to make as to the condition
of the kidneys is to place some urine, passed the first tiling in the morning, in a covered glass, and
let it stand until next morning. If it is then cloudy, or there is a brick-dust like sediment, or if
particles float about in it, or it is of an unnatural color, the kidneys are not healthy, and no time must
be lost in adopting remedial measures, or Bright’s Disease, Diabetes, or some less serious but more
painful illness will result.
The Liver. —ln the liver various substances are actually made from the blood. Two or three
pounds of bile are thus made from the blood every day. The liver takes sugar from the blood,
converts it into another form, and stores it up so as to be able to again supply it to the blood,
gradually, as the latter requires enrichment. The liver changes uric acid, which is insoluble, into
urea, which is completely soluble, and the liver also deals with blood corpuscles which have lived their
life and are useful no longer. When the liver is inactive or diseased we suffer from Indigestion,
Biliousness, Anaemia, Sick Headache and Blood Disorders.
The health of the liver and of the kidneys is so closely connected that it is almost impossible for
the kidneys to be affected, and the liver to remain healthy, or vice versa.
It is nearly thirty years since scientific research, directed specially to diseases of the Kidneys
and Liver, was rewarded by the discovery of the medicine now known throughout the world as
Warner’s Safe Cure. It was realised, at the outset of the investigation, that it was necessary
to find a, curative agent which would act equally upon the kidneys and upon the liver, these
organs being so immediately associated in the work of dealing with the body’s waste material, and,
after many disappointments, the medicine which possessed the required action in the fullest
degree was at length discovered. Warner’s Safe Cure cures all diseases of the kidneys
and liver, and, by restoring their activity, these vital organs are enabled to rid the body, through
the natural channels, of the urinary and biliary poisons, the presence of which in the system, are the
cause of Rheumatism, Gout, Neuralgia, Lumbago, Backache, Sciatica, Blood Disorders, Anaemia,
Indigestion, Biliousness, Jaundice, Sick Headache, Gravel, Stone, Bladder Troubles, and General
Debility. Warner’s Sate Cure cures all these disorders simply by removing the cause of the disorder.
This is the reason why cures effected by Warner’s Safe Cure are permanent cures.
A treatise containing full particulars, and accounts of many remarkable cures effected, even when
hope of recovery was despaired of, will be sent post free, by H. H. Warner and Co’., Limited,

Salvarmy Culls for Australia.
This amazing item of information comes from
Westralia
Perth, Thursday.—The Minister for Lands has arranged
with the’Salvation Army regarding immigration on the
following lines: —The Army authorities in England to
nominate immigrants at the office of the Agent-General,
the immigrants to pay half the boat passage, and the
Government the other half; if the immigrant remains in
the State, or resides for six months on the land selected
for him by the Crown, the passage money to be refunded
bv the Government; the commission to be paid to the
Army or persons nominating such immigrant at any time,
after the passage money is refunded will be : Single men,
£2 per head ; married men, £3 per head, with three chil-
dren, £si The Minister is willing to negotiate similarly
with other religious bodies.
Apparently the case may be condensed thus :
(1) The Salvarmy selects the immigrants from its own
crowd, consisting mostly of the wastrels of the slums —
from among the ignorant, feeble-minded characters
with the retreating chins and retreating foreheads, who
are the slaves of Booth and the abject followers of the
big drum.
(2) Westralia supplies these wasters with land.
(3) Westralia pays their passages in full if they remain
six months in the country.
(4) Westralia also pays £2 per head to the Army—
£2o,ooo for 10,000 slum wrecks who will vote as the Army
tells them to, and waste the State’s substance by sending
it to Booth, and who will bring the physical degeneracy
of the back lanes of the big English cities to taint the
blood of Australia.
In view of the fact that the Australian Govern-
ment lias exclusive power to make laws dealing
with immigration, it is to be hoped that it will
deal energetically with this proposal to plant
colonies of Booth’s underfed and physically
degenerate serfs in Australia. The population of
Australia is increasing just now at the rate of
over 53,000 a year. Its case isn’t so bad that
it needs to import the culls of any sect or creed.
Especially it doesn’t want to have its population
selected for it by any pope, bishop, priest, parson,
or big-drum howler. The Westralian agreement
represents the last extreme of lunacy- On the
terms offered Westralia’s own agents could get
the best agricultural classes in England, and
could save the commission or head money that it
is to pay the Army for exporting Booth’s cast-offs.
The M.L. Globe-Trotter.
A shout from Maoriland :
Everything seems to indicate that Maoriland aims at
becoming a side-show for the benefit of the ignorant
globe-trotter. It is really pathetic to observe the eager-
ness of the Government, which is firmly convinced that
the tourist, being the only specimen of the human race
who brings cash into a country without taking any out of
it, is the sort of thing that cannot be too much en-
couraged. You can’t go into the smallest and most
tenth-rate township without meeting a Tourist Bureau.
The Government has a Tourist Department, with scores
of high-paid officials; it advertises extensively in the
English papers about the dusky Maori maidens and the
amazing geysers ; it sits up nights thinking out new ways
to bring the tourist to the country ; it has imported huge
quantities of fish and game, and is going to import wild
beasts for the tourists to shoot at; and it acts generally
as if the salvation of the place depended on the arrival of
the person with a monocle and a vacant stare, and that,
if he doesn’t come, the islands may as well relapse into
oblivion forthwith. The other day, the farmers of the
roadless and boggy section of Hawke’s Bay determined to
get a road so as to cart their milk to the creameries.
Accordingly they represented to the Government that,
away back at Whakaremomona, there was some hair-
raising scenery that the tourist ought to see, and the
Govt, got up in its nightshirt and immediately voted the
money for the road. The most ludicrously pathetic in-
stance of the Govt.’s anxiety to attract the tourist
was the clause in Seddon’s Licensing Bill which pro-
vided that a tourist might obtain a license to drink in
the prohibited areas, although it was proposed to make it
an--offence for anyone, except the Government Agent who
supplied tourists, to have a single drop of alcohol in his
possession, in those districts. It isn’t possible to pick up
a city newspaper without seeing ten or a dozen para-
graphs about the Tourist Bureau, and how many gallons
it perspired in the preceding 24 hours in its efforts to
write up a spicy advertisement to catch the tourist, and
how it had purchased cigars and whisky for the tourist,
and how it is making arrangements to eventually lie
down and let every tourist have a free walk over its joint
and collective body. It is the only real enthusiasm, the
only hobby of the Government. Whisper “ tourist,” and
the Government rushes up frantically, and buys you a
drink, and leans its ear up against you with feverish
anxiety for any sort of a suggestion you have to make.
Now, this is a young State, with lots of possibilities and a
lot to do before it can put on frills and take an evening
off. What it ought to do is to put on its moleskins, and
go to bed early, and get up early, and hustle vigorously,
and pay its debts, and gradually get quit of Bull-Coiie'n,
and eventually get a credit balance. It can do so easily
enough ; but as long as it has the tourist notion on the
brain, arid looks hungrily for his money, and believes
that it must remain poor unless it gets money from out-
side, it won’t be in the proper frame of mind for honest
work. There isn’t an instance on record of any country
amounting to anything which places its trust in the
arrival of people who say “Haw!” at the geysers and
glac.ers, and that is what Maoriland is doing more and
more every day.
Sydney Consecrated Press on the debate in
N.S.W. Assembly on Arthur Griffith’s Bill to
education absolutely free :
By bringing forward once more the question of free
education, Mr. Arthur Griffith hoped, we presume, to
gain some popular favor in his Senatorial campaign. He
will probably find that he was mistaken in his hopes.
There is no popular demand for pauper schools. . . . The
most extraordinary feature of the debate was the attitude
of certain Catholic members of the House. The election
of a Catholic to Parliament is usually regarded as a gain
to the Catholic community. It is generally thought that
at least they will put the Catholic case when the occasion
arises, and endeavor to prevent injustice. But here we
find Catholic members like Messrs. M’Mahon, Daley,
Dacey, Burgess, Sullivan, Power, Gormly, D. Mac-
Donell, and Fitzpatrick voting for a proposal to still
f urther penalise Catholics in the matter of education. . . .
Catholics have certainly nothing to gain by the presence
of such men in Parliament, and the sooner they face their
constituents the better.
It is piffle of this sort which is largely responsible
' Dill-Mackyism. Fortunately, the great mass
of Roman Catholics ignore it, and the mischievous
rag which prints it, just as the great mass of Pro-
testants ignore the shrieksome campaign of Dill
Macky. The Bulletin would not give the
matter the notoriety of republication but for the
fact that the squeal of the Green Pig paper shows
again, what has been patent to every fair-minded
man since Cardinal Moran’s defeat at the Federal
convention elections, that the Roman Catholics of
the community don’t vote as R.C.’s and are not in
any numbers influenced by their spiritual leaders
in matters of polities. Here we have a list of R.C.
members, from all sides of the House, refusing to
follow the wishes of their church in a matter of
politics. There could be no clearer proof of the
want of logical basis for the Dill Macky madness.
From the report of an interview with Editor
Mather, of Johannesburg Tribune, who was
lately visiting London :
“ The British, the men who have sacrificed everything
during the war and for whom nominally that war was
entered upon, are more bitter against Lord Milner and
the domination of the financiers than the Dutch them-
selves. ’
“ Is the position, then, worse to-day than it was before
the war?”
‘ Incredibly worse. We had a certain freedom under
the Dopper rule ; there is none under the rule of De
Beers. Even a public meeting cannot be held without
permission of the authorities.”
(( And the authorities are ? ”
‘ The creatures of the financiers almost to a man.”
“Viator” : Next to the Capitol, nothing
interests you so much in Washington as the
Smithsonian Institute. Its history is very pecu-
liar. Smithson, who died in 1829, aged 64, was a
natural son of the Duke of Northumberland, and
did not consider himself well-treated on the
father’s side. However, he inherited a large for-
tune from relatives of his mother, a widow named
Made. Smithson said, “I will do something to
make my name remembered for ever, when the
Northumberlands are all forgotten, and kings
have perished from the earth.” He was an
irreconcilable Republican, though English,
but had never been in America. He
bequeathed all his property to the _U.S.
Government, to found a Smithsonian Institute,
for the increase and diffusion of knowledge. The
amount was £104,000. The institute was not
founded till 1846, by which time the money had
swelled up greatly, and a number of other con-
tributions were added. The outcome is the
greatest scientific institution in America, and
Smithson is reckoned in the front rank of the
national benefactors. The Smithsons of Nor-
thumberland now call themselves Percy, but the
institute throws the old plain family name at them
like an unburied crime.

DIARRHOEA.
Diarrhoea, in its first stages, can be cured by
a few doses of that pleasant, reliable, and effec-
tual medicine, Chamberlain’s Colic and Diar-
rhea Remedy. In many instances one dose is
sufficient, but as a rule three or four doses are
necessary. It is a good thing to keep in the
house. Get a bottle to-day; it may save a life.*

his cases, and the harder the task the better he
likes it, and wins all the time. 21 Bligh-street.*
