A DOG AND ITS DUTY.
“Last elections these Protectionists came crawling along and
said, ‘Let us have fiscal peace; let us return good Protectionists;
never mind their Protection; it is not a thing to divide good,
honest men.’ But the moment they got the power they showed
their teeth —both rows of them—and brought down a wretched
system of taxation.” —GEORGE REID.
This is an allegorical representation of the way
they showed their teeth.
“ As a work of fiction, Sydney Telegraph
is well worth a penny.”—G. H. Reid a while
ago.
From Kalgoorlie (W.A.):
Piggott’s no-confidence motion in W.A. Assembly
failed to shift anything. Piggott seemed most hurt
by the threatened invasion of rabbits, but most people
realise that Piggott as Premier would be a worse calamity
even than rabbits.
Laborite Prendergast jerked in a neat but
unfinished interjection during the discussion on
the Vic. Assembly Electorates Bill: “Footscray
is, in sentiment, as far away from Melbourne as is
Geelong; indeed, the natural boundary between
Footscray and Melbourne is a bad smell.” Two
srne'ls, Mr. Prendergast. Melbourne smells of
land-banks ; Footscray, of bone-mills.
More Melbourne Age remarks on Irvine :
The system of borrowing from trust funds in the hope
that good times will enable repayments to be made has
been adopted, with the result that a practice has been
followed which, if copied by private institutions, would
land the officials in the dock.
One very loud joke last week was the
tremendous speech of Mr. “Jack” Want on the
blessings of Freetrade, and his denunciation of
trimmers of all sorts. And Mr. “ Jack ’’ Want
was the sham Freetrader who was violently
ejected from office for “sneaking in Protection”
in Jennings’ time, not so many years ago.
Melba's popper, old Dave Mitchell, has
offered the Victorian Government 2000 acres of
land “suitable for closer settlement.” Needless
to mention that Mr. Mitchell doesn’t propose to
present this bit of his adopted country to the
Government as a Xmas box, but wants to sell it
(probably for more money than any private
purchaser would pay). Therefore his proposal is
public-spirited, in a sense.
Ex-Home Secretary Foxton (Q.) has a
curious record on franchise matters. He sup-
ported the property vote consistently from the
first, till 1901. Has supported since then one-
adult-one-vote and the baby vote; and, later, he
came forward in furtherance of a queer Bill com-
mingling the old property vote with the female
franchise. Airey (Labor member) dubbed him
the Apostle of Lop-sided Franchise.
The Tasmanian Legislative Council, has
now thrown out every item of the Propsting Gov-
ernment’s Reforn programme—its rejection, by 14
votes to 4, of the new Electoral Bill is the last
straw. It is no use for Propsting or anyone else
to be a reforming Premier in Tasmania without a
reliable scheme for slaying the Upper House as
part of the programme; he might as well be a
dervish, or something in a museum.
Premier See, of N.S.W., has at last sent
in his bill to the Australian Government for pro-
perties taken over. He claims £4,000,000 and
interest from the time the properties were trans-
ferred. Treasurer Turner’s reply about the back
interest is so businesslike as to be almost snappish.
He has returned to N.S.W, the three-fourths of
its customs and excise revenue mentioned in the
Constitution, and enough in addition to cover the
interest See talks of twice over. He couldn’t at
the time say what part of the returned money was
interest on See’s bill for properties taken over, for
owing to See’s slowness in supplying the bill he
couldn’t tell what the interest would be. But
See got the interest whether it was called interest
or not, and, if See doesn’t like it, Turner reckons
See can lump it.
Extract from the Australian
Leather Journal, 15/10/’O3
Tiie Exodus from Australia.— A London
correspondent, who is in touch with leading
manufacturers in England, and therefore
in a fairly good position to “ feel the
pulse ” of the world’s progress, as it were,
writes as follows “ Australia seems to
me to be the most stagnant and least
attractive place, in a business sense, on
the civilised globe at the present time.”
This opinion would seem to be shared by
many local people. New South Wales has
lost in actual population (excess over im-
migration) over 7000 people in a little
over twelve months , Victoria, 4000 odd ;
and Queensland a considerable number
Every steamer from Australian ports takes
away people to Canada, the United States,
South Africa, Maoriland, and other coun-
tries. Many of the people are known to
have considerable capital, and, in many
cases, whole families have gone. For this
condition of things the drought is largely
responsible, but much is due to our out-
rageous labor legislation, which has deter-
mined many employers—the very class a
new country wants—to depart ‘to lands
where they will be allowed to develop their
business in peace. We deserve all this if
we allow the affairs of the country to be
conducted by men whose economic, poli-
tical, and general knowledge is below, in-
stead of above, that possessed by the ave-
rage man in the street.
The rest of the paper is mostly taken
up with chronicles of the great pro-
gress made in the businesses of sub-
scribers, lengthy descriptions of new
brick tanneries and boot factories
being erected by manufacturers, and
encouraging reports of the flourishing
condition of the leather industries.
A wild sensation was caused in
Victoria’s House of 48 Rich Landlords,
the other day, when one of them
(Dowling) so far forgot the honored
traditions of the Chamber as to pro-
pose a surplus-wealth tax. He antici-
pated that there was quite £25,000,000
worth of “propputty” in Victoria
which might be subject to such a tax ;
£2,700,000 of which would be in land
values. A flabbergasted Council at-
tempted a debate, but had to give
up after a little and adjourn to col-
lect its intellect.
Retiring Senator Mackellar was
re-appointed to the N.S.W. Legisla-
tive Council on the last day of the
session. The Council may not do
any work for the next six months and
certainly will not for three, and an
economical Government could have saved Mac.’s
railway pass by not re-appointing him till his
services were really required to save the country.
“Rriinu”: Remembering his love and
consideration during the late war for the push
with the jug-handle nose, it is not surprising that
King Seddon has informed the M.L. House of
Reps, that he favors the abolition of the totalisator,
and hopes to introduce a Bill next session to deal
with the “evil.” With its horde of evil-faced,
loud-mouthed, hungry gang of harpies, Punter’s
Point, Wellington, should even now be a warning
of the greater evil that will assuredly follow the
tote’s suppression ; but Maoriland has a large
section of “ moral agitators ” with a roar that is
loud and insistent, and a sorrowful tendency to
roar in the interests of a rotten cause.
The disgraceful Vic- :
torian Government’s latest
proposal for repudiation re-
lates to those men in the
Victorian Railway and Pub-
lic Service who are entitled
to a pension on retirement at
the age of 60 years. Irvine’s
new Bill provides that the
Ministers may, in conse-
quence of any change, made
in any department, ret\re any
officer whether he has at-
tained the age of 60 or not,
on an annual allowance of
much less than what he is
entitled to under the existing
law. The present law re-
quires that before a per-
manent officer can be dis-
pensed with he must be
shown to have been
guilty of some miscon-
duct, or the Govern-
ment must take the
responsibility of abol-
ishing the office which
he holds. Under the
new law, when a railway
official has served the
State for 40 years, and is
within arm’s length of his
pension, he may be sacked on
the excuse that there has been a re-arrange-
ment of office-boys at the Observatory, and
on that pretence be deprived of a considerable
part of his pension. Of course, this may not be
done, but when a Government deliberately asks
for power to do a shameless act of fraud the only
possible theory is that it means to use that power.
“Teddy” Smith, who represented one-
fourth of the Argus Senate brigade, invariably
wept when the hostile crowd got to work on him.
His speech, which he read, seemed to be pre-
pared, but he was capable of one remark on his
own. When assailed by groans, laughter, etc., he
always protested : “This isn’t fair. Didn’t I pay
for the hall ? ” In the midst of his deepest sorrow
he always remembered he paid for the hall.
Be the gentle importer ! Mercury, or
Hermes, was the boss robber of his age. His
ability in “going through” Neptune for his
trident, Venus for her girdle, Mars for his
sword, &c., won him the approbation of Jupiter,
who gave him the job of “Messenger of the
Gods.” Mercury, among the ancients, was the
tutelary deity of merchants. He was also the
patron saint of thieves, swindlers, and pick-
pockets. Verb sap.
South Australia didn’t show itself dead-
tired over the Commonwealth elections only. It
has just had its municipal elections, and the
result was the same in that case ; nearly every-
body who had held a seat was sent back un-
opposed. In the city a mayor and six councillors
were wanted, and the only election was over one
councillorship; in that case the rather fantastic
J. Z. Sellar, one of the oldest Labor battlers in
the State, lost his seat.
Queensland Government has done without
any fuss what a few N.S.W. retrenchers have
tried year after year to do but without success—
practically abolished the useless position of Judge’s
tipstaff. The tipstaff, who used to bear the
emblematic rod of justice, has for years past been
little more, in most cases, than Judge’s valet; and
Queensland has concluded that it can’t afford
to pay a Judge’s valet. His Honor must pay for
his own valet or do without.
A. H. Barlow, the new Queensland Education
Minister, is putting in part of the recess attend-
ing schools, not for an hour or so while the
youngsters sing “Gorsave,” but for whole days
while ordinary work is in progress. Barlow
confesses that he went to the department with
no personal ideas on the suisjteet of education as
practised in and he finds it a han-
dicap. It’s all right as long as, after a week
or so of this, Barlow doesn’t get it into his head
that he is an educational expert.
Paris Nesbit was reproved by Chief
Justice Way during the recent Criminal Sittings
in Adelaide for appearing in court without a
junior counsel to support him. The K.C. main-
tained that the practioe had not been observed of
late, but said he would be glad to go back to it if
so desired. This means more fees for young
lawyers, and increased expense for any accused
person who wants a really first-class man to
defend him. To get,the first-class man that he
wants the accused will Also have to hire the third-
class man that he doesn’t want.
It is now pretty clear that”that cool, calm,
honest politician, Iceberg Irvine of Victoria, was
wholly insincere over the Government repurchase

Irvine, at Nhill, declared that the Government
had a Bill prepared for the repurchase of these
properties, and that the Bill would be introduced
this session. He has now announced the near
approach of the close of the session, and yet the
Bill has not made its appearance in any shape or
form. Plainly the beautifully honest Irvine has
proved himself in this shatter, as. well as many
others, a bigger politjca], humbug than all the
humbugs that have gone before him.
A Victorian pressman :
As soon as old King David Syme thought he had the
Irvine party, which he helped to create, broken up, he
set about getting another crowd that would bow the
knee before his throne. He did this in an artful manner.
Two or three of his minions went round the members,
saying that the King wanted to get together a moderate
party—a party in which the Labor members on the one
side and the extreme Tories, like Frank Madden and
M'Outcheon, on the other, would be left high and dry in
Opposition. The King seems to have here missed the
trend of things political in Victoria. The division of
future political parties throughout the Commonwealth is
going to be the Labor party and the Conservative party.
The so-called Liberal party will go to pot. Its advanced
members will throw in their lot with the Labor party,
and its timid, shilly-shally members will fall back into
the ranks of Toryism. Before that day comes the Labor
party will have to get a more comprehensive name.
What is wrong with the “ National party ? ”
FROM THE WOMAN’S POINT OF VIEW.
HIM : “ Well, how’s a 'preferential tariff going to
suit you? ”
HER: “I really don't know; I've never seen any-
body wearing them.”
Not a day passes now without Melbourne
Age telling the public at some length what a hope-
less failure, weakling, political fraud and incapable
is that Irvine whom it so recently boosted to the
skies. And still nobody can clearly see the
reason, for Irvine was just as glaring a fraud
when David’s paper supported him as he is
now. David still refuses to nominate Irvine’s
successor, and the country begins to wonder
whether Syme means to do without a Premier,
and run the State business at the Age office.
Vic. Railway Commissioners, on. Dec. 9th,
notified that “starving stock” rates and the
acceptance of promissory notes for stock freights-
would absolutely cease on the last day of this
year. It must be over six months since Bent pro-
fessed to abolish these rates. It is indeed marvel-
lous to learn that, when Victoria is loaded with
such a harvest that the railway department hasn’t
the least idea how to shift the grain, the rich
squatter should still be getting concessions at the
public expense on the ground that his stock are
starving for lack of grass.
The U.S. immigration officers lately,
blocked a young Maorilander who had got a billet
in a local bank and was going to it under contract..
He probably went to the U.S. by reason of
the daily papers’ stupid lie that Australia
is the only country which has immigration-
restriction laws. The U.S. opens its gates
almost as wide as Australia does to desirable
immigrants; but it bangs them harder than ever
Australia has thought of doing in the face of the
immigrant who goes there with his hands tied by.
a contract bond.
Sydney Evening News—same old lie :
East week Mr. Deakin refused to discuss the Petriana
incident at all —in any fashion or shape whatever. He
probably thought the matter would blow over and be no
more heard of, in place of, as it is, causing an outburst of
public indignation only a little inferior to that raised by
THE EXCLUSION OF THE SIX HATTERS.
And if the other party raised a fiction of its own
to counterbalance this, and talked off-handedly
about the hanging of the Evening News’s father,
that paper would probably say the other party
was no gentleman.
About the best back view of Peacock and
Co. that Vic. Assembly has had this session hap-
pened when the Opposition left the House in a
body on Thursday night over the Electoral Boun-
daries Bill. Premier Irvine asked the Oj position
to let the Bill go through committee without
debate, promising to consider any amendments at
the “report” stage. It was an extraordinary
proceeding, but the Opposition took Irvine’s word,
and then, having thus got the Bill through Com-
mittee, he refused to accept any amendments at a
later stage. By a most audacious political dodge
he diddled theoOpposition out of its chance of
moving amendments, and it retired in silent
protest without even throwing an ink-pot at the
impudent trickster.
ELECTION TIME.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There is a somethin’ in the barmy air
What makes a stir around the corner pub.,
" here boozers—raving on an empty tub—
Denounce all wealth in which they ’ave no share.
An’ w’en ole Blowhard meets me goin’ to town,
And steers me gently to a private bar
To pour out polities an’ good “ three star,”
Instead o’ passin’ me with ’orty frown,
I try to ’ide the twinkle in me eye,
An’ listen ’umbly to ’is kind remarks
About the unemployed who fill the parks,
For I can smell elections drawin’ nigh.
E ain’t agin the honest working man
Like me (I ’aven’t touched a job this year) ;
It’s these ’ere Socialists we ’ave to fear—
The fools who try to alter Nature’s plan.
Gord made the rich to ease the toilers’ pain,
And give ’em shelter w’en their strength is spent
(I can’t exactly grasp the argyment—
Until ole Blowhard fills ’em up again).
But w’en ’e’s quite run down and said “ Good-bye,”
Before I’ve started on my devious way
I’m beckoned in by Hardcase, M.L.A.,
And then I’m sure election draweth nigh.
So, after all, the bush cove gets no pull,
An’ I, for one, am not a-goin’ to w’ine ;
For if the ’ayseed’s vote’s worth ten o’ mine,
’ls candidate can’t fill ’im more than full.
N.S.W.

Dear Sir,—l have been troubled for the past two years
with stricture, and been under skilful medical men in
South Australia and also in Victoria. I have undergone
three operations but only received temporary benefit.
As the doctors said there was no cure for me, I was
recommended, to try VITADATIO. I purchased one
bottle, which I was pleased to find acted like magic on
my complaint. I have taken five large bottles, and can
truly say up to the present time I have received great
benefit, and fully believe it will make a complete cure of
the above complaint. You are at liberty to make what
use you think of this, as VITADATIO cannot be too
highly recommended. I remain, yours truly,
EDWARD JENKINS.

A new method has taken possession of Sydney
soniety. Formally it was the mode to entertain at one s
own home. Now the thing it to do it at The Hotel
Australia.*
Old John Throssell, who was three months
Premier of Westralia, is already talking wildly
about Secession because his State hasn’t yet got
its transcontinental railway ; and a foolish bishop
named Riley has been saying something in the
same strain. The S.M. Herald chipped in with
its little contribution to the effect that old John’s
demand for Secession if his enormously expensive
line wasn’t handed down instantly from the shelf
was “an eloquent commentary on the way Mr.
Deakin and his friends have governed Australia.”
Yet, if “Mr. Deakin and his friends” had pro-
posed spending £4,000,000 —for one-third of which
N.S.W. would have been responsible—to build a
railway to Westralia, how the same fair-minded
paper would have raved !

Royalties don’t appear to be feeling well
just now. The Empress of Russia suffers from
inflammation of the ear, the Emperor of Germany
has something very wrong with one ear, likewise
hi 3 larynx ; the Queen of England is almost stone
deaf. And yet royalty protests every time an
archduchess tries to improve the blood of her
ancient family by eloping with a healthy plebeian.
In San Francisco a number of liquor shops
have been robbed in a large, violent manner of
late, and a local editor assures the public that he
doesn’t suspect the mayor of doing it :
I wish to state distinctly that I do not think it is a gang
from the City Hall that is doing all these saloon hold-ups.
There is nothing in the moral make-up of our city officers
that would prevent them going, masked and armed, into
saloons and robbing proprietors and visitors; but they
lack the nerve. They are a lot of peaceful thieves, these
office holders, and prefer to do their stealing through the
offices they hold. They haven’t time, anyway, to go
skihooting around the town at night—they are too busy
spending what they have stolen from the taxpayers
during the day.
Transvaal Leader publishes a long
article on the alleged horrible state to which
Australia has been brought by “socialistic legis-
lation,” and professes to get its facts from that
remarkable person Captain Pearse, whose cock-
and-bull yarn about the frantic rush of people
escaping from Maoriland to Argentina was
recently shown up by Agent-General Reeves. The
principal delusions which the Transvaal Leader
circulates are:
That in Australia “ seven shillings being deemed a
living wage it was enacted that no man should he paid
less /or a .day's work, WHATEVER THE NATURE OP
HIS EMPLOYMENT."
That in Australia all shops are compulsorily closed at
5 p.m.
That thousands of small shopkeepers were consequently
ruined.
That the accumulated deficit on the N.S.W. State
railways is £17,000,000 (it is really about £8,000,000).
That the N.S.W. State railways have not paid interest
“for several years.” (This is quite true, but it is also
true that they never paid interest. The “ several years ”
is an attempt to represent that the deficit is a new thing
arising out of Labor legislation.)
What liars these Tories are !

HOME CYCLOPEDIA

THE POPULAR “PLAIN HOME TALK."
By Dr. E. B. FOOTE.
18 Plates. 36 Ohromos. 400 Illustrations. 1248 Pages.
IT IS FREE FROM TECHNICAL TERMS, and shows up Human
Nature as it exists. Here are answered, in plain language, a thou-
sand questions that occur to the minds of young and old, but about
which they feel a delicacy in consulting their physician. Chapters
are devoted to innumerable interesting subjects concerning mar-
riage, which makes it a superior guide to the action of men and
women both in and out of marriage. No man or woman who
anticipates a bright future, and wishes to enjoy married life in its

A Woman’s Power.
The smiles and favor of a beautiful woman are
heaven enough for some men. But beauty and
grace in woman are founded on good health. Good
health depends on good digestion, and that in-
variably follows the use of Mother Seigel’s Syrup,
a tonic made of fruits, roots and herbs. Beauty
and strength spring up beside it, as flowers on a
sunny slope. It brightens the eyes, clears the
skin, and gives vigor, vivacity, and health to the
languid and weak. All women need it occasionally;;
tens of thousands use it daily. “I was'greatly
troubled,” wrote Mrs. Brankier, of 48, Pacific-
street, Newcastle, N.S.W., on February 28th,
1903, “by chronic indigestion. Several doctors,
whom I consulted at different times, quite failed
to do me any good. But about twelvemonths ago
I heard of a wonderful cure of indigestion in this
city, effected by Mother Seigel’s Syrup, and
resolved to try it myself. After taking the Syrup
for a few days I felt like a different woman, less
than four bottles being sufficient to. effect my
complete cure, eradicating all traces of indigestion
from my system. I can strongly recommend
Seigel’s Syrup to all persons afflicted with indiges-
tion and stomach derangement, being convinced
by my own experience that they will find it a
speedy and reliable cure.”*

SPORTING NOTIONS.
First England-Australia Test match, progress-
ing as Tub Bulletin goes to press, commenced at Sydney
Cricket Ground on Friday. Australia won the toss, the
first occasion for eight years on Sydney Ground. Trumper
and Duff opened. Both batsmen weathered the first
oyer, Hirst’s; but Arnold’s initial delivery brought
disaster. Trumper essa3'ed to cut and edged
it into slips where Foster had a remarkable, very
low, arm’s length catch, which carried him down
on his. bent-up elbow. Hill went in. First ball of
Arnold’s second over provided a further sensation:
Duff attempted to cut a quick high-riser from the pitch
but only snicked it, and Lilley was right behind the
wicket. Then Noble went in to try and stem the rot, bub
the gloom deepened when a few minutes later 1 Clem Hill
Was trapped by Lilley trying to cut one from Hirst.
Three of Australia’s best bats had lost their, wickets
trying the cut and only 12 on the scoring board. The
great Hill was out for five. It was awful. Armstrong
joined Noble and the Australian audience leaned on its
neighbor’s shoulder and swore softly under its breath.
Even the golden-headed girls in the Ladies’ stand used
dainty little swears and looked as though they wanted to
cry. Noble and Armstrong settled down to “ play the
game.” It was painfully slow, but it was better than
the sound of falling wickets.
T-he fielding was brilliant. Two hours saw
score lifted to 83. Armstrong disappeared with 48 oppo-
site his name through an off-break from Bosanquet. He
had four 4’s in his little lot, and with Noble had brought
the score from 12 to 118. Just under 3J hours 200 runs
and Noble’s century arrived simultaneously. Then
Hirst’s full toss curled off Hopkins’ bat and he was gone for
39, after an hour and a quarter. Clouds and wind
threatened rain, and Howell went in to hit up runs, but
was caught at five and made wa3' for Gregory. Just
before stumps-draw, Bosanquet, freshly on, scattered
Gregory’s sticks with a charming off-break at 23. Score
stood at seven for 259.
Spite of heavy rain overnight, Saturday’s wicket
was little affected. Nearly 40,000 people were crammed
into the enclosures, and the last three Australian wickets
fell for 26. Laver (1.b.w.) gathered three ; Noble added
one to'his previous day’s 132, and then hooked Arnold to
short-leg, Foster held it with one hand. Noble
had made 16 fours, and stayed at the wickets on Friday
4h. 41min. Saunders was last man in, and had eclipsed
his previous best and was promising well when Kelly
succumbed at 10. Saunders had made 11. The innings
was over for 285.
It was ten minutes to 1 when Warner and
Hayward opened for England. The English captain
snicked one off Laver’s first over, and wicketkeeper Kelly
held it grimly. 1—0 —0. Tyldesles'. joined Hayward,
and in 25 minutes had made 30. Then Howell bagged
Hayward with an off-break' that fetched the middle
stump. 2 15—49. Bowling was erratic, and Tyldesley
slashed it brilliantly. After lunch run-getting slackened,
and only 15 came in 20 minutes. At 53 Tyldesley
went down to Noble. The play was excellent. The
hundred was reached in as many minutes. Then a
spell of monotonous defensive play was broken by
Laver catching Arnold at short-leg off Armstrong.
Two hours 35 minutes of play resulted in 150 only. Two
hundred showed in 3hr. lOmin! Half-an-hour before
stumps draw the crowd began to leave, thoroughly tired
of it. Finish saw Foster 73 and Braund 67, still going
strong, and the score 243 for four men out. Last pair
had added 126.
* * * *•
The Bulletin goes to press earlier than usual
this week, and it takes its last look at the game at a late
hour on Monday afternoon, when the Englishmen’3 score
stood at nine for 486—Foster not out 225, Rhodes not out
15. Foster’s score is a record-breaker. The highest
previous score in a test match was made by W. L. Mur-
doch in England, when that brawny Australian knocked
up 211. The record in Australia was Sid. Gregory’s 201 ;
while the highest made for England was Ranjitsinjhi’s
175. On Saturday Foster played very disappointingly
from a spectator’s standpoint. He is still playing dis-
appointingly from an Australian standpoint.
* * * #
‘ ‘ Gate ” on first two days of test match reached
£2495—£1750 being taken on Saturday. Saturdays of
test matches engaged in by Maclaren’s team two years
ago were better—Saturday of first test then saw the total
takings £1934. N.S.W. Cricket Association will this
time have a deep dig into the spoils, which spells con-
siderable benefit to Cornstalk cricket generally.
* * * *
“ 0.K.” : For years people have been complain-
ing that cricket, as played by experts under gate-money
conditions, often becomes needlessly tedious to all on-
lookers save the few whose appetite for “ the game ” is
insatiable. The cry was for livelier cricket—until they
got it. Warner’s introduction of the English closure rule
is now objected to, on the ground that “ it was never
intended to apply to matches of longer duration than
three days,” which is tantamount to saying that a captain
who thinks he can safely shorten the game by declaring
an inhings closed is not entitled to hasten his victory'.
With just as much reason one might protest against any
matchLterm mating before both sides have, lost twenty
wickets. If it is an objectionable thing to close a first
innings when only seven or eight wickets' are down,
why close a second innings merely because the game is
won ? Why not pound the ball.to the bitter end, and see
how many runs it is possible to win by? At Melbourne,
the other day, the Englishmen won by what was prac-
tically 12 wickets (nominally one innings) with numerous
runs to spare. Australia had never previously seen a
cricket match concluded with so little waste of time in
unnecessary batting—nevertheless the local people
grumbled. It hurt their amour propre to find the local
champions held cheaply by the visiting captain, though
the result proved that he was quite justified in venturing
to save time.
* * » *
“Hugh Stone”: In an English magazine a
writer, discussing “village cricket,” asks: “ How is it
that the village umpire always takes a bat with him to
his post ? ” But this peculiarity is not confined to English
villages. In every match I have seen in the bush the
umpire dragged a bat about after him. Down in Riverina
my fitness to stand was once questioned because I
declined to load myself with timber on a hot day. It
was the man at the other end who seemed most hurt.
He declared he had umpired for thirty years, and had
never seen an umpire officiating without a bat. I had
evidently lowered the tone of the position.
' * * * *
How the old players keep their places in
Australian tests. Seven of the present team played
against Maclaren’s crowd three years ago, and all but
Laver were in the last Australian team which visited
England. Every member of the team has taken part in
at least one test match. Sid Gregory has most innings
(58 for 1458 runs) to his credit while Clem Hill with 1581
runs for 37 innings has a long way the highest average.
No other present day player has accumulated 1000 runs
in test matches.
* * » •
In Adelaide electorate cricket Harry Blinman
made 113 last week against the bowling of George Giffen
and others. Blinman has been playing cricket as long as
the other veteran, and was in Lord Sheffield’s year only
just left out of the test matches. Before the rise of
Darling, Jones, and Hill, “two Giffens and Blinman,
Lyons and Jarvis ” was for many years the start of every
S. A. team.
* * * *
There were only 12 starters in Queensland Cup,
and but few gave St. Quentin a chance, but he got there
after a tough struggle down the straight with stable-
chum Heronry. Fitzgrafton, Jessie (last year’s winner)
and Heronry disputed favoritism at 4’s. Fitzgrafton’s
easy win of the Derby a few days previously made his
chance look unusually promising, but he only finished
eighth in the slowest Cup run for five years. Fitz.’s
bubble reputation is now quite burst. Three-year-olds
have won the race eight times and four Derby winners
(Wheatmeat, Petronel, Fernando and Balfour) have
notched the double.
* * * *
St. QiOntin is a 4-year-old by St. Blaize from
Question, of the Dyraaba stud, Richmond River (N.S.W.).
He has won thrice present season.
* * * •
A particularly angry rash appeared on the
fair face of Sydney ponydom the last two weeks. Lately-
formed Pony and Galloway Association deputationised
Kensington R/O. (principal pony meeting promoters) con-
cerning grievances. and wanted a board of appeal to deal
with disqualifications, and, considering the K.R.O.’s
attitude unsatisfactory, declared war. Forest Lodge and
Brighton courses were placed at the strikers’ service, and
it was further announced that Belmore course would
be reopened.. The new body determined to race same
day as Kensington and maintain a general bo3'cott.
Things looked blue for Kensington and Rosebery Park
and threats of disqualification were passed freely. Entries
for following Kensington meeting were particularly poor,
while the P. andG. Association totalled 104. A conference
was arranged, and strikers scored heavily. Kensington
R.O. ceased to be the autocrat of ponydom. Said now
that many, members of the victorious body are not
satisfied with concessions wrung and that there is a
chance of the new body’s swelled head ending in a burst.
* * * •
This was the amazing method of procedure of
Marlborough (M.L.) Racing Club in regard to a case of
“ inconsistency.” Hinekoa (lOst. 51b.) won Blenheim
Handicap, 1J miles, beating Reikura (7st. 51b.), and others.
Later in day in six furlong Flying Handicap only two
started, Reiktfra (7st. 71b.) getting a big break on and
defeating Hinekoa (lOst. 71b.). The stewards weren’t
satisfied, but contented themselves with disqualifying
the jock for rest of meeting. Next day Hinekoa, carry-
ing the thumping weight of list. 81b., won the mile and
a distance - handicap, . Reikura didn’t start. Then
stewards held another inquiry to consider first day’s
running, but postponed decision until Hinekoa (lOst. 91b.)
met Reikura (7st. 61b.). over a shorter course later on.
Only the pair started, and Hinekoa won. Then the
stewards took both races from her, ordered tote invest-
ments on her to be returned less 10 per cent., and passed
owner and nag into oblivion for 12 months.
* * * *
The very latest about the ridiculous Cplling-
wood tote fiasco is that the man who ran the alleged
wood and coal yard—Avhich was part of the tote yard,
and boasted an ornamental stack of firewood which was
never touched—has written the Iceberg Government
(through Detective O’Donnell) for £5OO damages for
trespass. That weird joke of Irvine and Co. is likely
to cost Victoria something handsome before it is finally
buried.
♦ - * • •
The best recent excuse for a horse’s in-and-out
running.comes from Bendigo. Hauled up after he had
romped in on the second day and asked to account for
his poor show the first day, the Earl of Carrick’s jockey
explained that on the morning of the first day the horse
had gorged himself on his bedding. Excuse accepted.
* * * *
Post mortem on body of Tas. nag Inchman, who
dropped dead in his box, the other day, revealed the
heart in such a badly diseased condition that ’twas con-
sidered remarkable he raced so long.
• * * *
Fecundity of Rosalind, dam of latest Canter-
bury (M. L.) Derby winner Roseal, has been remarkable.
Twenty-three, years ago she threw White Rose, and has
had 18 foals'since, but never figured in a race.
* * * *
“ Euroa’’tells the story of a cabman’s curse:
We got into his cab in Bourke-street, and when he put us
down near the Hill gates, we gave him ss. “What’s
this? ” he asked. We said it was his fare. “ Fare 1 ” he
shouted." “ Drive three of yer for five bob on Cup day ?
I want hajf a quid ! As we moved away we remarked
that we didn’t wish to buy his turn-out. “ Buy it 1 ” he
yelled after us, and his horse had a scornful look on its
face, “ I don’t suppose yer have half-a-quid among the
three of yer ; yer couldn’t buy a Frankfort. Ver blanky
bounders—may yer never have a bit of luck all day.” We
were superstitious and felt that more than anything else.
And how his curse, stuck to us ! Wakeful was beaten,
and all our other “ certs.” went under. In the Steeple,
our horse had a splendid chance of winning, but the curse
brought him down. It seemed to have spent itself by
the last race, though, and the horse we had “ emptied
out” on, romped home lengths ahead' of anything else.
We shook hands in silence. But when we went to collect,
the bookie had fled ! That broke us up completely. We
didn’t have our fare home, and it was a long walk to
town. - We tried “ lugging,” but everyone we asked had
either “ blown out ” or had his bare fare onty. We were
miser able, ami how we swore as we tramped across the
cour-se I We hung about the pubs, near the course for
awhile, in hopes of striking a friend, but it was not to
be; and we continued on our walk to town. After passing
the New’market station we came upon a crowd around a
breakdown’ in the middle of the road. The horse was
down, both shafts were broken, a wheel was gone, a
window smashed, and some of the crowd were cutting
away the harness. A man was kneeling on the horse’s
head, shouting out orders in frightful language. It was
the cabby who had driven us out 1 Somehow, after that,
the walk seemed nothing, and we reached town in good
spirits.
* * * •
The falling off of it. Ex-M.L. champion nag
Advance figured in a . circus ring at Palmerston, t’other
day, as a medium for some of the company to display their
buck-jumping capabilities. That fine animal very ungal-
lantly bucked Miss Hyland and her saddle off first, but
the young lady’s brother beat the nag badly, goading
him to maddened fury, and giving the spectators a buck-
jumping display such as has rarely been witnessed. The
cruelty and the “.come down” (Advance was a great
horse) of the thing disgusts—it is on a par with allowing
nags that have won fortunes for their owners to finish up
drawing cabs or vegetable vendors’ carts. There’s little
sentiment in the make of most present day racehorse
owners.
* , » * •
Conclusion of Austral meet, on Melbourne
Cricket Ground, Saturday, attracted 20,000 spectators.
Final of the big thing saw S.A. champion F. H. Scheps
(160yds.) gather the winner’s portion (£450) by 30yds.,
after a brilliant sprint over the last half lap, during the
early part of which he shot out 20yds. clear as if pro-
pelled by a father-in-law’s boot. Hawker was second, and
Mayer (probably 3Qyds., further off) third. Atop of this,
and same day, Scheps annexed the Victorian Mile
by half a bike-length, and in a hair-raising finish
with Don Walker. Scheps isn’t 21 yet, and, although
riding for some time, is only in his second active season—
his initial success .was a Maiden Plate at Adelaide less
than two years ago, and then followed Eight. Hour Wheel
Race at Broken Hill. Sydney November meet last year
saw him scoop Sydney Wheel Rade (£100), and a half-
mile handicap—this after being disqualified for careless
riding in his heat of the big struggle, and subsequently
having a fine of £5 substituted for the embargo. Con-
vincing evidence that Scheps has improved remarkably
was his great battle' with Walker over the finishing 50yds.
of Saturday’s mile struggle.
* * . * *
‘ ‘ Mac. ” :_The ’Murkans did not reap the pro-
phesied success at Saturday’s Austral. Downing, in the
first heat of the day, lost his pedal and was counted out.
M’Farland rart third in his heat, but was badly left in the
semi-final. He annexed the International Scratch. The
big Yank, in a dressing-gown of some tea-cosy material,
looked like a stage ghost caught in the daylight as he
paddled about the arena, on show, between the heats.
Gave one the impression of some languid Goliath
being persuaded out of his tiredness to display his
prowess. Was persuaded to start in the 10 mile,
and in the last 50 yards his wheel skidded. Turned
a complete somersault, landed on his feet and
bowed to the crowd. Contrast to Lawson (who would
make a fine gladiator or tailor’s dummy). He was
putting in his run near the scoring board with a soft field
of five, and touched the winner’s machine. He turned
two or three catherine-wheels and brought the rest
down ; pointed dramatically to the winner putting in a
tame finish on his own, then pelted his bike pettishly at
his trainer, and tearing his numoer off, scratched himself
for the Austral. Reported he said the grass track did not
suit him, which was a pretty liberal condensation of his
views on the matter.
* * * *
The troubles of N.S.W. League of Wheelmen
having fizzled out, the “ Council ” has decided to revise
the “ Constitution ” before the general election comes off.
The constitution badly wants amending. The rules and
regulations dealing with disqualification and the inflic-
tion of penalties require careful overhauling; also,. the
reduction of the bloated Council of 25 members is as
necessary as the other reduction of members just now
making so much printing work in the daily press.
* » * *
Struggle in Melbourne last week between Syd-
ney and Victorian “fedders,” Billy Sullivan and Micko
Walsh, was sensational. Walsh forced the fighting and
punished hard, but, losing his head, he had to put up
with a deal of thumping himself. Second round was
another determined tussle, till a left swing from Sullivan
floored his vis-a-vis, whereupon referee Nerricker
declared against Sullivan—alleging he had fouled his
man. Crowd angrily and derisively hooted the decision,
and a riot seemed imminent, while beaten pug made as if
to strike the referee. Nerricker appealed to be heard in
explanation, but the house boo-hooed him lustily, and
tried to rush the stage, jeering vigorously at Walsh the
while. Then police essayed to clear the hall, without
success, till the gas was turned out. Remarkable the
number of “arguable” things Walsh has been mixed up
in. His fight with Thorn, in Westralia, was odorous ;
then followed his fouling of Silver, and one or two other
little affairs.
» * * •
Hook Keys (Australia’s most promising light-
weight) qualified for a meeting with champion Bob
Turner by decisively beating Lyn Truscott at Sydney
Gaiety A.C. last week in lhr. 20min. House was a mass
of excited men from floor to rafters. Keys’ clever work
and far better general knowledge of boxing stood him in
food stead, and though not as well as he might have
een, pulled him through. Truscott stood fearful
punishment, and, always aggressive, showed a bold front
throughout. His superior poundage always gave him a
chance to the end; in the 11th round he looked a sure
winner, but Keys had too much skill and knowledge of
ring dodges generally.
* • # *
Fight at Sydney National S.O. recently’tween
Blackmore and Jack Willis was short and far from sweet.
Big crowd that packed the hall expected much, but got
decidedly little. Management was quite justified in
bringing the pair together—possibilities looked pro-
mising. A big amount of money was wagered at 5 and 6
to 4on Blackmore. From the first Willis was never in it,
and didn’t seem to want to be, letting a succession of
chances go unavailed of till a left on the jaw, quickly
followed by a right on the other side and a few other
close attentions dropped him for seven seconds. Rising
badly rattled, Willis proved easy prey. A left hook sent
him boardward3 again, and another effectively com-
pleted the contract—all in one round. Willis never had
a hope, but Blackmore is no marvel—so far he has picked
his own marks.
* * * *
Strongly-built and muscled Newcastle pug
Whitelaw met slimly-built, lank-limbed Ted Nelson at
National S.O. Sydney, a few evenings ago. Betting men
laid odds on the coal-city bruiser, and, in the third
round, offered 2to 1. Whitelaw hustled and roughed his
man fiercely, and seemed to have him going often, but
Nelson stood up, jabbing a clever left home, and other-
wise keeping “in it.” Though the other man’s force did
play havoc with him at times, Nelson made the enemy’s
face a picture to be remembered. Whitelaw’s wealth of
muscle hampered freedom of limb considerably, and
precluded hard hitting and quickness of action. The
tenth round saw Whitelaw stagger Nelson, and go in to
finish him, when a flukily-lanaed short-arm left jolt
under the chin spread-eagled the Newcastle man beyond
the allotted 10 seconds, and just when the victory seemed
his.
* •* * *
Melbourne’s Jack M‘Gowan fought “Jim”
Hiscutt in Broken Hill theatre last week. House was
crowded, and battle proved interesting all the way, but
never excessively exciting, and was awarded to Mac. in
the 20th round.
* * * *
The fight between Tim Hegarty and Bob Rollo,
at Kalgoorlie, recently, didn’t draw enough to provide
the purse fought for. A tame affair ended in Hegarty
being declared winner, and those who had attended
filed out and made remarks. Later on, Rollo wrote
the Kalgoorlie Sun that Hegarty had refused to fight
unless he (Rollo) agreed to be beaten, and “ force of cir-
cumstances ” compelled him to accede.
* * • •
Kalgoorlie saw another sight when Cullen
met Jessop. Gate is said to have totalled nearly £5OO.
Weights were declared within half a pound of each other,
but if they were correct appearances lie dreadfully. Still
both scaled under lOst. Cullen attacked, and Jessop hit
back straight and coolly till he reckoned the “ time had
arrived” for him to take the aggressive, and he made
things so sultry that the betting came round to his side.
Thence till the eleventh round the house was in a frenzy
of delight and excitement; then Cullen’s left arm dis-
located, and, though he tried to keep going, his seconds
very properly gave in, and ended the best fight seen in
Kalgoorlie. Jessop and Turner are now being matched
for the Australian lightweight championship.
* • • *
Nob likely that heavyweight champion pug of
the continent, Billy M’Coll, will fight again for a year or
more, if ever. Rontgenrays have shown the shoulder,
injured in Scanlan fight, to be much worse than thought;
Medico consulted said ligaments round the joint were
severely ruptured, and ’twill be long before limb regains
normal power.
• • * »
“E. Durst”; Island boy Wickham tries to
break the 100yds. swimming record. About sft. sin. in
height, pleasant-faced, wedged-shouldered, barrel-chested,
abnormally large thighs, tapering lower legs, and plate
feet—an ideal speed swimmer. Two handicap men have
jumped off, to make it a race, and meet Swimming Asso-
ciation’s rules. Wickham stands like a runner ready to
start, all eagerness for the pistol shot; hangs a bit, then,
torpedo-like, hits the water. No stroke —just a semblance
of a mad crawl. Four limbs are striking the water indis ■
criminately, turning it into foam, and the brown body
makes lightning progress. Contrary to all ordinary rules
of speed-swimming, he rolls his body, outstretches his
fingers as he makes wild grabs at the water, fairly pulling
himself along, his head well above all the trouble, looking
for the turn. He makes it clumsily, and batters away again
A few yards off the second turn the vim goes out of his
efforts, although the feet of the limit men are splashing
on a level with his head. The strain he has put on him-
self is more pronounced as he struggles round the turn
and tiredly pushes off into the last 33yds. In the next
ten yards he gains nothing on the pace-makers. His
head hangs, his body rolls from side to side, his arms fall
limply into the water, and the splash from his kicking
feet is less. The loaf puts new life into him, he bolts
again, passes his men, and bores over in front of them.
The crowd howls for him to “ shake it up,” but he hears
nothing. A few more grabs and he hits the bar. “ How
I get on ?” he gasps. “No good, Alick.” “Me think so,
water too heavy.” And “ Dick” Cavill’s 583-ssecs. record
still stands.
* * * *
Three hundred yards swimming supremacy of
N.S.W. contested at Bronte (Sydney) t’other day was
Dick Cavill’s from go—he stayed and toyed with the
opposition till completing 200yds. and then went away as
he liked winning by a dozen yards in comparatively slow
time of 3.49. Dick owns the world’s best (3.34 3-5) for the
distance, but will probably not get near that again as he
doesn’t look as well as he did last year, though there is no
one in Australasia who can beat him at any distance bar,
perhaps, the hundred. Tkeie island toy Wickham might
be found troublesome.

Obit. Old-time M.L. jockey Harry Fuller, who
won Christchurch Dei by on Trump Card 26 years ago.
Unfortunate fellow suicided at Sefton.
Bowling match N.S.W.-Victoria, ended as
most people who follow that exciting and exacting
pastime anticipated—in favor of the latter. The Victorian
team was the cream of the strongest crowd of bowlers on
the continent, while that of N.S.W. was, although the
best available, generally' mediocre and unrepresentative.
First game saw N.S.W. go under—l2o to 59; but the
second, against exactly the same 16, saw N.S. W. finish
106 to 86. Then some wiseacres insisted upon a different
distribution of strength in the Cornstalk team, and
N.S.W. was beaten by 14, notching the same number of
points as did Victoria when it was beaten.

“ Hiphen ” : Mick Healey, the well-known
Irish penciller, had just been struck for a “ monkey ” at
Caulfield on Guineas day, and was evidently feeling not
in the best of humors, when a gaunt, be-whiskered
cockey approached him, and, without a word, held out a
ticket for payment. Mick looked at the ticket for a
moment. “ What the ,” he said briefly—” that’s not
my ticket 1 ” The be-whiskered punter from Kyabram
stroked his beard silently and eyed Mick solemnly for a
full moment. “ Oh, well,” he said as he turned away,
“ it’s one o’ you Jews l’’ Mick didn’t speak. The case
was beyond language.
* * * *
The death of Jack Grossland, a once famous
Lancashire express bowler who, somehow, failed to
trouble the Australians of his time, has revived a story
re the honesty or otherwise of his violent trundling.
On one occasion, when Crossland was no-balled by the
umpire for the alleged offence of lifting his elbow, he
angrily offered to demonstrate the difference between
right and wrong in professional practice. Firstly he
bowled the ball in his usual style, and it travelled a great
distance on the hop. “That’s bowling.” said Crossland.
Then he took the ball again, and hurled it fair over the
railings of the cricket ground. “That’s throwing, that
is,” he said. As a “chucker” pure and simple Crossland
had no rival. -
John Norton’s recent escapade in the
north savors strongly of an advertising joke.
Just as the boarders had sat down to breakfast in
an hotel at Mackay (Q. ), John stepped into the
dining room and commenced blazing at the
atmosphere with a revolver. Having scared
everybody away from the fried goat or whatever
it was, he retired and waited for the Mackay
bobby to do his duty. Then it transpired that
persons discharging firearms in Mackay could not
be prosecuted, as there was no municipal by-law
dealing with the subject. John seems to have
been a municipal reformer drawing attention with
a gun to the need fpr improved local government.
■Then he publicly fined himself £2O as a donation
to the local hospital, and the piebald meeting
said “hooray.”
“ Adelaide ” : Crowding into the Supreme
Court recently to see the High Court welcomed
to S.A. by the profession, I got my (I regret to
own) first sight of Barton. His mouth fascinated
me all the time Griffith was talking an address
to the Bar ; it is so exactly and unexpectedly the
non-human mouth which the caricaturists give
him. He looked straight in front of. him, and
appeared, in general, to be dead to this world—
except when he gave one absolutely wicked glance
at the photographers who were clicking and
snapping close by. But when his turn came to
speak he came to life rapidly, and spoke with
much dignity and some eloquence. By far the
finest speaker of the occasion, however, was
Attorney - General Gordon. The absence qi
Kingston was much commented on.
A very curious election item from Sydney
Telegraph
Mr. Chanter, in his Riverina canvass, is publishing
broadcast a placard challenging anyone to prove that he
voted against the suspension of the fodder duties. Mr.
Blackwood has replied to it several times, and Mr. Reid
did so most effectively last night at his Hay meeting.
The only vote taken in regard to the repeal or the sus-
pension of these duties was taken in committee while
Mr. Chanter was in the chair. It is difficult, therefore,
to see how he could have voted one way or the other.
Mr. Chanter should have delayed the publication of this
placard until nearer polling-day. He has given his oppo-
nent too much time to circumvent him.
Thus, according to the Telegraph, Chanter told
the solemn truth when he said that he didn’t vote
against the suspension of the fodder duties; there
was only one division, and Chanter, being in the
chair, couldn’t vote at all. Consequently, if
Blackwood and Reid replied “effectively” on
this point they must have departed widely from
the truth. But the Telegraph intimated quite
cheerfully that Chanter should have kept the
truth back till later, and that by telling the truth
early he gave his Freetrade opponents too much
time to “circumvent” him. Its own party’s habit
of circumventing the truth appears to strike the
D.T. rather favorably than otherwise.

Both fatman and laborman may have cause for
disappointment over the result of the Federal elections.
But every Australian may be perfectly sure there is
nothin!: disappointing in a parcel of Xmas “fixings”
from Swallow and Ariell, the famous Melbourne firm.
Generaliv known as buscuib manufacturers, S.A. are
benefactors in general. The charm of their mincemeat
is exceeded only by the witchery of their Xmas pudding,
which have practically abolished the old Hinglish fashion
of home-made pudding. Their jams and jellies are
triumphs of mind over matter. The lightness and bright-
ness of their cakes are imparted to the consumer every
time. First you Swallow and then you Ariel, in a
Shakespearian way of speaking.*
The Goldsmiths and Silversmiths Alliance, of 90
King-street, Sydney, is making one of the prettiest
displays in the city this Christmas. The well appointed
showrooms are full of beautiful novelties in the jewellery
line, and their windows, which have only recently been
refilled throughout, show to what advantage good
jewellery can be displayed. The Alliance has very
exquisite designs in rings, brooches, bangles, and pen-
dants. Its enamelled goods, which are all the present
fashion in London, are really masterpieces of artistic
workmanship. Another pretty novely is the peacock’s
feather jewellery, which has become quite a craze
amongst American ladies, whose great object in wearing
it is to upset the old fashioned superstition about this
article of adornment being unlucky. The G. and S.
Alliance is presenting to its patrons a very useful and
highly ornamental souvenir in the form of a handsome
Silver Plated Memo Tablet and Pencil, which speaks well
for the enterprise of this up-to-date firm.*
Messrs Craig, and Aitken, the celebrated Sydney
Tobacconists and Hairdressers, have moved to new
premises, 654 George-street, where business will be
carried on as before with army and navy tobacco, corn
cob tobacco (light and dark), cherry blossom (light and
dark) and other brands. So those who want a good smoke
now know where to get one. Oraig and Aitken are
agents for the plum razor, which brand shaves most of the
men in Australia to-day ; and they are sole owners of
Oapillarine, the wonderful moustache producer. They
stock all barber’s and hairdresser’s (requisites, their wig-
making is more of a speciality than ever, and any lady
who requires transformations or anything in the way of
ornamental hair will do best by sending to Craig and
Ai tken. A lady’s hairdressing room with lady attendants
is one of the most successful departments of the business.
The gentlemen’s hairdressing saloon—but every gentle-
man knows that.*

The wife of one of the members of the Public Service
Board goes round among friends and says, “ Nobody can
get into the public service until my husband has con-
sulted me.”
After all, in spite of its wonderful lying
capacity, its astounding impudence as a faker of
figures, and its unlimited use of the “suppress,
Sydney Telegraph is not a good fighter. It has
no idea of the artistic restraint that is necessary
for successful misrepresentation. All its candi-
dates were making “splendid fights”; their
Kilarity and the respect in which they were
increased so rapidly that the supply was all
used up before polling day ; noisy bounders were
represented as having won -such golden opinions
that thousands of Protectionists “would not
vote.” And when all the universe had decided to
vote for Freetrade on Friday, what was left to be
said on Saturday ?
Of the seven 8. Australian Rep. seats only
two were fought; Glynn,
Poynton, and Holder got there unopposed. S.
Australia has had female franchise for some time,
and this probably accounts for it; for the last two
or three elections made it clear that, not knowing
much about politics, the women go strongly for
men who have made a name, and the. unknown
newcomer has little or no hope. His politics
don’t matter much. And the fact that b. A 1 }?"
tralia has practically no provincial press, while
its city papers aren’t anything of party fighters,
no doubt helps.
One of the features of the recent election
was the aspect of moral shock which the Sydney
Freetrade papers put on because Bill Lyne
applied to his supporters tO_ raise £lOOO for the
Protectionist campaign. Every party applies to
its supporters for funds, but the Telegraph spoke
of the event with bated breath, as if it were quite
a new iniquity. And if one remarks in a
sufficiently horrified tone that Bill Lyne had an
egg for breakfast, he may gradually create an im-
pression in some minds that no man has an egg
for breakfast unless he is a villain of the deepest
dye.

Extract from Goulburn (N.S.W.) paper:
We owe it to Mr. Deakin’s Government that Australia
has a law which makes it penal to save shipwrecked
foreigners and land them in the Commonwealth. The
other day the steamer Petriana went ashore on the
Victorian' coast. The helpless vessel was within a few
chains of the shore, and the crew might have been
landed in safety without loss of time. But it was
composed of Chinese and Malays, and the people who
rescued and landed them would render themselves
liable to heavy fines. The Federal Government graciously
permits shipwrecked foreigners to drown in Com on-
wealth waters, hut they may not he landed oh the
Australian shore.
As an utterly unscrupulous lie this about takes
the medal. Nobody was drowned, or even in
danger of drowning ; the whole of the foreigners
were taken off, and cared for, and sent back to
their native country. It is a pity the libel law
isn’t wide enough to enable the Deakin Govern-
ment to burst up such unscrupulous rags as this
with heavy damages. Australia will soon be
known, not only as the country with the biggest
debt per head on earth, but also a 3 the one above
all others where the daily press has most com-
pletely lost the alleged English virtues of truth,
fairness, and honesty.

The color question’s strong, we know,
And just now it applies;
In “ HAVELOCK” ’tis a question which
The smoker always buys.

Practical Value of the Book. This book will prove of great valueand practical assistance to Parents
—enabling them to impart to their children a perfect breathing method which will tend to prevent Pulmonary
Diseases, Post-Nasal (adenoid) Growths, and other defects tending to cause nasal stenosis. Dr. SEARES
SPICER, Surgeon, Throat Department, St. Mary’s Hospital, London, when advocating the study and practice of
hreathing as a preventative of adenoid growths, &c., said: “As a matter of fact, the manner of breathing of
every child, just as much as its food and clothing, housing, and air, exercise, bathing and education, required
constant and unremitting attention from the moment of birth.” A practical and valuable book for VOCAL
STUDENTS, TEACHERS, LAYMEN and EXPERTS, who desire to secure the marvellous benefits to be derived
from a perfect breathing method in all VOCAL EFFORT or in the TREATMENT OF THE DISEASES already
enumerated. Special Sets of Breathing; Exercises. Special Sets of Vocal and
Breathing- Exercises. Special Sets of Physical Exercises. These exercises are most
minutely described, and the ILLUSTRATIONS in connection with them will be taken from photographs of the
author in the act of the demonstration of his methods. The author feels justified in stating that the breathing
exercises are the most PRACTICAL and COMPLETE that have, so far, been published. NOTE. —A Pamphlet,
containing particulars of the contents of the book, will be forwarded to any address on receipt of stamp.

copies will be published to supply the orders received. The book will be delivered as early as possible in the
coming year, 1904. Address order to Mr. F. MATTHIAS ALEXANDER, Royal Chambers, corner of Hunter and
Oastlereagh Streets, Sydney, where his methods are imparted. Interviews should be arranged by letter.

Nothing has happened yet in the way of
decorations for members of the Australian Parlia-
ment, but some are met with who expect that,
at an early date, the rain of ribbons and titles will
commence.
One of the closing scenes in N.S.W.
Parliament:—
Mr. Ashton : Did you notice what your Auditor-General
said on the question ? (Hear, hear.)
The Minister for Mines : 1 don’t care what a dozen
Auditors-General say.
The Auditor-General is supposed to be the final
authority on N.S.W. finance, therefore his office
should be held in respect, and Parliamentary
small boys shouldn’t run after him publicly with
clods. The present larrikin Government appointed
the present Auditor-General, and if it doesn’t
care what he says it is either the wrong kind of
Government or it has appointed the wrong kind
of Auditor-General.
Nowadays hardly anything is ever finished
in N.S.W. Assembly. If a member has a really
important suggestion to make he waits many
weeks or months for a chance to bring it forward,
and then its fate is briefly reported : ‘ ‘ The time
for private business having expired the motion
lapsed.” There is no means by which the House
can be forced to give an opinion on any matter,
nor is there any finality or satisfaction anywhere.
The proposal of Meagher, M.L.A., to establish a
sinking fund for the repayment of the public debt
was a matter of some importance, but it fizzled
out like the rest. Some method by which a mem-
ber could stop the stream of eloquence and demand
a vote on his proposal would do a lot of good.
The House would then have to make up its mind.
As a result it might gradually begin to have a
mind.

“ War E.” on bores :
Will some legislator introduce a Bill for the repression
of bores. Sunday afternoon is the only time I have to
mvself and it is invariably ruined by a bore who dis-
cuss-s the capabilities of British generals, the advantages
of superphosphate in wet seasons, the probabilities of
a Russian invasion, and whether Dill Moran or Pat Macky
is the more interesting preacher to stay away from.
I’m prepared to swear an information before a J.r. that
this man is a bore who should be suppressed. Sentence
should be six months tied to another bore.
That magnificent white sea elephant,
Auckland Admiralty House, is to claim the atten-
tion of Parliament, the local Harbor Board having
prayed the Government on its bended overdraft
to run an Act through enabling it to lease the ele-
phant as a lodging house, or something, and thus
partly save the expenditure of £5OO a year in
maintenance. The rearing of the pile was a showy
act of ghastly extravagance. It went up in a
blaze of splendor to slow music, and when the
authorities paused for applause it wasn’t forth-
coming, the Admiral of the fleet putting their
scented locks out of curl by flatly refusing to live
in the place. So the best that can be made of a
bad job is to lease it to the head dustman, or to
someone who will effectually disguise it as a three-
course meal factory, or to a Chow of royal blood
who will turn it into a steam laundry, or make it
a home for aged and incurable Harbor Boards.

Government Notices.
Department of Lands,
Sydney, 7th Dec., 1903.
I RECTOR OF FORESTS.
Subject to the provisions of the Forestry Act,
the Bill for which has been introduced in the
Parliament of 1903, applications will be received
by the Under-Secretary for Lands up to 29th
February, 1904, next, from officers of the Public
Service, and from persons outside the Service, for
appointment to the position of Director of Forests
(Professional Division); yearly salary. £7OO mini-
mum, £7OO maximum; to be fixed by statute.
The duties attached to the position will be (sub-
ject to the administrative control of the Under-
Seocetary for Lands : ~ '
(1) To re-organise the forest system of New
South Wales upon a practical and scientific
basis, including measures for effective
conservation and reafforestation of
forests, and for the systematic working
of same in accordance with recognised
forestry principles.
(2) To efficiently supervise and control the
staff of officers connected with the
Forests and Forestry Department.
(3) To visit and personally inspect the forests
of the State, to determine (subject to the
concurrence of the Minister) questions
pertaining to conflicting interests, to
report upon the Timber supply of the
State, and in the interests of same ; to
classify the forest area necessary for its
efficient maintenance.
(4) To design and carry out schemes for the
reclamation and utilisation of waste land,
for preventing the erosion of soils and the
spread of sand-drifts, by planting and
cultivation of suitable indigenous or
exotic vegetation and timber trees and to
perpetuate the regeneration of valuable
timber trees which are in danger of
extermination.
(5) To carry on a system of training Foresters.
(6) To manage all business pertaining to
Forestry, to report thereon when directed,
and to advise the Minister on matters
connected with the administration of
same.
Applicants must not be over forty-eight(4B)years
of age, of good physique, and in good health, and
must have a thorough knowledge of the principles
of Forestry. An acquaintance with the Forests
and general geography of New South Wales is
desirable, and consideration will be given to
British or Indian Forest Training. Applicants
should state their date of birth, their experience,
and their qualifications, and must produce
authentic evidence of same, also credentials as to
POT BTo l rde?, r EDWARD MACFARLANE,
5a69. Acting Under-Secretary for Lands.

bowls or any other form of sporting requisite, apply Mick

Coolgardie (W. A.) has been exciting itself over
a new find made by the Hampton Syndicate near the old
“ Billycan Rush,” three miles east of Coolgardie, where
some Italian woodcutters secured about 7000oz. of gold
four years or so ago. The syndicate was formed on
Oct. 3, and consists of 260 shares, of which 60 are paid.
Calls of Is. a week were made, and, after six calls, or £60
in all had been paid, a rich strike was made at a depth of
16ft. In a very short time 400oz. of gold was dollied, and
the members of the syndicate received a div. of £5 a
share. The find is supposed to be the cap of a lode, but
until further prospecting is done, no one can say if there
is any permanency in it. Judging from Coolgardie’s
previous rich finds, it is probably a mere surface blow.
* * * *
Golden Link’s (Kalgoorlie, W.A.) Eclipse lease
continues to open up well in the oxidised zone, and it is
estimated that above the 100ft. level there is enough ore
in sight to keep the 20 stamps going for two years.
This is a pretty big contract, as the mill is treating 5000
tons a month, or about double the output of any other
20-head mill. Very large profits have been and are being
earned.'and the co. is now in a position to declare a sub-
stantial div. The Golden Link lease, adjoining the L.V.
Consols, is looking much better in the deeper levels, and
may soon be adding to the output. In this co. 353,183
shares have been, issued, and there is a liability of 2s. a
share. This liability will probably be wiped off out of
profits, as Bull is not fond of mining scrip which has an
assessment in view
* * * »
Kalgoorlie Amalgamated (W.A.), which prac-
tically surrounds the Eclipse, has been publishing assays
from £-oz. to 2oz. per ton from ore taken from the 90 and
170ft. levels. So far nothing of any permanent value has
been established, but, judging from the position of the
property the co. should have a future if well managed.
• » * . *
Hannan’s Reward, Mt. Charlotte (W.A.), is now
in charge of J. L'. Sinclair. The mine is of little value, and
Sinclair has a tough row to hoe ; but the property is so
intersected by rich leaders that fossickers are tumbling
over each other to obtain a tribute. Before long the co.
will probably turn out 200 to 300oz. a month, but there is
practically no hope of dividends.
* J ‘ - * * *
Lancefield, Laverton (W. A.), which was sampled
some mo.nths ago by Bewick,' Moreing and Co., is now
practically owned by that firm, which has bought up the
bulk of the shares. At the time of the examination
B.M. and Co. said that the ore was too low grade for the
price, and threw up the option. The lode is very wide,
averaging 18ft. at the No. 1 level, and increasing to 26ft.
at No. 3. This width of ore has been driven on for a
length of about 1000ft., and has been producing about
700oz. of gold a month from ore averaging about 8dwt.
Cer ton. The co. has, for the past three or four years,
een paying monthly divs. of 4d. per share, or £360 on its
21,600 shares, and a drop in the average of ldwt. per ton
would have knocked thingg endways. If equipped with
100 head,of up-to-date stampers, treating 12,000 tons a
month, the proposition might become a valuable pro-
perty. As B.M. and Co. do things on a big scale it is
quite on'the cards that the mine may yet rank as a fairly
large producer
* • • •
Queensland Menzies (W.A.) is again suffering
from the rotten policy of never looking an inch ahead.
The sixth, or 446ft. level, is nearing completion, while
the seventh, or 534ft., has only recently been started.
At this depth the eastern lode is 140ft. from the shaft
and the western 210ft. The crosscut is being driven at
the rate of about 40ft. a month, so that it will be three
months or so before the eastern lode is reached, and
nearly five before the western is encountered. There-
after, some opening-up will have to be done before really
rich returns can be looked for, and regular dividends
resumed. Tho co. is still producing about 750oz. a
month,,so it is very unlikely that it will have to undergo
reconstruction, but .it is very unsatisfactory for share-
holders that divs. should have to be suspended, even
though only temporarily.
'* * *
North-Western Associated, Kalgoorlie (W.A.),
has just-been given a corpse-reviver. The co. was floated
in 1896 with 375,000 shares, and £50,000 working capital,
which has all disappeared with surprisingly little to show
for it. -Out of the original 15 leases, containing 294
acres, only two, the Boomerang and Womera, of 42 acres,
remain. The assessment of Is. 6d. a share will produce
£28,125, which should give directors another long lease
of life. The leases are situated on the north-east belt, of
the Golden Mile, and have good possibilities, as that part
of the district has hitherto been very badly prospected.
For a long time past the ground has been held by tribu-
tes whb have taken out several hundred tons of loz. ore,
and made a real good thing out of it.
* * * *
Kalgurli (W.A.) is opening up very well at the
1000ft., or 10th level. In driving north, about 300ft. from
the main shaft, ore averaging 2oz. per ton has been met.
The mine all over is looking better, and next year’s divs.
may show a marked advance on the current year’s.
At Poverty Point.
An item ot interest in one of the Melbourne monu-
mental yards at present is Dick Stewart’s gravestone.
When completed it will divide honors with the Spring-
thorpe Marble at Booroondara. The pedestal—a mound
of stones and a tall cross roughly hewn out of grey
granite—throws into relief a life-size figure of an angel
with flowing robes and a pathetic little mouth, looking
up beseechingly. It is none other than our own Nell—a
very striking likeness —in white marble. It is said the
figure was executed in Italy from a photo.
Beau Nash, who is introduced in “Monsieur Beau-
caire,” played some curious pranks for wagers before he
settled down as master of ceremonies at Bath. He once
imitated Lady Godiva by riding naked through a village
—but on a cow instead of a horse. On another occasion
he the main entrance of York Minster, clothed
only in a blanket and a carefully-studied expression, as
the people came out of church. At Bath, Nash lived by
gambling and by making a levy on the gaming-tables.
‘ ‘ Petramos ”: Who said that theatrical poster work
was being revolutionised? Bill Anderson, by the time
this sees print, will probably have coined money in Ade-
laide with “In the Shadow of Night,” the posters of
which are about Hhe ..wildest things that have shocked
suburban boardings Ifor years. The boss poster resembles
a glare and a surrounding gloom. A red devil appears
to be simmering over a brimstone fire, and a white angel
(feminine) is floating towards him. Whether her clothes
are insured, or whether she gets impaled on the devil’s
horns I don’t know.
“ L.W.” : The result of the Slapoffski-Musgrove trial
will'possibly be an improvement in the manners of
theatrical managers. The manners of many theatrical
managers want improving. In all human probability
had Mr. Musgrove explained politely to Papa Slap, that
he, Musgrove, did not think Papa’s daughter a suitable
lady for the 00., there would have been no trouble, and
Mr. Musgrove would have been spared the unpleasantness
of being held up to the world as a gentleman who
doesn’t know how to talk like one.
Nellie Stewart’s value was talked of during the Mus-
grove-Slap. suit. Nellie wouldn’t put herself down for
less than £BO a week ; anywhere else, she reckoned
she would be worth £2OO, but she knew she
wouldn’t get it in Australia. Vincent, J. 0. Williamson’s
manager, though he didn’t want to say, confessed at last
that he woulsj offer £lOO, and spring to £l5O if he
couldn’t get her for less, but wouldn’t take her at £2OO,
though she was worth it.
A poetic gleam from the Slapoffski-Musgrove squabble.
G.M., from England, offered Slap, an engagement for
pantomine and comic opera till a grand opera season
came round again. If he accepted the offer he was to
cable “ Bunions,” and if he refused “ Boils.” “ Were you
glad of the offer ?” “It is difficult to say.” “ What did
you cable ? ” “ Bunions.”
“ E.” : Teddy Lauri, in the Slapoffski-Musgrove case,
referred to himself as the “ greatest dancer in the
Southern hemisphere.” He was right. Lauri is the best
Australia has seen since the old Gaiety days. He is a
master of all styles, is marvellous in “ deportment,” and
has the technique of the old Italian school. He dances
with his whole body, and not only with his legs. Fred
Storey in “ romantic grotesque,” Johnnie Coleman in the
“ Scarecrow,” Hugh Ward in “ modern eccentric,” and
Barney Fagan in a “ shuffle,” had all interesting move-
ments ; but Lauri, outside of his acrobatic evolutions, is
a virtuoso who insists on such elements as beauty, grace,
and lightness. His expressive power, by means of
gesture, movement and rhythm—the art of the old
pantomimists—is something that has rarely been seen
here before ; and he has shown that the Dance, in its
highest form, consists of the “beautiful in movement,”
and not merely in a meaningless flutter of skirts, or in
laborious leg gymnastics.
What is happening to the old style of musician—he of
the long hair, loose collar, and entranced gaze? Most of
the musical witnesses in the Slapoffski case looked just
like wharf-labor professors or pick and shovel artists—
though Slap, himself wanted but the flowing collar to sit
for Beethoven or one of the old Dutch masters.
The Willoughby-Geach co. purposes a new farcical
comedy—•“ The Boy From Buffalo”—at Auckland, on
Boxing Night. It is described as a fat, full-blooded
farce, big enough to fill the bill without the assistance of
a curtain-raiser, and almost worthy'to be called ‘ ‘ another
‘ Jones.’ ” Willoughby, of the gladsome socks and spirit-
ful waistcoat, is ever looking forward to the time when
Geach, having totted up the week’s receipts, will cry
“Another ‘Jones’!” in a voice broken by emotion.
Their joint dream of dramatic achievement goes no
further than that for the present. At some future period
they may possibly drop into sentiment, and speculate in
a beautiful revival of “ East Lynne.”
If Madame Slapoffski, as the “Fortune Teller,” was
worth more than £35 a week to an Australian manager,
how much would Etnilie Melville, at her best, have been
worth in the same character? What are the relative
values of such various qualifications as vocal ability,
acting ability, dancing, personal beauty, and “ indefinable
charm of manner” as regards a lady who undertakes an
all-important part in comic opera? ,
A correspondent writes :
The shunting of Frank Farnell, M.L.A., into a paid
Government billet is.only another proof that N.S.W.
Parliament has outlived its ideals. The shunting of
Varney Parkes was of the same class, though with a little
of the veneer of democracy upon it. A compact made
publicly in Parliament between Parkes and Jennings,
when the latter was Premier, and a bucolic member was
mentioned for a police magistracy, was that until a
member was six months out of his seat he should be in-
eligible for a State appointment. That compact was
accepted by the House as a fair, square, decent arrange-
ment.' Now the Government appoints a man while in his
seat, and times his entry on his new duties so that he shall
draw his Parliamentary pay until the day the new pay
begins.
The other day seven sailors were picked
up at sea in an open boat, and brought to Mel-
bourne by the Cromarty. Immediately they
landed they were lodged in gaol, though nothing
was known against them, and they were mostly
white, and spoke French. It was not for five
weeks that they were proved to be criminals, and
even then the crime alleged against two of them
was the mere trifle of having- helped to steal the
boat they were found in. But all seven were
shipped back to New Caledonia, and the blessed
Argus, which for five weeks had known nothing
derogatory to them, said never a word. But they
were mostly white; also they were gaoled under a
State law; the Federal Government had nothing
to do with them. Else we should have had
another Shipwrecked Sailor Lie.
Extract from the cries of Mr. “Jack”
Want:
England had not one of these blood-sucking Common-
wealth Governments which wrung £9,000,000 a year out
of the pockets of the Australian people.
England has got a Government which wrings a
lot more taxes out of each inhabitant yearly than
the Australian has to pay, but no one could
expect Mr. “Jack” Want to know that. Nor
could any one expect that Mr. “Jack” Want,
because he didn’t know anything, would abstain
from saying a lot of things. “ Jack ? s ’’speeches
are mostly filled with the things he doesn’t know.
