A feeling of disgust is sinking into Vic-
torians in regard to the new management of the
railways. This feeling was strongly marked in
the Leg. Assembly on the question of the carriage
of grain. Of course the awful Kyabrammers of
Victoria, who are constantly on the look-out to rob
the State, are sore at the determination of the new
Railway Commissioners to charge a fair rate for
the carriage of the coining season’s wheat. But,
apart from this, the public is slowly awaking to
the fact that the Iceberg Government, with its
bullying Bent, has so disorganised the whole rail-
way service that it is impossible to get any good
work out of it. _ Also the notion is gaining ground
that the expensive Tait, who was imported from
Canada on the recommendation of a relative of an
Irvine supporter in the Assembly, is more of a
society pretty-man than a genuine railway mana-
ger. lnere can be no doubt whatever that the
Commissioners have utterly failed to appreciate
the importance of the suburban traffic They
have allowed a large share of it to fall into the
hands of the trams It was the suburban traffic
which paid best before the advent of the Canadian,
and which really carried on its back many non-
paying country lines.
J
The man who can make a good phrase is
greater than he who taketh a city. President
Roosevelt makes a good beginning, with his pro-
posal to break into Colombia under “ Civilisa-
tion’s right of eminent demesne." The making of
the Panama Canal is a work of sufficient urgency
to justify considerable straining of abstract justice
when a place like Colombia gets in the way, but
why not burgle without the beautiful language?
A burglar with a text tied to his crowbar isn’t an
edifying sight.
“Burglar Bill" : Saw a confirmed opium-
eater come into gaol once to do six months. Too
much of a rebel to go near “ the poisoner ” to get
a stimulant to hold him together, he battled with
the torture on his own. Started him with me
constructing trousers, but when he had sewn 14
buttons round the feet of a pair of trousers, I put
him on at buttonhole-making. He cut the holes
four inches long, and worked like a Bawler
sewing-machine, going full speed, with a jerky
motion. Told me he never slept a moment for
the first ten nights. Then he straightened up,
and ran on all right to within a month of his dis-
charge, when all the old craving came back on
him. Day before he went out he had the same
symptoms as when he came in. I made him knock
off work; it was a lot of trouble to me cutting the
buttons off those trouser-legs.

An Eventful Day at Yam.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The boy behind the old stable was very lonely.
Naturally a youth of abounding vivacity, he was
yet capable of profound sadness, and this after-
noon he sat in his solitude, playing a listless game
of solo “knucks,” and indulging himself in a fit
of gentle melancholy. The day was fair; the
wide flat lay spread before him, flooded with
warm, golden sunlight, filtered through a film ol
smoke; the cavernous bluestone quarries yawned
invitingly ; the thick bush beyond was rich with
the fullness of summer. He could hear the queru-
lous chummering of a million parrots. He knew
that they hung head-down among the white-gum
blossoms, clustering like briar berries, and he had
in his pocket a “ging” with a shop-made wire
prong, a “ ging ” of marvellous power and deadly
accuracy. Yet he dared not, for the life of him,
venture from his cover.
Kid Carter was “ wagging it.” At dinner-time
he had secreted himself within the fence sur-
rounding Plover’s old butcher’s shop, the sole
remaining building in what had once been the
township’s second-best street, thinking the ex-
ploration of the big, empty house and its out-
houses would be sufficient for an afternoon’s
amusement. It was now only about half-past
two. The attractions of the dark, dusty rooms
were exhausted; the last “spoggy’s” egg was
pillaged from the chimneys ; and all the rubbish
in the outhouses carefully prospected.
Kid Carter had knocked boards from the back
of the stable, and crept through into the open,
but only to find his weariness deepen to a pro-
found ennui, touched with sadness. The glowing
world was all before him, but he knew that the
wide flat was right under all the prying eyes of
the township, and an attempt to escape in that
direction would mean immediate detection, and
would surely send Mother White, or Mother Lee,
or Mother Hogan, or perhaps all three, trotting
breathlessly to Mrs. Carter’s kitchen-door with a
joyful betrayal.
Kid mused on the .mpious vindictiveness with
which he was beset by the mothers of the town-
ship. It was abominable, inhuman ; he was only
a poor boy trying to do his best in a perverse
world, and everybody was down on him. When
he rushed into a flaming house, as he intended
doing one of these times, and rescued little
golden-haired Flo Stevens from an awful doom,
sustaining shocking injuries in the act, but
bearing them with a sweet and smiling fortitude,
these people would understand him better, and
would know how cruelly they had misjudged him.
But at that moment Morrissey’s dog Botts
came sniffing round the other corner, and the
dream was dispelled. The imp and the hunter
awoke in Kid, and a stone from his “ging” sent
the dog howling. Botts returned a minute later,
and started a distant, desultory barking at the
boy, like a dog who takes little interest in the
business, but can find nothing better to do. This
threatened discovery, and conciliatory measures
were progressing favorably, when suddenly Botts’
attention was diverted by somebody still out of
the range of Kid’s vision, and the boy scram-
bled into the stable again to await developments.
It was Mrs. Tim Gleeson who came into view,
and Mrs, Tim’s apron betrayed the outlines of a
hidden billy. Mrs. Gleeson’s appetite for beer
was notorious, and her sprees were the township’s
most popular sensations; yet the lady had an
extraordinary diffidence about her comings and
goings between her house and The Drover’s Arms,
and was strangely shy of being seen in possession
i of a quart billy. She had still an ingenuous belief
that the township regarded her as a person whose
sobriety was a standing reproach to her neighbors.
Mrs. Tim Gleeson was a short, stout woman.
Her face was mottled, and in figure she resembled
three balls of dough placed one on top of the
other, and then slightly depressed. Botts was
barking at her heels, and making mischievous
snaps at her short skirts. Under cover of the
building she turned upon him fiercely. He was
an amiable dog, but his intentions were likely to
bring her into public contempt.
‘Gerrout, ye idjit baste,” she cried, “would ye
ruin me ? Would ye bring shame on a dacent,
sober, God-fearin’ woman?”
She hurled stones at the tyke, and then,
assuming the modest deportment that character-
ised all her pub trips, she passed on. Botts
skulked after her, a little disconcerted, but still
with the air of a dog with a duty to perform.
“His-s-s!” said Kid encouragingly from the
stable.
Botts needed nothing more. Giving a joyous
yap he bounded after Mrs. Tim Gleeson, and
barked noisily at her heels, and frisked about her
in an exuberant larrikin spirit all the way to
Paddy Hannan’s. Mrs. Gleeson walked de-
murely, ignoring the dog’s attentions, but conscious
that the eyes of half the women of the township
were upon her, and that those women hated her
for her rigid sobriety and her orderly conduct.
Mrs. Gleeson entered by the store door. Never
in her drunkest moments had she been known to
use the bar door. In fact, the drunker Kitty
became, the more anxious she grew to preserve
her reputation, and even though she walked like
a lame beetle on wet paint, and her hair streamed
in the wind, and her bonnet hung down her back
on one strand, her skill in masking the incrimina-
ting billy never forsook her. While the billy was
out of evidence she felt safe from the tongue of
scandal and the finger of scorn. Shingles ex-
plained everything else. Shingles was Mrs Tim’s
pet complaint.
Botts waited for the lady to issue forth, and
renewed the attack, dashing at her from all sides,
and barking in an excess of canine devilment.
Kitty made a detour of the old shop in order to
mask her movements as much as possible from the
inquisitive women of Yam ; a sure proof that her
spree was well advanced. Screened by the
deserted house, she reproached Botts again with
great bitterness, and much language that must go
unrecorded.
“ Hell to yer sowl for a low-lived mis-begotten
mongrool cur! ” she wailed, with a touch of
emotion so profound that Kid writhed giggling
among the litter. “Fer whoi do ye plague a
poor body wid her on’y boy a-lyin’ in the grass,
an’ the shingles that bad she don’ know the joy
av loif one moment from another?” Botts was
visibly disconcerted by the rebuke, and barked
foolishly to keep himself in countenance. “Divel
blast ye ! ” yelled Mrs. Tim, with a sudden
change of mood, “take that!” The first stone
missed, but she ran wildly at another, and suc-
ceeded at length, sending Botts limping and yelp-
ing dolorously. Mrs. Gleeson picked up her billy
again, composed herself into her attitude of proud
humility, and went on.
“ Hiss-s ! Soolem ! ” whispered Kid Carter,
and Botts forgot his troubles, and was off again,
harassing Mis. Gleeson’s retreat. The dog
returned in a few minutes, wriggling his body,
whisking his tail, and grinning with all a mongrel’s
impish fatuity. His manner plainly implied an
understanding between himself and Kid, a part-
nership in villainy. Kid applauded him, and
encouraged his advances, inveigling Botts into the
stable. The boy knew that Mrs. Gleeson would
be passing often. It was one of the lady’s idio-
syncrasies to buy a small quantity of beer at a
time, under a foolish illusion that she needed only
just one wee drop to relieve the worst symptoms
of her shingles. This habit had earned her the
name of “ Threepenn’orth More.”
Mrs. Gleeson came again, and again. Botts
was encouraged to invite public attention to the
passing of the lady with his clamor and insane
oaperings. The third time “Threepenn’orth More”
went by with caution, looking anxiously to the
right and left, but there was nolfoe in sight, for
Kid had replaced the boards of the stable w., 11,
and Botts was gagged within. A few seconds
later the brute was after her, and Mrs. Tim’s soul
was ablaze with wrath as he tugged her skirts and
tumbled under her feet, yapping the while like
something frantic. The bobbing of female heads
behind fences and at windows and doors, and an
occasional dry cackle of laughter induced her to
carry her nose high and take special precautions
with the billy. She felt that behind the sleepy,
lazy quiet of the blinds and the buildings, the
women of Yam were wide awake, and eager for
an exposure. And she was right. The township
was watching developments.

remorse. Kid s a d , • ~ ■, , • j’ -

and when the poor, generated womani came
again, her m

She went on, and this time her manner betrayed
agitation. She bought her beer, and was return-
ing along the grass-grown path of the decayed
street in the eye of the township, when ° , 8
made a snap, and secured the apron ; then, back-
mg valiantly, he towed Airs. Gleeson hither and
thither and up from behind the fences rose, the
heads of the women of Yam, and they stared with
all their eyes.
Suddenly, rip went the apron, and Botts fled
with his trophy, leaving the fateful billy ex-
posed to Kitty’s critics. But Mrs. Tim was not
beaten yet; like a flash, she dumped the utensil
on the ground, and, planted between it and the
enemy, endeavored to hide the evidence of her
guilt with her skirts. Her arms were folded on
her breast; her nose was high in the air. Stand-
ing thus, she defied the whole township.
Botts returned immediately, and, circling round
the tragic figure, he harked noisily. The dog had
been trained to carry Morrissey s billy, and, some
unfortunate instinct possessing him, he nipped up
Mrs. Gleeson’s quart pot and dashed off with it.
Tliis was too much. Kitty’s dignity, her
anxiety about her good name, everything was lost
in her profound ooncern for the safety of her beer,
Running and rolling she followed the dog, and
out from their yards came the women,
with the courage of their numbers, and
followed Mrs. Tim, along with the screaming, en-
raptured children liberated from school a few
moments before. Kid Carter joined the throng.
Botts was a dog of humor. He entered into the
spirit of the thing with rare gusto. A dozen
times Mrs. Tim seemed to have her fingers upon
him, and as often he eluded her. Then wearying
of the whole thing all in a second he dropped his
prize and fled. Mrs. Gleeson picked up the billy.
She lifted it slowly, and turned it bottom up-
wards. Not a drop of drink remained. Her
white face was tragic—it awed the women. They
felt themselves in the presence of a catastrophe.
“ Mother av hivin ! ” said Kitty in low, intense
tones, “an’ twas me last penny in the world !”
Lifting her voice to a shriek, she cried: “Where's
Morrissey ? Bring me to Mick Morrissey ! ”
Running with a queer drunken roll, she headed
for the home of the owner of Botts, and the crowd
followed. Mick, most peaceable of men, was
busy weeding his tomatoes. Mrs. Tim entered
by the front gate, and arming herself with a great
stick came upon Morrissey all unprepared, and
sent a him down with terrible whack on the head.
She recoiled herself and sat heavily among the
onions.
Morrissey lay for a minute face down in the
soft soil. Then he pulled himself together and
slowly arose, and stood staring stupidly at Mrs.
Gleeson. A sense of his wrong came to him in a
while, and with it a profound craving for ven-
geance.
“Where’s Tim Gleeson?” cried Morrissey in
his turn, and out of his gate he ran, and off in the
direction of The Lucky Brothers mine. The
crowd followed. Tim Gleeson was coming off day
shift, and the men clashed on the common.
“Yer dirty, low, dhrunken trollop av a wife
done it,” cried Morrissey, pointing to his wound.
“Ye lie!” replied Gleeson, seeing fight inevit-
able, and knowing nothing else to say.
They fought. Morrissey was too precipitate.
He was still dazed from the blow Mrs. Tim had
given him; and Gleeson was in good condition.
After three rounds, Mike was so badly knocked
about that he had to be removed to his home in a
cousin-Jack barrow. And then Kid Carter went
to tea.
He was so willing and obedient that evening,
and so good to the baby, that Mrs. Carter felt a
great gush of motherly tenderness stir her heart
as she stood by his bedside, gazing down at
the innocent happy lad smiling in his sleep.
Morrissey’s head was reported very bad next day,
but Kid never dreamed of finding any connection
between his infamy and Mike’s misfortune.
EDWARD DYSON.
“ Melb.” : At Flemington there are,
as near as I can be troubled with counting,
two dozen fine elms in the space between
the grandstand and saddling paddock, and
beyond them, that is on the west or pad-
dock side, there are about twenty fine pine
trees, clustering by themselves in a haughty way,
in imitation of the elms. The elms seem to say
“English,” and the pines “American!” The
fashionable betting is all under the elms; gam-
bling isn’t countenanced under the pines. So
there arises the din from a tumbling ocean of
summer hats beneath the elmy shade, and the
birds are all frightened away, and bolt to the
pines for shelter. But when the nasty Cup is
over, the birds return with songful glee to the
Flemington elms.

The Drums of Ages.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Drums of all that's right and wrong—of love and
hate and scorn,
And the new-born baby hears them and it wails
when it is born.
Drums of all that is to be, and all that has gone by,
And we hear them when we're dreaming, and we
hear them while we die.
Drums of martyred innocence and drums of driven
guilt
Beating backward from the future when the first
rude town was built;
Beating louder through the slave days and the
dark and hungry nights,
While the hovels filled the valleys and the castles
crowned the heights;
Beating louder while the mansions shifted east
from miles of slums —
Don’t you hear them ? Don’t you hear them ?
Don’t you hear the alley drums?
Drums of human sacrifice and drums of war at
home—
While the Romans conquered nations they were
beating loud in Rome.
Children heard them through the ages, mothers
paused and glanced behind,
Madmen saw and heard the drummers, but the
rest were deaf and blind.
Peasants starved on fields of plenty, workmen
rotted in the slums—
Till the drummers came to Paris and the nations
heard the drums.
Drums of hope and bursting hearts —the drums of
Westward Ho !
From the homes of generations and their native
land they go.
’Groom and bride and grey-haired mother, bent
old men who go alone—
Fleeing bitter persecution for the terrible un-
known :
Seeking freedom, rest, or justice—and. the peace
that never comes,
And the wilderness was conquered when the
pilgrims beat their drums.
Drums of Greed that followed fast where men
had made the way,
Waking drums of stern rebellion when the exiles
turned at bay,
Spreading death and desolation, breeding old-
world hells anew,
Until England lost a nation for the blindness of a
few.
Still the dirty Jewish talon reached from palaces
and slums
Till a hundred thousand English died to stop the
farmers’ drums.
Drums of tortured hearts o’ men —the drums that
never ceased —
Throbbing through the British Empire from the
heart of London East;
Growling louder still wherever, in the wake of
those who lead,
Comes the murmur of the board-room and the
stealthy steps of greed ;
Growling through the Southern cities, murmuring
in the Western gums,
Till the Empire falls to pieces at the beating of
the drums!
Drums of all that's right and wrong—of love, and
hate and scorn ;
And the new-born baby hehrs them, and he wails
when he is bom.
Drums of all that is to be, and all that has gone by —
And we hear them when we're dreaming, and we
hear them while we die.
K.S.W.
HENRY LAWSON.
An Improved Failure.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
On a morning of the week before I lefb the Cold
Country I read in London the hereunder printed
Bulletin par:
Re that Bulletin prophecy (20/6/’O3) to the effect that,
as the British array is about to be re-armed with a new
rifle, Australia would, doubtless, be expected to kindly
relieve the Cold Country of as many as possible of the
obsolete weapons. John Forrest stated, in the Reps.,
t’other day, that Australia had already 13,000 Lee-Enfield
and Lee-Metford magazine rifles, and that others were
being imported as rapidly as possible—7ooo Lee-Enfields
were already on their way out, and others would follow,
as soon, no doubt, as the British authorities can replace
them at ’Ome with the Lee-Enfield modified 1 Australia
has been buying Britain’s old iron at Britain’s own price
for a long time ; but surely there is a time in life when
the poorest Tommy or Johnny should refuse to do with
pa’s cast-off clothes any longer.
In the afternoon, Alf. Hales and myself were in
the offices of one of the best-known of British
gunmakers. We wandered up the stairs to talk
to him; but, as soon as the conversation turned
on guns, the Boer War came up, and the modified
Lee-Enfield, and then the Gunmaker did the
talking.
He showed us the rifle used in the South African
War, and explained that one of the reasons why
the British troops did such bad shooting was that
the rifle itself was a weapon made almost harmless
by the designers. He showed us how the first
sights, being so unreliable that a man sighting by
them could scarcely hit a haystack, were removed
from the top surface of the barrel and replaced
halfway between the top surface and the side sur-
face—an attempt to correct an error with a foolish-
it’s useless pointing out even a glaring
error like this to the authorities,” he continued.
“They persist in it rather than acknowledge a
m “*3O that an error involving the loss of £lO,OOO
may be covered up with a persistence involving
the loss of £100,000.”
“Exactly. These rifles were never any good,
and that must have been known before they were
sent to South Africa. ......
(Are these the rifles that Australia is to pay
f °The Gunmaker continued. “ For another thing,
the new rifle will be no good. It is too long in
the barrel. And they know it’s no good ; yet
special costly machinery has been built at Enfield
for these rifles, and, as you know, machinery of
that class, except for the specific work it is con-
structed to manufacture, is mere scrap iron.”
“ So this new rifle is being made at the Govern-
ment factory?”
“It isn’t. A big contract has been given to
the Birmingham Small Arms Co.”
“Mr. Chamberlain is a big shareholder of the
8.5. A., isn’t he?” I asked.
“We won’t go into that now. Another big
contract has been given to the London Small
Arms Co.”
“ And what is the Government factory doing ? ”
“Well, the Government factory has just
shortened hands.”
“In the name of all that’s senseless, what
for?”
“ You see, there are only a certain number, say
5000, of expert gun artisans in England. Some-
times we have trouble in getting men. Well,
as soon as the Birmingham Small Arms Co. and
the London Small Arms Co. received the contracts
for the new rifle, the Government shops at Enfield
obligingly dismissed 600 hands, and the two con-
tractors absorbed them at once without loss of
time or increasing wages.”
And yet the Government workshops had erected
new machinery for this special class of arm !
The expert went on to show us a bewildering
variety of guns, from flintlocks of 200 years ago
which were much surer weapons than the Enfields
of the last Boer war, to the model of the improved
Enfield, now said to be also a disappointment, and
after that we saw many kinds of magazine
apparatus, cartridge clips and improvements for
the throwing out of the used cases.
“One would think,” said I, “that the gun
artisans would be responsible for the invention of
these improvements; but the patent office records
show that they are mostly the inventions of
officers.’’
The expert smiled. “Actually they are the
inventions of workmen. But what would happen
to the workman at Enfield who patented his
invention over the heads of his superiors there?
Dismissal and probably boycott. So the work-
man, knowing this, takes his invention to an
officer, and the officer patents it in hisj own name,
thus getting a reputation for ability which he
doesn’t possess, and the actual inventor is given
a few shillings a week rise in wages.”
Then we talked guns again, and the expert
showed us how in sport there is as much fashion-
able pretence as actual love of sport. There were
braces of seventy-guinea sporting guns, silver-
mounted, and bedded in velvet. These come back
for “ repair ” every year whether they have been
used or not.
Marvellous is the extent of pretence in this pre-
tentious island, from the spick and span “ sports-
man ” who bravely slaughters a few brace of
driven birds, to the flat-chested English Miss
who brandishes a little dog whip over a waddling
Dachshund and says, in a squeaky treble, “ Down,
sir,” to the animal that is too fat to stand on less
than four legs.
Is Australia buying guns from»,.a department
that seems to be as rotten with mismanagement
as the Remount Department, or the Intelligence
Department, or the Clothing Supply Department,
and presumably every other department of the
War Office? And if, as is alleged, the misman-
agement of the British navy is almost as glaringly
idiotic as that of the British army, what is Aus-
tralia to get for its £200,000 subsidy ?
Thinking all these things, I went back to the
hotel for dinner ; and there, just as if he had
never moved since dinner last a frantic
Jingo still asserting the purity of British official-
dom—still the puppet British mon-
archical system by exaggerating the corruption in
the public life of the United States.
And the humor of tins' ostrich amused me so
that my appetite appreciated by 50 per cent.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
Hardshell Tories who think that the
N.S.W. Industrial Arbitration Act is all one-
sided might have got some light from part of the
Court’s proceedings recently. “The Brick-
makers and Brickcarters Employees’ Union pro-
ceeded against; a number of members for non-
payment of dues. Orders were made.” So, as
the law says, a member can’t suddenly leave off
being a member just when he pleases—when an
award is given against his union, for instance.
The union is under a heavy financial obligation
to obey every award; and the member is under
an obligation to continue his membership for a
reasonable time, and to keep the union in funds
sufficient to pay the forfeit if the award is broken.
“Caber” : A small error occurs in
Bulletin (22/10/’O3) anent the Queensland Labor
Party : George Kerr, the Barcoo blacksmith, was
not the first Laborite elected. George was one of
fifteen of 1893 ; Tom Glassey entered Parliament
in 1888 as member for Bundamba. Glassey, John
Hoolan, and T. J. Ryan were joined by G. H.
Hall, who won Bundaberg in a bye-election.
Ryan couldn’t stand his oats and left the
other three who rbfused to follow the
present Federal Chief Justice in his apostacy
of 1890. Hall was bumped out in 1893 by a couple
of votes, but Glassey won the Bundaberg seat
back next election. Not one of the first Labor
Party is now in Queensland politics.
One Australian in S’Africa, Mr. D. Mack
—a nephew of “Jimmy” Tyson—got angry at
the constant insults to his countrymen, and wrote
to the Cape Times, inter alia:
I am not referring to the class of men who came here
and served against the Boers, for those men were un-
deniably hard-up “tags” from the towns there, and few
of them typical representatives of the country. They
were glad of the bone England threw them, and would
gladly take another if they got the chance. Those fellows
are ready to slay anything for dollars. They have done
our country no good, but cast a stigma on it by their
acts in and out of the army, that will take us perhaps one
hundred years to wipe out. Pity is any of the wasters
returned to our shores. I hope you keep the balance
here, for there is little beer and music left for them if
they return now, but plenty of hominy, I guess. Neither
the first contingent nor the last, nor any other, was
composed of typical bushmen, and not a single
“station” man in my country ever thought so at any
time. The genuine Australian would, be slow to go
abroad to fight against any country, and I state emphati-
cally he is too much of a “patriot” to attempt such a
thing ; but God help any invader, be he Englishman or
anything else, for they would find Australians as patriotic
and brave as the little band of Boer warriors whom some
of my supposed countrymen came to mob for filthy lucre.
Australians are unjustly judged by people in England,
and the outside world, as being all of the same kidney as
the fellows who served. God forbid 1
After all, the S’Africans can’t be blamed for
judging us by our export samples. That’s part
of the price we have to pay. But what a quaint
satire it is when Australia has to defend its char-
acter by declaring: publicly that its “heroes”
were the scum of the country, and that owing to
the blackguardly nature of the errand on which
they went they would naturally be scum.

“ELM-EV KGUCE,” 151 ELIZABETH STREET, SYDNEY
The evanishment of the Musgrove dramatic co. is a
matter for conflicting gossip. Australia loses a bright, ver-
satile actor in Beatty, anyhow. Many inferior artists have
received more credit for their work than the unassuming
gentleman who played Charles 11., Don Caesar de Bazan,
the Professor in. “ Mice and Men,” and the humorous>
bibulous father of “ Dolores.” Beatty was fairly con-
vincing, sometimes quite convincing, in several different
characters, but for the actor who strives to hide his own
personal identity in a part instead of obtruding it and
labelling it “Booster” (or whatever his name be); there
is no chance of a “star” popularity. The mannered
artificial mummer, not the finished natural actor, gets
the adoration of womankind—generally speaking.
“Hiphen”: In the “Great Millionaire,” Blandolt
gave a wonderful sketch of an Australian—Bill Balwyn,
from the Barcoo, In the very first scene you could see
he was a true ’Stralian. He carried a billy with him,
which he proceeded to boil on the stage. I didn’t see
him put any water (much less tea) in it, and when it was
(presumably) boiled he took it off the fire, packed it up
in his swag, and proceeded on the wallaby. This is what
all true Australians do. Anyone who has seen Rupert
Clarke boiling his billy in Oollins-street will know that.
“L.” : Herr Gustaye SJapoffski has found an enthusiast
who has offered £BOOO to run grand opera seasons in
Melbourne and Sydney. Herr Slap, leaves shortly to
bring out a company, which will play almost exclusively
Wagner, including the great music dramas that have
never yet been staged in Australia. Marshall-Hall, at
time of writing, was wearing a joyous smile, while the
philosophic Dietrich is exclaiming “ Ach Himmel ” and
chewing up twice his usual number of cigars. Other
Wagnerians are joyous in their respective styles.
Henry Jarrett, the Old American theatrical manager,
happened to die in London the other week. George Rig-
nold was under engagement to Jarrett when he scored
his memorable American success as Henry Vee. The
girls, young and old, chased the godlike actor in their
dreams, and strove to waylay him at all hours of the day,
thus compelling him to remain secluded in his private
rooms at the hotel, lest they might see the real George
and suffer a measure of disenchantment.
“ Altiora Peto ” : With regard to “ L.B.’s ” demand for
a national theatre (B. 12/11/’O3) it is well known that the
State-subsidised theatres of Germany, Austria and Franco
have outlived their usefulness ; that the ComiAie
Francaise, for instance, is degenerating in its standards
and in its relation to the living drama of the day. As
far as the question exists here, it is a much more difficult
one than under a less democratic Government, since such
an institution, supported by public- funds, would arouse
the hostility of a large part of the people. Private enter-
prise is much more likely than State aid to succeed in the
conversion of the theatre into an educational influence.
As libraries, colleges, music and art are largely supported
by private endowment, there is no reason why a theatre
for educational purposes should not also be founded by
some not-yet-dead Australian millionaire. A very good
example of the subscription theatre is the German
theatre in Irving Place, New York City, which often
gives more great plays in a season than all the theatres
on Broadway together.
Christie Simonsens, who has been Bland Holt’s business
manager for many years on and off, talks of returning to
ordinary commercial life. The job of “ looking after the
front of the house ” from 7 p.m. to 11 p.m., or later, for six
nights a week is probably the most weaiusome task in the
world for any man who thinks his time could be better
employed. Some attaches of the theatrical profession
dtn’t think their time could be more profitably employed
—and they are right. It couldn’t.
“ Vul.” : Whether it is the fault of Duse herself, or
whether the trouble rests with the hyper-moral outlook
of Censor Redford, it can’t be denied that the former is
suffering greatly at the hands of the latter of l’ate.
Following on his refusal of last year to grant her a license
to produce in England one of D’Annunzio’s plays, he
lately refused to allow her to stage the same author’s
“ The Dead City,” and now again declines to give her a
license for Maurice Donnay’s “ L'Ahup >anger.” While
the first two refusals in connection with D’Annunzio’s
pieces surpast understanding, this latter is moderately
understandable. The Donnay piece is rather unwhole-
some, to say the least. It treats of a bitter rivalry
between amother and her own daughter for the affections
of a man who has for some time been the aocepted lover
of the former. Even blas£ Paris is said to have been
moderately agitated over this situation.
H. M. Mack (Manila) writes: Harold Carr and Eva
M’Donald, of the Pollard company, have left the rest and
returned to their native land. Hongkong and Shanghai
were no better, financially, t nan Manila, and it now
remains to be seen whether India will “ pan out ” any
better. Am afraid not. Even the Neil-Frawley company
did poorly here. The tired European in Asia is hard to
please. Ada Delro.v’s Vaudeville troupe has been doing
good business to the soldier element, at the Orphehm,
where you can drink beer at small tables and join in the
chorus if you feel that way. Daisy Haroourt’s risque
songs meet with entire approval, especially the one,
“ Dew it Agvne,” us the Americans say she pronounces
“Do it Again.” Daisy grew angry at a man, in a stage-
box the other night, because he hissed her. She came
out and threw a glass of water in his face, then went off
crying ! Ada, the old Ada who has weathered so many
storms, still whirls and whizzes in her fir.e-danoe and
never seems to grow stiff in the knees. She must be
nearly 19 now ! Sam Rowley bawls out something about
a sundowner wanting “to hang up his hat somewhere,”
and other Australian ditties, which don’t appear humor-
ous to the ’Murkans. Manila has also made acquaintance
with the Hill-Sylvainy company from Australia, who
opened at the Zorilla Theatre. They promise to do well,
especially with the unicycle trick-riding feat down a
ladder, or something equally impossible.
Nina Boueicault—dainty Nina, of the first Brough and
Boucieault co.—plays heroine in Barrie’s new play,
“ Little Mary.” She’s the angelic cook who enthrals the
stummick of mankind.
